PUBLISHED AT SAN FRANCISCO, CAL., U.S.A.
The Argonaut publishes bright weekly letters from
New York, Paris, and London, from skilled correspondents
who .are in touch with the intense life of those great cities.
It publishes frequent letters from other points in the
old world, and from the older world of Asia come occa-
sional letters from India, China, and Japan. Its short
stories are famous throughout the world. It is hard to
describe their peculiar lingo, but an idea may be gathered
from.this, our standing injunction to our contributors:
“ Whatever you are, never be commonplace.” The re-
sult is that the Argonaut short stories have an individu-
ality of their own. The Argonaut endeavors to present a
weekly review of the literary movement. Brief notes on
books, rather than lengthy reviews, together with notes
upon authors and journalists, will keep the reader well
up with the literary gossip of the day.
For sale at Tim Bulletin Office after the arrival of the
American mail; price, Bd. ; posted, 9d. ; mailed, regularly
to any Australasian address.

THE RED PAGE.
The new edition of “ Webster’s Inter-
national Dictionary ” has value for one thing
among many others—that it indefinitely
fixes the derivation of larrikin. The fixing
is much more definite in an editorial note
supplied to some of the dictionary’s clients,
which states a clear relationship with larli,
provincial English lar-r-k, or larack, to frolic
about :
Here is a quotation from “The Dialect of
•Leeds,” a book which was published in 1802, eight
vears before the word appeared in print in Mel-
bourne :
“ Larack. —To frolic.
Ybas larracking abart ower niich fur my
fancy, said of a giddy person—one always at a
frolic. ‘ Shoo larack’d abart an’ did nowt else
wal shoo wur fair grown up’—romped about, &c.
—Dial, of Leeds, 1802, p. 339.”
few years after the word appeared in print in
Melbourne, we find the word “ larrikin ” given in
a called “The Ancient Language and Dialect
?» by Dr - V ' W ‘ R Published in
1882. ihe work was written about 1880, so that
if the word were of Australian origin it would
have to have travelled to Cornwall in ten years,
and become so common there that Dr. Jago (a man
who, in the preface to his book, describes himself
as “ long descended from Cornishmen, and with a
clannish fondness for Cornish words and phrases”)
and other writers mistook it for a Cornish word.
Here is a quotation taken from his Dictionary :
“ Larrikins. Mischievous young fellows, larkers.
Mischievous larrikins who pull the young trees
down.’— The Cornish uuin. ” —F. W. P. Jago, The
Ane. Lang, and the Dial, of Cornwall, Truro, 1882
p. 205. ’
With a view to getting further light on the sub-
ject, Dr. Jago was written to, and replied as fol-
lows :
57 Ebrington-street, Plymouth, England, March
I do not think the word larrikin can
have been first used in your part of the world.
From all I can find it has been long in use in
Cornwall. A Cornish lady friend of mine, nearly
80 years of age, tells me that she has known the
name “ever since she was a girl; ” and another
friend, aged 70 years, says that he has heard the
word used so far north as Berwick, so the use of
the word is very far spread.
n Cornwall we distinguish between a “ rough*”
and a ‘ larrikin.” The latter is considered to be
rpiP uuschief, ” but this must have fun in
it. - bus it would really seem, and it is my
opinion, that larrikin is synonymous with, or
another form of the word larker, i.e., one who
lar.cs, or plays practical j'okes.
We Cornish consider larrikins as being young
fellows ready to play all sorts of tricks. Larrikins
used (not so much now) to exercise their talents in
a variety of ways in Cornwall. I will mention a
few of them, thus—
1. Going into a field or garden and planting the
oaPhages, turnips and small shrubs upside-down.
2. At my school in Truro, Cornwall, it was
larrikin law that every cup must have its handle
broken off when new cups were had to replace the
broken ones.
3. At Bodmin the larrikins would send the rib
of a horse, well cleaned and daintily wrapped in
silver wrapper, to any crusty or selfish old bachelor
to remind him of his duty.
And so on, for a dozen instances. Thus
the actual word, in the same sense, with an
obvious derivation, has had a lengthy Eng-
lish vogue; and to say it was invented in
Melbourne about 1879 by Sergt. Dalton or
another is absurd. Possibly some imported
Melb. policeman applied the English word
to the Australian species ; and the use
“growed,” like Topsy. The pity is that
this information was not specifically incor-
porated in Webster’s Dictionary.
All things considered, the new Webster is
about as useful an English dictionary as
exists. It cannot, of course, aspire to the
place of the New English Dictionary by the
side of the student; and it is not quite as
handy for everyday use as some less bulky
tomes the new Chambers, for example.
But it is far fuller than Chambers’s ; it sup-
ports its meanings with quotations and more
numerous illustrations ; and it is without that
narrow theological bias which disfigures all the
Chambers publications, and which in the
Chambers dictionary shades or distorts the
meaning of words like Catholic, freethinker,
to suit Presbyterian prejudice. A special
merit of the VV ebster dictionary is its appen-
dices, including a pronouncing gazetteer, a
biographical list, lists of quotations, pro-
verbs, phrases, proper names, and so on.
All these things are better sought in a dozen
separate volumes, but few people have the
space or the cash for a large reference library.
Webster’s Dictionary, with a little of every-
thing in its single volume, is particularly
fitted to be the guide and friend of the bush
Australian.
* * » *
And an extra-special merit of the new
Webster is its Australian supplement. There
is a list of local words and phrases— not com-
plete by any means, and open to many
cavils, but very helpful ; a local gazetteer
compiled by F. J. Broomfield, whose only
fault is annoying abbreviation, carried too
far ; a biographical dictionary, which is on
the whole very satisfactory ; and a local
chronology. The biographical section dis-
closes some ages during 1899
Julian Ashton ...48
Louis Becke ...44

Some of the ladies may be congratulated
on looking remarkably young for their ages.
In Chambers s Biographical Dictionary,
Melba hops up four years, and is only 34.
And surely ? But it is safer not to say.
F. Rollitt complains of the lack of en-
couragement to literary aspirants in Maori-
land—
There is absolutely no Maoriland literature, and
the position of literary men here is a disgrace to
the country. In nearly every town through both
islands there are schools of art and schools of
music, and both the Government and the public
give these a fair amount of support. But neither
public nor Government give a cent to encourage
literature There are in the country several large
and excellent weekly papers, which yield consider-
able profits to their proprietors, but, to the shame
of these proprietors, they do not allow their papers
to assist m any way the literature or the literary
men of their own country. It is true that there is
now being formed a Maoriland Literary Associa-
tmn. I originated the idea myself, planned it,
and brought it before the public, but it is not re-
ceiving so much support as a new football-club
expects.
* * * *
The bald saying that only old countries can pro-
duce poets contains a certain amount of hard truth,
and applies equally to the higher class of prose
writers. Old countries offer all the prizes to
writers ; new countries offer none. A new country
does not attract the artistic or poetic temperament,
but rather men of action—settlers, traders. New
countries do not want makers of verse or of books
but makers of farms and roads. At the same
time, countries even as new as Maoriland should
encourage literature as much as music or paint-
ing. If only the commercial value appeals to the
government or the public, then both can look to
the immense markets of the old world and assist
the writers here to obtain the best prices for
novels, short stories, sketches—just as the farmer
is assisted to find buyers for his butter, cheese,
eggs.
* * * *
Maoriland is so rich in material for the writer
that it is a pity it cannot be utilised. Millions of
people will buy books that tell them of something
new, that give them fresh pictures. From the
kelp-girt shore of southern islands that sentinel
antarctic solitudes to the Auckland Peninsula,
lapt by semi-tropic seas, there are backgrounds to
fit any of them. Lonely, storm-swept rocks, shel-
tered caves, frowning cliffs, broad beaches, for
romances of sealing and whaling and wrecks and
daring voyages. Deep ravines, sombre gorges,
unclimbable mountains, to fit the thousand grand
wild untold tales of gold-digging. Log huts
within sight of pretentious mansions, rich fields
where once was swamp and forest, for stories of
pioneer settlers and man’s struggle with the wil-
derness. Harbours wind-swept and rain-washed
as Preservation and Patterson’s, sun-warmed and
sheltered as Waitemata and Whangaroa, with
cutters and ketches and broad-beamed scows for
sailors and sea-life. The barren dreariness of the
Auckland gum-fields for failures and wasted lives.
The deep swamps with their buried forests for
rich finds of resin. The tree-climbers’ camp in
the shade of giant kauris —all the quaint, strange,
wild features of this industry peculiar to northern
Maoriland. And then —richest of all, perhaps—•
Maori life, and lore. Old legends striking as
Grecian epics. Stories of heroes and heroines, of
magic and monsters, of homeric combats, self-
sacrifice, endurance, battles, sieges, and the fierce
struggles between the Maori and the Pakeha
making history dramatically sad.
* * * *
This is the material Maoriland writers have to
their hand—and more besides ; for there are the
aims and aspirations of the newest of nations, the
life-history of a people whose thoughts in the
future may influence the world. And yet with
such abundance of material, with such glorious
chances, Maoriland has no literature, and her
literary men may starve before the public or the
Government lift a finger on their behalf. Litera-
ture has greater claims for assistance than paint-
ing or music, for these things only reach limited
audiences, whilst the writer speaks to tens of
thousands for all time. The Maoriland painter or
musician can find a market for his work in his own
country ; but there is no market here for litera-
ture. It may be said that the Maoriland writer
has.the world’s markets before him. That is true ;
but the markets are a long way off, and the buyers
are not eager. Besides, there is the question
of training. A writer does not spring into being
fully fledged. He wants schooling and advice
and criticism and encouragement. He gets none
of this in Maoriland, because there is no publica-
tion which receives original matter, and no school
which teaches. If the four great weekly papers
would offer for original literary matter, say, half
as much yearly as they pay for sporting news and
fashion notes, our literary workers would have
some encouragement, and Maoriland would soon
possess a literature ; but whilst the public and the
papers are so blind and mean, the wealth of
material may lie idle and the young literary genius
may starve, and Maoriland remain disgraced as
a country tnat does nothing to encourage its own
literature.
* * * *
But—and but—and but. Some of Mr.
Rollitt’s complaint is sentimentally justified ;
but is it justified by Facts ? When the
Govt, spoon-feeds the farmer, the result can
be definitely judged and measured. If
fleece-quality is improved there is an
increase in the market - price paid for
the wool. If 100 tons of meat are ex-
ported in place of 10, there is an obvious
difference in cash receipts. But where is
the standard for spoon-fed literature ? It is
not the farmer’s standard of sales and pro-
un ’ far th ® vvorst lil; ei-ature often sells best.
Who is to judge what is worthy—especially
6( j /o e it is worthy, when the tyros are
stumbling and learning ? There is much to
be said for the British pension-system for
-proved literary merit ; but for a subsidy-
and-patronage system to the beginner of
unknown merit, is there anything at all to
urge beyond the beginner’s desire to be sub-
sidised ! Surely, surely Mr. Rollitt errs.
And does M.L. Govt, do nothing for the
literary aspirant? What of the £370,000
annually expended in free education, and
the £7OOO annually granted to Universities ?
When the sharpened tools are placed in his
hand, cannot the young author use them ?
* * * *
Mr. Rollitt speaks of Maoriland’s splendid,
unique material for literature. If the “ liter-
aiy man were as splendid and unique as his
material, would he wait for a Govt, endow-
ment before using it ? How Kipling would
smile at the idea of being subsidised by
Govt, to make his studies of Indian native
lite ! He worked because he had the im-
pulse, because he could. ; and because the re-
ward was plain in sight if he succeeded.
hen the Mabriland Kipling comes he will
need no more help than the Indian Kipling.
He will never dream of asking the Govt, to
make a market for his wares : they will make
heir own market. No teaching, no coddl-
ing, can help him. He must teach himself,
and tight with his own hand. Literature is
not to be learnt from formulae : every man
of genius creates his own formulae, and every
man of talent learns by himself how to
alter his predecessors’. Mr. Rollitt wants
to put the cart before the horse. If he and
his comrades write good stories the public is
eager to buy them. And if they write bad
stories, why should the public be taxed to
pay for them ?
* * * *
It is complained that the public prefers to
support music and painting rather than
literature. Well, if the public is built that
way it has a perfect right to do as it pleases.
-Lhe, world was Pistol’s oyster—is every
man s. He is entitled to get out of it just as
much as he can. And it is entitled to give
him just as little as it likes. That is the
hard practical Fact. And there is no ethical
or sesthetical argument as to what might,
could, would, or should be done which com-
pels the public to recede one inch from its
vantage of Fact—unless it chooses. If it
chooses to especially encourage music and
painting, it is because music and painting
seem to give it, or seem likely to give it, a
more satisfactory return. . If Maoriland
literature makes a better bid, it will get a
better price. Let Mr. Rollitt catch that
literature hare, and he may be very sure
the public will help him to cook it.
* * * t
It is somewhat alarming to find a recent
Australian whine so echoed from beyond the
Tasman sea. One hopes the local literary
person is n’t going to make a habit of it, for
he has n’t the least justification.
The fault, dear Brutus, is not in our stars,
But in ourselves, that we are underlings.
There is no country in the world which
takes more interest in literature than Aus-
tralia does ; and Maoriland is quite close
enough to win under Australia’s mantle.
True ; the market for occasional verse and
prose is small and unremunerative. But no
“ literary man ” worth the name ever dreams
of contentment with “ occasional ” verse and
prose. He aims at a book, a dozen books—
some solid achievement. And if Australia
has not yet population enough to pay him
for his labour, there is London £2O away or
less. When an Australian miner can walk
from Townsville to Coolgardie for gold, an
Australian writer can easily work to Europe
for gold and fame—if the Root of the Matter
be in him. If! Anyway, the Whine is
cowardly and silly. When Mr. Micawber
came to Australia he begat far too many
descendants who wait for something to turn
up. Let the local writer go ahead and turn
up something.
P.S.—“ Dad’s ” in town—first time for 4"> mtr.i.
Forty years in the wilderness I—good oJ
Dad !

w Maorilander ” writes—
N.S.W. Public Library is a gloriously managed
or mismanaged, institution, or perhaps the write
is particularly unfortunate. I inquired not long
since for a file of This Bulletin for the previous
half-year. “Not back from the binders,” though
three months had elapsed since the termination
of the period. Asked more recently for The
Bookfellow, and got three different answers from
three different assistants. First man said,
“We don’t get it;” second, when the printed
list was produced, “Couldn’t find it;” and the
third said “it had gone to the binders.” Then
only two numbers of this gigantic monthly had
been published, and the task of binding them
would surely prove too much even for the staff of
the Govt. Printing Office. And why is- it that
the English weeklies, such as t\\e Saturday, Litera-
ture, Spectator, are always—-not occasionally only
—a week or two, sometimes three weeks, later than
in the booksellers, or at other libraries ? Wanted
some copies of the appendices to the journals
of the M.L. Parliament, and am told that a gap
of several years exists in the tile. Last session's
M.L. Hansards and Parliamentary papers might
as well not have been kept at all if they are always
as incomplete and as absolutely without any sem-
blance of order as when last I called for them. As
to the newest books, I am told that the institution
is run for the convenience of a firm of'booksellers,
which may account for delays in getting books.
And, with respect to the selections—are they made
by an American that so much absolute Yankee
trash—to say nothing of ephemeral English stuff—
finds its way there, while many works of authors
who have reached the classical stage, and of others
(particularly in fiction) who are on their way there,
are left out in the cold ? But these complaints are
enough for one day’s growl.
The i npression among many frequenters
of the N.S.W. Public Library is that all new
books and magazines are retained for the
convenience of the trustees and staff, and
when these have had their weeks or months
of leisurely arm-chair perusal, students and
the public are permitted to have a look.
* * •’ •
Since it is announced that “ Rolf Boldre-
wood ” has a new romance, dealing with
Maoriland, in preparation, the time seems
fitting to implore this ex-representative Aus-
tralian author to re-write it five times ant
put a little pith of matter and mettle o ’
style into it—if he can. It is becoming tire-
some to read notices like this in Lond.
Spectator —
Rolf Boldrewood’s” A Romanceof Canvas Town,
like much of his later work, marks a sad falling
off from Robbery under Arms , and is in great part
made up of sheep, sentimentality, and poetica
quotations.
Or this in Literature —
We learn, by the courtesy of the publisher, tnat
Mr. William S. Walker, author of “When the
Mo poke Calls, ” is an Australian and a nephew of
“Rolf Bold re wood.” The careful reviewer might
have guessed a closer relationship. The faults of
that author reappear in an aggravated form; the
construction of the sketches is clumsy, the Eng-
lish is deplorable, and the pathos is laid on with a
trowel.
And when a representative author keeps on
producing bad work he prejudices others
who row in the same bush boat. Ex uno
omnes is a fallacy which the average review-
ing mind will wot eliminate.
* * * •
One of the stories in Mrs. David’s (wife of
Professor David, Sydney University) book
of Funafuti memories has for point the very
literal interpretation of the Bible by the
missionarised, Christianised, Sunday-hatted
natiyes. “A woman,” says Mrs. David,
“ was unhappy in her married life, and ap-
pealed to the native magistrate to set her
free from her husband, who ill-treated her.
The magistrate puzzled over the law as set
down in the Bible, and finally told the
woman that if she wanted to be divorced it
was necessary for her, according to Christian
law, to break the Seventh Commandment.
She did.” Mrs. David seems amused, and a
little shocked. Philosopher Nietzsche would
not have been shocked. One of the quat-
rains in his just-translated “ Poems ” runs—
THE NEW TESTAMENT.
Here the holiest book of prayers,
Weal and sorrow, see !
At its portal stands and stares
God’s adultery.
Who will write the best song, who will paint the
best picture,
Whose music is best ?
He who understands man, knows the heart of him
loves him *
Above all the rest.
Put stars in your song and put skies in your
picture,
Put mountains and seas ;
But one heart-throb that’s tuned to the heart of a
brother
Is greater than these.
What is art, what is art and the artist’s achieve-
ment,
Its purpose and plan ?
.T is the message that’s sent from the heart of the
artist
To the heart of a man
S. W. Fass.
A note from “ Steele Rudd ” —
Dear Bulletin, —You won’t hear much from
me this year; have been “off,” and ordered to
exercise vigorously—am doing so, going strong on
polo, developing a dangerous passion for the sport.
And when there is a horse to belt and bullock
about, Dad and Dave and the selection and the
roo and the Red Page can all slide -and it
won’t matter whether the ’roo swims or whether
lie don’t, or whether the woman murdered in her
bed would have been nicer butchered in the bath
or under the house. Lord, give us plenty sport,
and keep us full and merry, and never mind the
money! Poor Lawson, and that Hard-up Con-
fession of his, nearly made me sad. Would like
to have him in a spring-cart with the winkers off
the horse, just to shake those funeral services out
of Jhis bright head.

OF SUFFERING.
Bayswater, Sydney, October, 1898.
W. H.. Malyon, Esq., Man. Chemist, Ballarat.
Dear Sir, —For years past (about ten), I have been
troubled with “ Acne ” in its worst form, and have sought
advice from the best Australian and London doctors, with
only fair results. A Victorian friend of mine, some four
or five weeks ago, sent me a tin of your “Ligmolliene,”
and speaking very highly of its wonderful curative powers.
I naturally took same “ Cum Grano Salis,” and was in-
clined not to use it ; eventually I tried your ointment
with truly marvellous results, and am now entirely cured ;
and think people who suffer from skin diseases are greatly
indebted to you for your wonderful remedy. I shall
advise all my friends with skin troubles to use same.
Wishing “ Ligomolliene ” all the success it richly deserves,
Yours faithfully, GEORGE J. O’REGAN.
WHAT THE DOCTORS SAY:—
Doveton-street, Ballarat,
My dear Mr. Malyon, — August 21st, 1898.
With great pleasure I now sit down to record progress
with regard to my old enemy, “ Eczema Capitis,” from
which I suffered, notwithstanding constant treatment for
some years under the prescription of Professor M‘Call
Anderson and other eminent dermatologists, with only
partial and temporary relief. When I met you I com-
menced somewhat sceptically your ointment Ligmolliene,
and after about two months’ treatment I may honestly say
my enemy is d.-feated, and I am perfectly well. I will only-
add that since I commenced the treatment of myself I have
prescribed it to others, and am still doing so with brilliant
results. Please accept my hearty thanks, and remain yours
sincerely, THOS. LE GAY HOLT HOUSE, M.R.C.S., L.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Weary World!
The Weary World ! When skies were gaj
She joined the giddy dance of Day ;
Her rippling locks she backward threw,
With merry laugh she fled and flew,
A golden sprite of Mirth and May.
And then again, when skies were gray,
She left her wayward, wanton play,
To rest her slumb rous heart anew—■
The Weary World !
In starry cloak chat drooped away
The Night had wrapt the child of clay ;
And Dreams, as sweet as honey-dew,
Around a radiant freshness drew
And gently lapt her where she lay—
The Weary World !
P. LUFTIG.
Age to Youth.
“Sir, if I were young as you
(Where’s the use of wailing !);
Had such heart and lung as you,
Think you I’d be ailing
Just because a little fount
Earthward sank and left me,
Or a maid of no account
Of her lips bereft me ?”
Love is sweet,
But yet we meet
Many loves in lane and street.
“ Sir, I needs must laugh at you
(Why is youth so callow !),
Old age shakes his staff at you
For a foolish fellow :
All the world is green and gay
’Twas not made to sorrow ;
If you lose a love to-day,
Seek a love to-morrow.”
O'er the skies
A black niij lit lies,
Lawn will come and the sun arise.
“ Sir, I lift my hat to you
(If I stung you —Pardon !).
I was trifling—that to you
I mig't not unburd n ;
But her face was sweet above
All my years remember,
And your violent breath of love
Stirred an old heart-ember.”
Stars are far,
But some things are
Farther still than any star.
RODERIC QUINN.
The South Light.
If you want a taste of torture; if you’d lose the
love of life
With the wild, exultant pleasures of relief ;
If you’re wanting to experience the awfulness of
strife
And the horrifying helplessness of grief ;
Just take a trip to Sydney, and in forty hours or so
You’ll have your brain and body fully fed
With the torments and the terrors of a lifetime-
till you know
That the South-Head Light of Sydney looms
ahead.
When hope is well nigh sped,
And you wish that you were dead,
There’s a flash comes through your porthole
And you find yourself re-wed
To the joys of life instead
And the same old life you led.
Ah ! there’s something left to live for when the
South Light looms ahead.
When the boat was in the basin and you paced
the poop in pride
With a swagger chat was nautical and free,
A damsel in a deck-chair winked her wicked eye
so wide, ,
And you squeezed her as you took her down to
tea.
You were flirting in the Yarra, you were spooning
in the Bay,
You were cuddling as the vessel cleared the
Schanck:
Then your passion and your ardor faded suddenly
away, .
And you didn’t care a button if you sank.
You’ve a day and night in bed.
And a doubly bursting head—
But you ask her where she’s stopping when the
South Light rays are shed ;
And you seize the broken thread.
To repeat the things you said—
You’re a man and she’s a woman, when the South
Light looms ahead.
You don’t know where you come from ; what you’re
doing ; who you are,
As you squirm in hopeless torment in your bunk ;
And your head is throbbing wildly as the engines
jerk and jar, .
Like the sequel to a long extended drunk.
There’s a fortnight’s frolic waiting, but you neither
know nor care; . ,
You forget that ever wine ran rich and red ;
You only know that living is a curse too hard to
Till the South-Head Light of Sydney looms ahead.
Then that bright white light o erspread
On the waters brings a shred .
Of the hopes you felt in Melbourne, and the deck
again you tread.
While your thoughts leave death, instead
To the love of life they’re led ;
Ah ! there's something left to live for when the
South Light looms ahead.
Melb.
McG.

IN AMERICA.
The Legend.
IT was in the summer of 1778, Wash-
ington, with his army ever active,
determined to oppose the British
general, Sir Henry Clinton, who, having
evacuated Philadelphia, was retreating
through New Jersey on New York. The
forces met on 28th June at Monmouth.
La Payette conducted the attack. The
heat was so intense that some refused to
fight, and many fell dead from exposure
to the sun’s rays. Both sides fou-ht
desperately. As the day waned, and the
flagging spirits of La Fayette’s soldiers
were at a low ebb, a strange sight aroused
them. Marching with drum and life
was an old man, Jeremiah Wynton, and
his son, and grandson. The old father,
! with silvery flowing locks, sturdily strode
| through the field. The son with ban-
| daged head fifing lustily, the youth,
jcatching the spirit of his grandsire, rolled
I out with him the soul-inspiring rattle of
j the dium. Father, son, and grandson
J had accompanied the troops as volunteers,
and were ever in the forefront of the
fight. The fierce light of battle was in
their eyes, they knew no fear, and many
a wild cheer arose, as on that fateful
day Yankee Doodle was heard as never
was heard before or since.

SATURDAY, MAY 27, 1899.
The Twenty-two-and-Sixpenny Lie.
One of the most robust and useful inaccura-
cies fathered by the Sydney Anti-Federalists
and their organ, the Datly Telegraph
(which will directly lose at least £5OOO a-year
if the present Federation scheme be accepted,
and fights against it with an utterly frantic
unscrupulousness), is the great Twenty-two-
and-Sixpennv Lie. Said lie is a short, sawn-
off screech resting on no visible evidence, to
the effect that the proposed Federal Consti-
tution, if accepted, will cost every inhabitant
of New South Wales 225. fid. per annum in
extra taxation. The average individual—the
man in the street or the bald-headed indi-
vidual at the back of the omnibus—knows so
little of finance that almost any wild perjury
of this kind will pass muster with a fair pro-
portion of the community. All that is re-
quired is that it should be said loudly and
often—that it should be incessantly shrieked
in big print—that blind leaders of the blind,
and respectable-looking gentlemen who seem
as if they had a character for accuracy to lose
even if they have it not, should say it inces-
santly with the monotonous row of an empty
tin falling eternally downhill—and even so
utterly absurd a prevarication as the Tvventy-
two-and-Sixpenny Lie can do an immense
amount of service.
There are certain circumstances under
which the acceptance of the Federal Consti-
tution now under debate would cost the Corn-
stalk people 225. 6d. per head per annum in
extra taxation, with no compensating advan-
tage. They are these :
(1) If Ihe Federal tariff, when drawn lip, is
akin to the present tariff of South Australia or Victoria—
which is possible.
(2) If New South Wales, which now imports largely
simply because it has a low tariff, «-oes on importing
quite as much or rather more under a higher tariff -
which idea the experience of the Dibbs-MacLaurin tariff
in 1892-3-4-5 has proved to he pure unadulterated rot.
(3) If New South Wales, when it, by virtue of the higher
tariff, has all this extra money to spend, doesn’t puTthe
money to any useful purpose, hut annually throws it into
the sea—if itdoesn’t useit toabolish school fees, and reduce
railway rates, and supply ihe people with roads, bridges
and railways, aud to furnish old-acre pensions, and to
wipe off the deficit, and to relieve local taxation hut is
such an incredible fool that it wastes the whole lot.
(4) And if the New South Wales workman, when cus-
toms duties are put on all imported luxuries, jroes in for
bin illy' oil-paintinys, marble statuary, ehainpayne, silks
and satins, bell-topper bats, grand pianos, organs, harps,
harmoniums, telescopes, microscopes, thermometers,
mathematical instruments, stereoscopes, locomotives
perfumed soap, seltzogenes, marble tombstones, silver
and gold plate, perfumery, jewellery, marble mantel-
pieces, and dozens of other luxuries that are utterly un-
known to him, so as to bear his full share of the new taxa-
tion
If all these things happen—then, and not
otherwise, it is possible for Federation to
cost every inhabitant of New South Wales,
poor and rich alike, about 20s. per head
annually in extra taxation. But not other-
wise. In other words, if the ruling majority
in New South Wales is going to become
hopelessly insane, then the Twenty-two-and-
Sixpenny Lie may become partly true, but
under no other conditions whatever.
* * * *
No country, when under a high tariff, im-
ports so much as it did under a low tariff,
ind New South Wales, being a plain, ordi-
nary country, governed by the same laws as
the rest of the universe, is no exception to
the rule. Therefore, when it is assumed (1)
that the tariff will be more than doubled,
and (2) that this means that the people will
pay more than twice as much as they do
now, the anti-Federal party starts by assum-
ing one thing that it isn’t sure about, and :>ne
other thing that it knows is not true. New
South Wales had a low tariff in Parkes’s
time, a high one under Dibbs, MacLaurin
and co., and a still lower one under Reid.
A.nd the amount of taxation that these
tariffs took from the people was as follows
(Coghlan s New South Wales Statistical
Register for 1898, part 1., page 20) :
1889 ... Parkes’s low tariff £1,905,883 .. P £l 15^6
J 8 - Do. 1,888,321 ... 113 9
1892 ... Dibbs’s high tariff 2,677,890 ... 2 310
1893 Do. 2,127,645 ... 114 3
1894 Do. 2,065,839 ... 112 6
„ ~ . ... at the rate of
Half of ’95 Do. 963,189 ... 1 910
~ , perannum
1897 ... Reid’s low tariff 1,280,715 .. 019 4
1898 Do. 1,256,591 ... 018 10
The odd half-year arises from the awkward
method in which the public accounts are
furnished, and the years 1891 and 1896 are
omitted because they Were transition periods
belonging partly to the high tariff and partly
to the low tariff regime. During the same
period the imports of New South Wales were
as follows :
1889 ... £22,863,057 1894 ... £15,801,941
1890 ... 22,615,004 1895 ... 15,992,415
1891 ... 25,383,397 1897 ... 21,744.350
1892 ... 20,776,526 1898 ... 24,453,560
1893 ... 18,107,035
The moral of which is this : The high Dibbs
tariff produced a greatly increased revenue
for one year only ; after that New South
Wales began to learn how to depend more
on local production and less on imports.
And when it had learned that lesson the re-
sult proved these three things: (1) New
South Wales, under a high tariff doesn’t im-
port more in proportion to population than
Victoria. (2) New South Wales, under a
high tariff, doesn’t pay more customs taxa-
tion per head than Victoria under a high
tariff. (3) Comparing the low tariff taxation
of 1898 with the high tariff taxation of the
first half of 1895, the difference wasn’t
225. 6d. per head but only 11s., and, com-
paring it with 1894, the difference wasn’t
225. 6d. but 13s. Bd. Which is bad for the
Tvventy-two-and-Sixpenny Lie.
* * * *
If the Dibbs-MacLaurin Protectionist
tariff had continuedin existence a little longer
it is quite probable that it would have shown
a still further reduction, considering that its
movement was steadily downwards. And, if
it had been a higher tariff than it was, the
chances are that it would have reduced the
volume of imports faster than it did, and the
burden of taxation would have diminished
faster. But, so far as it got, the results
were that, in 1894, New South Wales was
taxed about 2s. 3d. more per head than Vic-
toria ; in the first half of 1895 it was taxed
about 6d. per head less, both provinces being
under a Protectionist tariff. So the reason-
able inference is that if, under a uniform
Federal tariff, Victoria and South Australia
are taxed at about their present rate, New
South Wales’s customs burden will be about
£2,loo,ooo—say an increase of 11s. per head
per annum on its present outlay. Which is
again bad for the Twenty-two-and-Sixpenny
Lie.
• * » »
The New South Wales Customs-taxation
for the present financial year promises to
reach £1 per head, and the working-man
somehow doesn't seriously notice that there
is any burden at all. In that case, it is just
possible that he won’t notice the extra 11s.
either, or even that ho wouldn’t notice the
imaginary 225. 6d. if there was such a thing
which there isn’t. At all events, what the
masses have to consider is that they needn’t
pay the extra 11s. per head, per annum, or
anything like it. Though it is called 11s.
per head, it will really be £5 per head on
o I P®°P ®’ an< * or eighteenpence,
or 3d °n ° ther s. A huge proportion of it
1 be taxes oil things that are utterly
beyond the reach of the great bulk of the
people. The working-man in New South
a es is already taxed on his beer, tobacco,
spirits, biscuits, candles, currants, •jam, tea
sugar, treacle, and raisins. A tax on wheat
won t affect linn, for New South Wales has
now reached the point where it can dispense
with imported wheat, and the tax will be a
dead letter. The duties on paintings,
statuary, gold and silver plate, and hun-
dteds of other things, will never reach him,
tor lie can’t afford to revel in such luxuries at
any time. In all probability his share of the
extra customs taxation involved in Federation
wont exceed ss. per head, per annum,
vi!V e i t le , * at Man wil l pay the rest.
Which is the reason why the Fat Man in-
vented the Twenty-two-and-Sixpenny Bogey.
Ihe extra taxation incidental to Federation
will hit him a considerable shock on the
pocket, and he wants to scare the Lean Man,
by means of the Twenty-two-and-Sixpenny
Lie, into voting to reject Federation in
older that his (the Fat Man’s) pocket may
be saved.
* • • •
The extra customs taxation upon New
South Wales, with a uniform Federal tariff
something like that of Victoria, will probably
(judging by the results of the Dibbs experi-
ment) be £700,000 a year. Of that the
province, under the wise provisions of the
c !i lus . e > mu « fc get back at least
£500,000. It is for the Lean Man, who is
m the majority, to decide what will be done
with it. He may use it, if he likes, to re-
duce the Fat Man’s land-and-income tax.
Probably, however, he won’t like. He may
use part of it to abolish the 3d. a week
school-fee, which is charged for each youth-
ful member of his family—and, if he does, the
workman with four young cornstalks to pay
for will save about twice as much in this
way as he loses in extra customs taxes,
and will be a pound or two per annum in
pocket. And he may use some more to
help in starting an Old-age Pension system
whereby to keep himself out of the Benevo-
lent Asylum in his latter years; in which case,
instead of paying 225. 6d. a year extra, he
would save about Bs. of 10s. a year. Only
he would be this much better off at the Fat
Man’s expense, and the Fat Mali in his
wrath invented the Twenty-two-and-Six-
penny Fraud to scare the Thin Man from
grasping at the opportunity.
*** * '
The only plea that can be advanced against
this theory is that the smaller States—the
allegedly necessitous States—must have a
very high tariff to enable them to make ends
meet. And this plea won’t bear examina-
tion. The above calculation is based on the
theory that the Victorian tariff is adopted all
round. That tariff yields £1 10s. 2d. per
head annually, or, if customs and excise are
added together, £1 15s. lid. per head. The
latest customs and excise returns for five
provinces are :
„ .. . . Per head per annum.
South Australia £i 13 5
Victoria 1 15 n
Tasmania 2 3 11
Queensland [ 2 14 7
VVestralia ... 6 5 9
A customs tariff which yielded £1 15s. lid.
per head would satisfy New South Wales,'
Victoria, and South Australia without fur-
ther taxation, and it is this tariff which would
cost the Lean Man in New South Wales
something like ss. per annum, or less, with
the prospect of getting it all back again in
remission of school fees and in old-age pen-
sions and other advantages. Tasmania could
live on it also ; the island can raise its land-
tax from |d. to Id. in the £, and, moreover,
it had last year a gorgeous surplus equal to
nearly 11s. per inhabitant, and with nearly
11s. to spare and some resources in the way
of direct taxation to fall back upon, the re-
duction of its customs taxation by Bs. isn’t
utterly hopeless. Queensland can live on
£1 15s. lid. per inhabitant, and make up the
difference by levying land and income taxes.
And Westralia, having no land or income
taxes or probate duties, has all these possible
sources of revenue with which to fill up the
gap, if there is one ; moreover, as the Com-
monwealth offers Westralia special terms by
allowing it to retain its border-customs for
some years, it doesn’t require to come into
the calculation at present. All the wild
whoop about the fabulous amounts which it
is necessary to raise to keep the “small
States ” afloat vanishes into thin air when
the figures are carefully inspected. And the
Twenty-two-and-Sixpenny Lie goes with it.
The Spider and the Fly.
That truly awful Tory, L. F. Heydon, of the
N.S. W. House of Fossils, is carrying the
Cabbage-tree Mob’s crusade into Queensland,
and his anguished appeal to the Banana pro-
vince not to federate on any terms is pathetic.
In the congenial atmosphere of Brisbane Tele-
graph (May 15), Mr. Heydon tells the Q.
people a number of things which don’t improve
his reputation for scrupulous accuracy. He

PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Oppressed Qutlander and the
Oppressed Victorian.
They held a meeting in Melbourne, last week,
to express sympathy with the oppressed Out-
landers and their political grievances in the
Transvaal. It was a curious meeting, and the
grief of sundry heavy city magnates over these
woes in the Boer republic was harrowing to
behold. In the Transvaal any white man, even
without property, may get a vote for the Lower
House after he is naturalised and has taken the
oath of allegiance, has put in two years’ resi-
dence, and paid £2 registration-fee. These
terms look a trifle harcT, still, in return, he
becomes a full-blown second-class burgher—an
elector of the Lower House, and enjoys all the
privileges of One-man-one-vote under Oom
Paul’s beneficent Government: —
In Victoria, the same individual can grefc a fraction ot a
vote for the Lower House by being naturalised and taking
the oath, and putting in from 12 to 18 months' residence,
instead of two years. That is to say, if he is a stranger
without property, he can get from one-half to one-eightieth
of the voting-power which may be enjoyed by the fellow
with property. The Transvaai law, when it takes in the
strange voter after two years, makes him a full elector so
far as the Lower House is concerned. The Victorian law
makes him a fraction of an elector—a remnant or hit or
scrag-end—so far as its Lower House is concerned.
Then there are other wrongs suffered by the
stranger in the Transvaal. After he becomes a
free and independent citizen and a voter
for the Lower House, he may, in another
12 years, and by permission of the Upper
House, become a first-class burgher with a vote
for each House—as large a vote as that of any
other citizen—and be eligible for a seat in
either House. His children have to arrive at
this dazzling distinction in the same gradual
fashion, but his grand-children are born as
complete citizens of the Republic.
In Victoria, unless a man has property, he may wait
120 years instead of 12, and be no nearer complete citi-
zenship. Victoria not only refuses a Council vote
to the non-propertied stranger, but also to the non-
propertied Victorian. It takes the alien, even with pro-
perty, over three years to attain complete citizenship.
Only a comparatively small fraction of the people ever
reach that lofty eminence, and the average man who does
get there receive a fraction of a vote —in other words, he
gets one Council vote while the immensely Fat Man is
allowed as many as 14. And whereas the grandchildren
of the alien who becomes a Transvaal citizen start fair as
first.-class burghers, the alien who settles in Victoria or
the original Victorian himself hands down his disabilities
to his descendants for all time —while the present law
stands.
So the meeting of humorless Fat Men of Mel-
bourne expressed their grief at the extensive
disfranchisement of Upper House voters under
the Transvaal law (the number of males dis-
franchised in Victorian Council elections is
greater than the whole male population of the
Transvaal), and hoped the British Government
would interfere on behalf of these British citi-
zens who are deprived of their political rights
in Oom Paul’s Land ! And if Paulus Smit,
and Johannes Pretorius, and Hans Muller,
and a few people like them met at Pretoria and
asked the British Government to interfere on
behalf of the disfranchised citizens of Victoria,
Collins-street would be strewn with dead Fat
Men who had perished from apoplexy brought
on. by sheer rage at the presumption of Pre-
torius and Smit.

More 2d. Postage Spectre.
Dr. MacLaurin on a new phase of the Federal
high postage :
I believe you will have to pay 2d. on every letter. It
will be impossible for Federation to adopt the penny
postage. We would not have the privilege of send-
ing our letters for Id. after Federation. 1 make that
statement because it would cost the Commonwealth
£350,01)0 a year to establish a uniform penny post-
age. That is the estimate of the statistician and
the postal authorities in Sydney, after making an
allowance for increased business under the lower rate.
Supporters of the Bill are reluctant to believe that it will
cost you anything, and in addition to that, they say the
Commonwealth can make Australia a present of this.
Everyone knows a penny postage cannot be established,
and as the rates must be uniform, it will cost the people
of New' South Wales £lOO,OOO.
Here the embittered Caledonian mixes tw’O
things up and tries to make them look like one.
At present, in N.S. W., Queensland, Tasmania,
and W.A., there is penny postage over a small
part of the province and twopenny postage over
the rest. At first MacLaurin said that the
Commonwealth must go in for 2d. postage all
round —that, quite as a matter of course, Vic-
toria and S. A. would impose their system on
the other four States. Now he says the same
thing a little differently :
(1) The Federal postage must be uniform (which is
true : it must be uniform throughout Australia just as it
is now’uniform throughout N.S.W.).
(2) It must be 2d. all round, or else Id. all round. For
some unexplained reason, though four provinces can
quite easily charge Id. town postage and 2d. country
postage, and five provinces used to do so, six
provinces can’t do it. It is quite impossible—Heaven
and MacLaurin only know why. MacLaurin might
as reasonably sav that, under uniform Federal post-
age it will' be impossible to charge different rates
for light and heavy letters, papers, books, and parcels,
and that, under uniform railway rates, a railw'ay-ticket
to Newtown must cost just the same as one to Melbourne.
He somehow forgets this. And having started by saying
this ridiculous absurdity the rest follows quite naturally.
The Liberal Australian Senate.
Casual prevarication in passing, by the Sydney
Telegraph (the paper which will lose £5OOO a
year or so if the Bill passes) :
The argument is often announced that the proposed
SENATE will be more liberally constituted than those of
other Federations.
It is not true. „
The German Bundesrath (or Upper House) represents
the State Governments, but NOT THE PRINCIPLE of
EQUAL REPRESENTATION. Twenty-six States have 58
members ; 17 of those 58 represent Prussia. _
The Canadian Senate consists of 81 (nominated) Sena-
tors from eight States ; and 48 of those Senators represent
the two States of Ontario and Quebec. No equal repre-
sentation there.
The Swiss “ Nationalrath,” or Upper House, is com-
posed of members chosen in direct election at the rate of
one member for everv 20,0110 souls.
The United States Senate is constituted on the Conven-
tion plan. Each State Legislature appoints two members
for six years, which is the same as having them elected
by the State voting as one electorate.
But what about the power to dissolve the Senate 7
Well, what good is that, while the small States can hold
out by re-electing the Senators while fighting for them 7
Such a specially versatile perverter as the
Sydney anti-Federal organ has hardly a right
to say that anything in this world is not true—
but that may pass. The special points in the
above tozer are these :
The Federation crowd doesn’t say that the principle of
State representation in the Upper House prevails in all
Federations; only in 14 out of 16.
In Germany the largest State (Prussia) has 17 Upper
House representatives for 32,000,000 people ; the smallest
(Schaumburg-Lippe) has one representative for 41,000.
So the latter has 46 times its arithmetically-correct share
of representat ion in the German Bundesrath. Tasmania
and W.A. will have only eight times their arithmetically-
correct share in the Australian Senate.
The statement re Switzerland is straight-out falsehood.
Switzerland's Senate is the Standerath (the Nationalrath
is the Lower House), and in the standerath every canton,
irrespective of size or population, has tw’O members.
(Anv tired person can refer to the “ Statesman s Year
Book” for 1898, pages 995 and 996.)
The equal Senatorial representation system also pre-
vails in the United States, Mexico, Chili, Argentina,
Bolivia, Columbia, Ecuador, Nicaragua, Peru, Uruguay,
Venezuela, and Brazil.
If the Australian Senate, where the small States have a
lanre share of power, conies in collision with the House
of Representatives, the country doesn't depend on the
dissolution of the Senate to bring it to reason. Neither
does it depend on the dog-tax, nor on the laws for the sup-
pression of Flossies and phylloxera, nor on the regula-
tions for eradicating ticks, nor anything else equally
ridiculous and irrelevant. It will settle the business, if
necessary, at the Joint Sitting of the two Houses, where
the vote of the two states that have the majority of popu-
lation can always carry the day against the four States
which haven’t the majority.
In the United States, the Senate, in which the States
have equal representation, is supreme, because-it can’t be
dissolved, and there is no referendum, and no joint sitting
of the two Houses by which the Chamber which repre-
sents people can vote down the one which represents
states. In Australia the Senate won’t be supreme, be-
cause it can be dissolved ; and there is a joint sitting of
the two Houses in case of deadlocks, at which joint sitting
the House which represents the majority of the people
can, by its vastly superior numbers, wipe the floor with
an obstructive State House. Also, the Lower House can
refer matters to be settled by the people’s vote without
the Senate’s permission.
The awful example of the United States Senate
is a scarecrow that has seen its best days.
Under the Draft Constitution, with its joint
sitting and its popularly-elected and dissolvable
Senate, and its Referendum, the Upper House
can't boss the situation. The fact that the
non-dissolvable and not popularly-elected U.S.
Senate, with no referendum and no joint sitting,
can do so has no relation to the case, any more
than the fact that the Afghan monarch can
boil his subjects alive without trial proves that
the British monarch can do likewise. They
are both monarchs. Also the other things are
both Senates. Only they are different kinds of
monarchs and different kinds of Senates.
The Old Cat Regime.
“ A Mere Man ” to The Bulletin
Whenever neglect or callousness is alleged against any
public institution, it is fashionable to shriek for the pre-
sence of women on the committee of management. The
utter fallacy of this is nowhere better shown than at the
Melb. Women’s Hospital, where women, and women only,
rule the roost. A seat on the Women’s Hospital com-
mittee is a hall-mark of Society more precious than a
seat on the dais at Guvmentouse, and the capacity of the
board to properly and humanely manage the affairs
of an institution which has taken more than its
share of public svmpathy and cash is in inverse
ratio to its social status. The horrible bungle
of a few weeks ago, when a patient was practically
left to die on the mat, has been followed by another
act even more disgraceful. At its last meeting
this delectable aggregation of respectability and saint-
liness calmly resolved that in future all patients should
be required" to produce their marriage-certificates when
presenting themselves for admission !! One puzzled
member asked what the unmarried ones were to do, but
the only answer was in the form of a tip from another
with a bigger heart than the rest, who said, “ We’ll have
lots of borrowed ones, that’s certain.” The effect of the
decision, as understood by the general public which knows
its Women’s Hospital committee, was that unmarried
mothers were to be calmly fired into the streets, while
their offspring, if unlucky enough to live, became hope-
less cripples, and the abortionist and the infanticidist
waxed fat. Certain unfashionable persons consequently
began making impolite remarks about the inhumanity of
the committee, whereupon said committee, suddenly re-
membering the matter of Govt, subsidies and private
contributions, tumbled over itself in its eagerness to state
that it didn’t mean that at all. According to the com-
mittee’s explanation, which must be taken with a smile,
all that was needed was the certificates of the supposedly-
married applicants —the single ones could come in as
before. Great trouble, it was remarked, had been caused
by single women saying they were married-and thus
avoiding the inquisition of the committee into their
morals, in addition to fraudulently getting a ticket with
“ Mrs.” on it at the foot of their beds. The decep-
tion was not one to be any longer countenanced, said
the ultra-virtuous Women’s Hospital commit tee, and the
marriage-lines would have to be produced in future be-
fore any self-styled married woman would be admitted.
What the devil the committee has got to do with the
marital condition of any patient is a question to which
Premier Turner (as supporter of the Govt, subsidy) and
the public (as supplier of the rest of the cash) will em-
phatically demand an answer. The fashionable and
curiosity-filled committee of this hospital will have to be
summarily taught that it is there simply to manage
the institution as effectively and humanely and economi-
cally as possible, and that, whether the necessitous appli-
cant. for admission is single, married, or polyandrous is
no more its business than the rearrangement of the
ellipsis. “ Mary pity women,” indeed, when their own
sex is caring for them !
Dr. Forbes Winslow’s protest, in his recently-
published “ Mad Humanity,” against the care-
lessness with which sufferers from mental
disease are treated, is strikingly illustrated by
recent tragedies in Vic. and S.A. The patient
in that case got up early in the morning, bor-
rowed a cleaver from his nephew, and hacked
himself, his wife, and their two children to
death. From the medical evidence :
About two years ago he developed symptoms showing
organic, disease of the brain. Sometimes he became para-
lysed on the right side, and for a time lost the power of
speech. Under treatment, the paralysis rapidly cleared
off but he was not thoroughly recovered ; his speech re-
mained indistinct. After that, he occasionally consulted
witness for headache, but would not receive treatment
lon-' enough. One day last week he consulted witness,
and'there were signs of returning paralysis. In cases of
this kind symptoms of insanity might arise at any
moment.
As Winslow says, most people would rather
own up to a criminal relative than a mad one,
and so take all sorts of care to hide the fact of
the existence of the disease—with results like
these. Only in most cases we haven’t yet come
to habitually regard madness as a disease ; we
hang on to stupid old fables regarding it.
In one case at recent Melb. Sessions an old man
was charged with assault by a damsel of 15, his
employee. She admitted having returned to
work for a few days after the occurrence, and
also that she did not tell her mother till over a
fortnight afterwards. Also, the mother appar-
ently thought so little of the matter, or credited
her daughter’s word so slightly, that she laid
no complaint for over two months, having mean-
while seen accused and tried to induce him to
do something for the girl. Evidence was given
by a disinterested witness that accused was en-
cumbered with a damaged arm in a sling on
the date of the alleged occurrence, and an em-
ployee swore that 'the girl did not reach the
factory till hours after the time she dated the
matter. The accusation was characterised by
defendant as an attempt at blackmail, and the
jury, after a short consultation, duly acquitted.
At the same Sessions, an elderly woman swore
that an unknown young man called at her house
early in the morning, that she struggled with
him for six hours (!), and yet there were neigh-
bors each side and her own children in the
house. There was no corroboration of this re-
markable narrative, and Judge Hood promptly
directed the jury to acquit. This matter of the
prurient, small female and the blackmailing
woman grows tedious, but so long as the law
permits women to make unfounded charges
without fear of punishment, so long will this
sort of thing continue to disgrace the courts,
and just so long will men be subjected to ex-
pense, anxiety, and possibly wrongful convic-
tion and punishment.
: *
Premier Reid’s classification of N.S.W. unem-
ployed is characteristic, because not original.
The same old commonplace has been repeated
for the last six hundred years, and the Laborers’
Act was passed by the English Parliament in
the 14th century to deal with the men who
“ could work and wouldn’t,” because they
couldn’t get their price. “Bluff King Hal”
and Elizabeth hanged “ masterless men.”
Premier Reid proposes to be more merciful,
and reduce them to slavery in “farms and
workshops.” Premier Reid is going to appoint
a committee, and it will not be hard to foretell
the result of its labors. It will be lound that
there are very few who can work and won’t.
Ninety-nine per cent, of those whom ordinary,
unobservant persons ciu&s under that head have
some mental or physical detect which unfits
them for ordinary labor. Leaving the dipso-
maniacs altogether out of the question, there
are, in every Australian province, thousands of
men with defective eyesight, weak hearts, ex-
citable brains, and obscure defects of other
kinds well-known to medical men, who are
quite incapable of hard work of any kind.
The large-electorate policy, which is part of
The Bulletin’s idea of a Leg. Council —the
principle, too, under which the Federal Senate
will be returned—has had a help-on as a result
of S.A. Premier Kingston’s hard fight in West
Adelaide. Kingston’s colleague is Labor-leader
Batchelor. Besides being a Labor strong-
hold, in no electorate is the R.C. influence
greater than in West Adelaide ; and the third
candidate was R.C. and no more. Consequently,
a man who stands head-and-shoulders above
every other politician in the province (with,
perhaps, a doubtful one or two exceptions), and
who any day would command far more voles in
a province-poll than any other, has been in
great danger of being absolutely left out of
Parliament—dropped between two little local
stools. And on the other side of the street a
politician whom everybody describes as a nice
old gentleman —one who shakes hands with all
his constituents and knows the number of teeth
every baby in the district has—was never for
one hour uncertain of his seat.
“F.5.D.,” concerning an important land-grab
question: —
That Sydney is destined to becotne vastly greater and
more populous than she is now seems sine. And there
is nothing' more certain also than that the inhabitants of
the great Svdnev of the future will curse the municipal
apathv and stupiditv of their predecessors who allowed
all the foreshores of her harbor to become alienated, so
that she possesses hardly a single decent water-frontage,
drive or promenade as a public possession. The tune
will come when she will have to dig deep into her purse
to redeem lands squandered and sacrificed to land-
jobbers in times gone by. But that is past. The aliena-
tion has taken place, and therewith enough. It will not
be believed, however, that North Sydney Council has
advised a still further alienation of a most iniquitous de-
scription. The case stands thus : After long litigation, a
certain Mr. Milson has failed to get possession of the
whole foreshore of Cremorne promontory, _ owing to
the salutary hundred-foot reservation. “ But," murmurs
this rich old gentleman, “think of the thousands
of pounds worth of valuable building sites I thereby
have to forfeit on thecharniiugMosraan Bay.” And he offers
to Govt. 4J acres of more-or-less unavailable land at the
end of the promontory, in exchange for the whole remain-
ing 9 acres or so of the most splendid water-frontage.
Sidney Daily Telegraph says it wouldn’t be nearly so
bad a bargain as it looks, and North Shore Council (also,
no doubt, out of purest, noblest patriotism) thinks Mr.
Milson’s offer a most worthy and disinterested.one, and
advises the exchange. Once done, the matter is irreme-
diable. Ail that I hope is that this folly (I would fain
use a harder name) should be nipped in the bud once for
all. Let the people of Sydney know what they possess in
this Cremorne promontory, and its waterside, one of the
most lovely in the world, and most accessible to their
city. If they want Mr. M.’s 4 acres, let them buy them
from him ; but, in the name of all the gods, let them not
throw away this splendid public inheritance, which a for-
tunate accident has saved out of the wreck of the public
domains. Let them, before permitting it, inquire into
the matter, and oust from power some of those who coun-
sel and permit such betrayal of the public interest.
Six members of the Amalgamated Workers’
Union, the chief Westralian goldfields’ trades
union, have been killed while at work within a
month; and this body begins to say things.
Three in particular—(l) that timbering (of drives
especially) is neglected ; (2) that the State mine-
inspection is a good deal of a farce ; (3) ditto as to
mine-accident inquests. As to the latter, the
request is made that the Association should be
represented at all enquiries. The official con-
tention is that the workmen are sufficiently repre-
sented by the mining inspector, .who always
attends ; but, as a matter of fact, since it is the
inspector’s duty to see that things are safe, he is
often naturally interested in proving they arc safe.
So the blame goes on to the dead man with
wearying regularity.
A pathetic plaint from British Papua-
written by a correspondent who “doesn't think
much of the new Governor ” :
Dear Bulletin. —I would like to get an opinion on my
four grievances doctor, ."medicine, storekeeper, and
Government. As regards tlib first, there is no .such
person on or near this goldtield (Gira, Mamba n\ er),
though there is a Govt, official who sends out bills—
medicine so much and advice so much, but he is not a
qualified man, and I understand he charges also for
medicine and advice to hospital patients (said hospital
erected by subscription and Govt, assistance), and should
like to know if he is officially authorised so to do. As
regards medicine, laudanum, in constant use here, is
sold without any name of chemist or maker. I have
drunk half a bottleful neat (size of 1/IJ4 Brown’s
chlorodvne bottle) and could not get a sleep out
of it. ' Valuable stuff, in 10 or 15-drop doses, to
save a man’s life from dysentery, eh? Also, if skin-
diseases are bad here, the ointments are worse. Any
sort of stuff put up in pots, with and without names, and
3s. to pay for as much as would go on a florin. Again, 1
have never seen such miserable tucker on any diggings,
and costing 6d. per lb. carriage for a trip ttiat can be
done by white men in six hours or less. Good opening
here for a go-ahead storekeeper, who understands diggers.
It’s not the price that bothers, but the quality and
scarcity. Milk (the sick man’s life) ’and vegetables we
have not seen for months. As for Govt.— and Governor-
well, perhaps the less said the better ! Then, as to the
field itself : We are not four miles from the original rush.
There were two good gullies got here, about New Year,
that panned out about. llOoz. each for 20 claims, hut
there’s no “ fossicking ’’-ground near them. I’ve been
here about nine months, and have never made more than
tucker, and there are lots more like me. There Are about.
150 of us waiting for a new rush, and 30 per cent,
sick—some with a disease which has killed many, hut
which we don’t understand. Swelling in feet, legs, and
stomach, arid flesh goes doughy, so as to retain a finger-
mark for some time. If any of your medical readers can
give this complaint a name, and cure, he will confer a
hoon upon us poor far-away wretches.— Digger Forlorn

The Liars ” is the Broughs’ latest play.
Without intending to be moral, it seems to be
working off a theory that came in
with Moses on Mt. Sinai about leaving the wife
of the other man alone. Unfortunately for every-
one who wants to be converted, the theory is put
into the mouth of a happy little man who is going
to marry the pretty widow of an old friend. He
works off yards of long-winded eloquence against
men who love women whose husbands have not
been as thoughtful as his own adored one’s, and
paints lurid pictures of the dreadful end of
people who run away with each other without
benefit of clergy. If the aforesaid woman hadn’t
been a widow at the time of the play the argu-
ments might have impressed somebody. Apart
from these speeches, which fall from Brough’s
lips for the most part, the play concerns
itself cleverly with a thin plot about a mar-
ried woman who dined away from home with
another man. Her husband’s brother wanders in
accidentally, exclaims, “George shall know of
this ! and rushes off to let George know. As a
rule, the tale-bearing biz. is committed to an ugly
sister, or a cousin, or an aunt. It is a novelty to
see the big brother with a beard telling tales on
the pretty wife. Then the married woman has to
rush out and drag in all the Liars she can find to
prove that she was not dining there alone with the
other man, because they were there too, and by
that time the third act arrives, and the Liars all
come on together, and the play throws off its one
full, strong, satisfactory situation. Anyone who
expects to be assisted to a few new lies, or a better
way of telling old ones, will be sure to find he is
as good a liar as anybody in this play, and will
go home happy on that account. And on that
account, and because the play is a cleverly-written
one, with a few quiet, unstrained efforts, and
many peppery epigrams chiefly directed against
women, this seems likely to be the success of the
Brough season.
* ♦ * *
Mrs. Brough, as the married woman with
a husband who doesn’t understand, plays natur-
ally, and sheds real wet tears when her lover and
she are parted. It isn’t a great part, and Mrs.
Brough can fit it exactly. Brough, as the inter-
fering friend, rises as far as the playwright will
allow. He gets the long sentiments off his chest
at every opportunity, and yet he creates a sympa-
thetic interest for himself by his tender method of
handling bottles of old port, and hugging his best
girl. In Brough’s hands Sir Christopher is the
character of tne play. Lovell makes a good-
looking ha,nger-on, and anybody can see that
IS . frying all he knows to be passion-
ately m love, and he does pretty well, but there’s
a lot of old wood in Lovell that wants burning out.
Leslie Victor, as Freddy, is a thing of joy, well-
drawn and only slightly over-played ; and, as it
is LmmaTemple who marries him, and requests
him to “ subside,” the reason why any woman
married him isn’t a matter of such importance as
it might otherwise have been. George Majeroni’s
I rencli waiter is an excellent piece of acting ;
.Joseph Carne leaves the best part of himself out-
side of his stiff, unconvincing sketch of Lady
Jessicas husband ; and Miss Bessie Thompson
puts a nice finish on her slight part. Also, Wini-
fred Austin’s lady’s-maid looks as if she is taking
part m a breaking-up show ; and Miss Vara
xtennie rather plays havoc- with the pretty widow
who is going to marry Brough.

The “Three Musketeers,” which blazed
forth at Sydney Criterion on Thursday night
ought to have been the great dramatic event of
the week, and under better circumstances it cer-
tainly would have been. There being material
for several dozen dramas in Dumas’ half-ton
story Mr. Espmasse fixed on the episode of
Milady, and Anne of Austria’s diamonds, and the
gorgeous Buckingham’s infatuation, and lie has
made a very clever play out of this legend. It
doesnt follow the book closely—but then the
book branches off into so many side issues, and
wanders through such great wildernesses of
romance, that the person who followed it closely
wouldn.t get home till three o’clock next morn-
ing. The present dramatist starts with the mar-
riage of the Comte de la Fere to the siren with
i', e b^ ded a - rm ’ whioh isn,t real] y in the tale at
all. Milady, instead of scooting with two of the
Queens jewels, burgles the whole 12; and in
place of Buckingham getting them duplicated,
which is a slow and prosaic process at the best
D Artagnan recovers them in single combat with
the siren who gets a considerable part of her
clothes dragged off in the scuffle. The blood-
thirsty adventures of the four musketeers on the
road, the ambuscades, the siege of Athos in the
hotel where he drinks 300 bottles of liquor and
smashes the rest, and most of the thrilling doings
on horseback can’t be crowded in—in fact, the
only visible horse in the story is the hereditary
greeny-yellow charger of the D’Artagnan family.
Ihe story of D’Artagnan’s quarrel with Milady
has been toned down beyond recognition—Mr.
Lspmasse would have got six months if it hadn’t!
But with all these necessary deviations the
Criterion version is a very excellent one. In
particular, the downfall of Richelieu is a strong
dramatic episode. Milady comes back in
triumph with the locked box which used to
contain the diamonds that Anne of Austria
gave to her lover, and the Cardinal falls from
his usual sagacity and takes it for granted that
the jewels are inside, which they aren’t. There-

** * *
w Lives ’ ” the latesfc Bland
Holt effort at Melbourne Royal, is an original
melodrama m so far that trouble falls heavily upon
everybody except Miss Watson, as the Wicked
Stepmother. Owing to this successful interloper,
Miss Ross gets cut off with her father’s curse. Also
Albert ]S orman and Bland Holt are reduced to
selling newspapers, Mrs. Bland Holt loses her
billet as parlormaid, and the confiding Baronet is
murdered m the last act, leaving the stepmother
aforesaid to inherit the bulk of his property and
marry, again. The authors of “How London
Lives have tried to remedy the awful injustice
of their plot by putting the bad woman into
handcuffs at the finish, but seeing that she has
done nothing to go to prison for the Crown
is bound to enter a nolle prosequi after the curtain
tails, and then she will return to the marble halls
other late Baronet and rejoice over his decease.
Moreover, she will have the satisfaction of bring-
ing her unfaithful paramour, Arthur Styan, to the
gallows, which is about all that any jealous woman
can wish for. The triumph of Sin seems complete.
This is a drama without a noble-minded son in it
consequently Walter Baker is conspicuous by his
absence from the cast. Baker not being there
Miss Ross marries Albert Norman, who re-
tires from his hungry home early in the
second act and says he is going to South
Atrica on business. He makes so much
mystery about his business that he is, pro-
bably, bolting with a newer girl. Anyway, the
drama sees him no more, and there are some
reasons for suspecting that he was lost on the
voyage out, if he ever went out at all.
♦ * * 9
Norman having passed away in silence,
Bland Holt takes care of Miss Ross for the sake
of old times, because he was the man who saved
her from a watery tank when she tried to drown
her parents’ curse. In fact, Bland Holt is the
only visible hero attached to the drama, and, if he
had any money, he would take up a commanding
position all the time. Under the circumstances
he can only pawn his clothes to pay the lady’s
rent, and, although he persuades his Hebrew uncle
to advance 2s. on a hard felt hat, the transaction
brings him no glory worth mentioning. Virtue is
its own reward in “How London Lives.” Brunton
has painted some excellent pictures for this cheer-
ful tale or woe—notably the rural setting of the
watery tank and a street scene where numerous
buggies are loaded up with special editions of the
evening paper containing a full account of a ship-
wreck. Thirty people, in addition to the principals,
rush around the stage at one time or another, and
the horrors of the pawnshop will help to keep the
Royal warm until further notice.

** * *
An outbreak of fine religious frenzy (a
sectarian lecture by an actor) at Melb. Alexandra
was followed by “The Sixth Commandment ” on
Saturday. The present writer is a bit shaky on
the Commandments, and has long forgotten
whether No. G was a long or a short one. Dan
Barry’s drama doesn’t carry the meaning of its
title on its troubled brow, consequently the
present scribe is not quite certain where the Law
of Moses comes in, seeing that the villains of the
piece are seldom breaking fewer than three Com-
mandments at one fell swoop. Daniel B. and the
rest of the revival made a hopeful start on Satur-
day, but tlie Alexandra of late is a mausoleum of
blighted hopes and a monument of woe. All the
same, Dan may be looked to for another stupend-
ous attraction next Saturday if the prosperity of
the Commandment relaxes in the meantime.

A new sketch, with 20 girls appertain-
ing to it, has been introduced at the Bijou,
also delightful little Lottie Moore and Bellman
are engaged on something that is either new or
much the same thing. The somersaults of
Lottie aforesaid can never grow old in any case.
Six nights a week Lottie's feet fly upward like
the sparks. There are 52 weeks in the year,
and two feet to a Lottie, and no doubt the little
lady has lost all sense of novelty in the proceed-
ing and would fain dispense with her somersault
for a while. But the inexorable gallery cries
“Over!” just as though she were a game of
cricket, and Lottie goes over like a lamb. These
remarks are by way of introduction. to the usual
weekly announcement re Cinque vail i. The King
of Jugglers is still booming at the Bijou, and the
management describes the boom as “boundless
and interminable.”

One hears people complaining that melo-
dramas are all the same now as they were 20 years
ago—and yet how old-fashioned “The Harbour
Lights ” seems by comparison with Bland Holt’s
recent productions ! “The Harbour Lights ” asks
to be taken seriously, like a Famij.y Herald story.
The modern melodramas are not so foolish as to
try to appeal to the hearts of intelligent people.
They merely profess to provide entertainment for
all classes. And they do it, as a rule. Take
“When London Sleeps,” for instance. A pre-
posterous story, but a most amusing drama.

Next week Captain Squeaker will be here, and I know

fer the devil. Halleloojer 1 ”
Poor old Foveaux, the Melbourne violinist, who has
ever lived a simple, inoffensive life, has fallen upon dire
poverty in his old age. He is now 80, has been teaching
the young idea for the past 40 years, and has turned out
some excellent players, who ought to remember him now
in his necessity. Any Bulletin readers who would ease
bran’s journey can send subscriptions to
Melbourne Age.
Cinquevalli and Dante have formed a quite warm pro-
fessional friendship in Melbourne. They like one another
and their social interests run in the same groove. They
are both anxious to have as little as possible to do with
the wine which maketh glad the heart of man at night
and enlargeth his head next morning.
Melbourne showmen smile at the amateur management
of the Castles concert. The fact of 1800 tickets, at ss.
each, for the concert of 23rd inst., being sold before the
box-plan was opened, is proof-positive that several
hundred seats could have been auctioned at fancy prices
—and it was done in the case of the Bendigo concert.
Perhaps the Melbourne management did, in a Shake-
spearian way of speaking, perceive a divided duty, and
thought it advisable to err on the conscientious side.

A very remarkable feature of L. F. Hey-
don s anti-Federal appeal in the Brisbane Tele-
graph is his cry to the Banana people not to
unite on the ground that the Federal capital is to
be in Melbourne-which it isn’t. If he had told
them that it was to be in Sydney, he might have
struck a better scarecrow. It was the selfish and
brutal tyranny of the Sydney Government which
drove both Queensland and Victoria into separa-
tion, and now L. F. Heydon urges Queensland
not to come back because (he says) she wop’t have
the pleasure of being ruled by her old tyrant, but
will be bossed (so Heydon alleges) from Mel-
bourne.
