The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The N S.W. anti-Federal party has actually
contrived to find a new perjury which, for a party that
seemed to have already used up every possible untruth,
is rather remarkable. The present idea is
(1) That the Commonwealth Bill by some mistake does
not make it absolutely imperative that the excise duties,
like the customs duties, shall be uniform.
(2) That consequently the Commonwealth may let each
State run what excise duties it pleases.
(3) That N.S.W., being a drivelling lunatic and the
idiot of the Australasian family, will necessarily charge
higher excise duties than Victoria, and so its manufac-
tures will be crushed out while those of Victoria will
thrive greatly.
The two starting points of every anti-Federal argument
are _(l) That N.S.VV. is the richest, most intelligent, and
in every way most brilliant province of the group, and
therefore should dictate terms to all its neighbors ; and
(2) that it is such an unspeakable fool in every respect
that it is going to be swindled by everybody with abso-
lute impunity.
* * * •
By later advices, it looks as if the miserable
cable mixed things up about the Maoriland year’s surplus
(reported last week as £274,717), and the actual surplus
for the year is now reported as £327,917, or £'399,717, or
something like that. The S.M. llerald’s Maoriland cor-
respondent states, in last Monday’s issue, that the revenue
was £5,186,428 and expenditure £4,858,511, and he cheer-
fully adds that the surplus is therefore £399,717. The
average influential paper’s arithmetic is a fearful and
wonderful thing.
* * * •
The N.S.W. courts have decided that when a
shipowner is carrying provisions of any kind as cargo,
and part of the cargo goes had in transit, he is fineable
under the law which smites any person having in his
possession victuals unfit for human food. This opens up
many awful possibilities for the shipowner. He is bound
to carry provisions or else he must reject so much trade
that he may as well go out of the business altogether.
And the new reading of the law requires that he shall not
only know, by looking at the outside of the package, or
bag, or meat-tin, that the contents are not bad, but that
he must be a prophet and able to say whether they will
or will not go bad during the voyage. For it often hap-
pens that the meat-tins or other things are far down m
the hold, and they can’t be brought up on deck every day
and officially smelt to find out how they are getting on.
When once the vessel starts they have to take their chance
till it is discharged at its destination. The law also looks as
if it was loaded as regards other people. If the s.s. owner
who is only acting as carrier (it may be quite uncon-
sciously) for another man’s bad meat has that unhealthy
commodity legally “ in his possession,” then any carter,
butcher-boy, wharf proprietor, owner of a public store or
market, railway commissioner, or other person who un-
consciously stores or carries anybody’s decayed provender
is also fineable. So is the person who unknowingly pur-
chases a bad egg ; he has in his possession vittles “ unfit
for human food,” until he finds out the badness of the egg
and indignantly throws it away. And why should the
law object to the carriage of decayed food by sea or
otherwise? So long as the food is inspected and destroyed
at the landing port there is a certain joy in the know-
ledge that the owner of it has had to pay freight. The
carriage of bad food is good for trade, and helps the ship-
owner to earn his crust, and encourages the noble British
seamen. Also in this case N.S.W.— the province which
received the decayed material—thereby obtained some
excellent manure gratis, and the stuff helped to increase
the size and consequently the influence of the province.
Rigid inspection is wanted, right enough, but the griev-
ance against the shipowner seems unintelligible.
* * • *
Amid all the talk of the N.S.W. taxation which
is to be “levelled up,” it may be worth while to
draw attention to one item which badly wants
levelling down. You can post a Sydney paper
from Sydney to Milparinka, or Bourke, or Armi-
dale, or Broken Hill, for nothing. You can post a
Sydney paper from Sydney to the furthest end of Aus-
tralia or Maoriland for hi. And if you post a Sydney
paper in George-street, Sydney, to an address in Pitt-
street, Sydney, the postage is id. No other province—
not even backward Westralia or Tasmania—ever struck
the wonderful idea of charging twice as much for carrying
a paper to the next street as for carrying it to the other
end of the continent.
* * * *
The Bank of New South Wales publishes a
prosperous-looking balance-sheet for the half-year to 31st
March, 1899. This time its curious habit, so manifest in
recent years, of earning just about enough to pay a 9 per
cent, dividend and enough over to lend a semblance of
variety to things, though still apparent, is less appar-
ent than usual. The half-year’s profits are £88,738, and
the div. at 9 per cent, per annum absorbs £87,750. Some
previous returns compare as follows :
9 per cent.
Profits. Dividend. Balance.
September, 1894 .. £78,614 .. £78,497 .. £ll7
March, 1895 . 81,714 .. 81,621 .. 93
September, 1895 .. 84,737 .. 84,536 .. 201
March, 1896 .. 86,460 .. 86.350 .. 80
September, 1896 .. 87,661 .. 87,458 .. 203
March, 1897 .. 87,898 .. 87,727 .. 171
September, 1897 .. 89,462 .. 87,750 .. 1712
March, 1898 .. 88,128 .. 87,750 .. 378
September, 1898 .. 88,042 .. 87,750 .. 292
March, 1899 .. 88,738 .. 87,750 .. 988
Quite a number of banks, nowadays, are quietly piling up
inner reserves to provide for depreciation of securities
whose gilt-edges have been somewhat frayed by rubbing
against six rough years in succession, and it looks as if
the B.N.S.W. was still doing this, and the profits
which are published were only intended to cover the divi-
dend. In that case, there are still better things to be
looked for when the process is completed.
Compared with the figures of a year ago the
B.N.S.W. shows remarkably little variation. Deposits
have decreased about £40,000 (the Sydney Telegraph,
being probably demoralised by continually building federal
arguments wherein 2) miles, added to £3 Is. 7d., make
three tons and a great increase in postage rates, anti a
dog, says-there is an improvement of about £900,000, but
that is a matter of no importance). Note circulation
shows an increase of £67,000. In most other respects
also the business exhibits an advance, and there is a
splendid cash reserve of £4,731,684, while cash and liquid
securities added together make £6,686,000 —about 26 per
cent, on all liabilities to the public. The B.N.S.W. still
holds its place as about the strongest financial rock in
Australasia, and appears like continuing to do so in-
definitely.

At the recent Bank of Adelaide shareholders
meeting, David Nock, who seconded the report, froze the
little gathering by flying off at a tangent to barrack for
the S.A. State Bank ; and the silence was more awful still
when he proceeded to say that its principal goodwork.was
the reduction of interest! And Mr. Nock isn t a John
Norton, either. On the contrary, he is a white-whiskered,
suave, benevolent old Wesleyan local preacher, who sat
for a brief while in the local Assembly many years ago,
and while there passed “Nock’s Act” for the str.cter
regulation of Sunday pub.-trading.

Quite a number of people felt their dignity con-
siderably bruised because Governor Beauchamp, at the
A.M.P.’s jubilee howl, mixed that society up in his speech
with English benefit clubs and friendly societies, as if it
was a sick and burial institution which met weekly at the
“Spotted Dog” public-house. And yet the Governor
was quite right in the main. The A.M.P. is a huge
workmen’s benefit society. The -average amount
of each policy in it is about £3OO, and when
allowance is made for the big policies held by
the limited wealthy class it is obvious that- the great mass
of members must be the small men with £lOO or £l5O of
insurance. The man who expects to be run over by a
truck some day, and wants to leave a little behind him
to keep the old woman from depending wholly on
washing, is the backbone of Australia’s stupendous
institution. Lots of ignorant persons write about
the A.M.P. as a tremendously bloated affair with
£15,000,000 of cash, which ought to be heavily taxed
because of its bloatedness. It is really a combination of
142,000 people, mostly with families dependent on them,
and mostly poor people with an average interest of 1108
in the funds. And it is to give absolute safety to all these
poor people that The Bulletin advocates rigid State audit
in addition to all the existing precautions. Also, it is
because of them that the justice of levying land-tax on
mutual life offices is questionable. The law says that a
man with £240 worth of land is exempt- There are thou-
sands of persons who own no land except their interest —
about £5O worth—in the A.M.P.’s property and mort-
gages. Yet they are not
An agitation against “ unnecessary Sunday
work” is being pursued simultaneously on the Westralian
and Broken Hill fields. At the latter the appeal is
mostly to the managers ; and, in fact, apart from the B.H.
Proprietary—the big mine—there is lit fie to complain about
for every concentrating-mill but the Proprietary’s is
closed on Sundays. In the West, where there isn’t any
miners’ association to carry on negotiations with the
managers, the churches have taken the organising in
hand. One typical mining-field parson, Tom Allan, has
done most of the work so far, and done it effectively.
Allan, the son of a S.A. farmer, spent some years at the
hardest sorts of manual labor, and, without being aggres-
sive, still sticks to semi-Socialistic politics.
* • * •
From a loss of £3552 for the half-year ended
June 30 last the British B.H. Co. jumped to a profit of
£7792 for the half ended Dec. 31. Better and cheaper
concentrating work, slightly better metal prices, and
greater extraction of carbonate ore were responsible.
The directors are, however, commendably frank. “ The
only hope of a really great future for the co. lies in the
discovery of a process which will utilise the enormous in-
trinsic values of the residues of the British ores.” The
Cowper-Coles zinc process, which the co. has been
financing for two or three years, has not panned out suc-
eessfullv: like the Ashcroft and other processes which
appeared so satisfactory in English laboratories, “anoma-
lies have been discovered when tested on a commercial
scale.” And “no other process has come under the ob-
servation of the directors which appears to answer their
requirements.”
Baker’s Creek Consols, N.S.W. Veins small
but mine looking fairly well ; 15 heads running, and pro-
posed to erect 15 more. Hill, formerly of Baker’s Creek,
boss. Working continuation of the B.C. veins. The

Queensland ’9B sugar-yield (163,000 tons) was
the largest ever recorded. Queensland has a surplus of
139,000 tons (over its own consumption) for export. The
total import of Australia and Maoriland for last year was
164,000 tons, only 25,000 tons above the Queensland sur-
plus. Every year sees more white labor employed in the
Q. industry, and every year sees a bigger output. Moral ?

Ravenswood is to have another chance. A. L.
Wilson has returned from London, having sold the
General Grant and Black Jack leases and formed a strong
syndicate to work the Donnybrook property. A fully-
equipped mill and a cyanide plant will be provided and
the block shaft will be sunk to 700 ft. vertical.

There has been a small gold-rush to the Reid
River, between the Towers and Townsville. Two men
who had a prospecting area in the district the other day
unearthed a 48oz. alluvial deposit, and a good many
miners are now on the scene.
• • • •
There are loud and long howls on the Murchison
concerning the way in which, by means of high-official
favor, certain English companies are being allowed to
dodge the labor-conditions. The Lady Forrest lias been
under exemption since Jan., 1898—If) months, and the
Gem of the Murchison since May 18, 1897—a shade under
two years ! The favor shown in exemption matters is
locally denounced as monstrous. The Murchison Advo-
cate remarks : “There is a London-owned mine within
three miles of Cue, with 20 head of stamps upon it,
which has managed to secure exemption from the labor
conditions for 25 months continuously. Whether the
fact that Darlington Simpson looms large in the forefront
of that co. has anything to do with the Warden’s leniency
is left to the public to decide. Many things are possible
for a man who utilises the Queen’s representative for
advertising purposes and drags him round the country on
the end ot a chain like a showman with a performing
bear.”
• * * •
From Leonora, Westralia : —A boom predicted
here —but thought to be a year off. The town is two
miles from Sons of Gwalia, employing 160 men ; further
away are the Star of Gwalia, and the children, aunts, and
wives of Gwalia. Latter all in embryotic stage, ground just
held, not worked. Result of distance of the township—
-16 sly-grog-shops on leases and other adjuncts of civilisa-
tion! traffic openly carried on ; profits of best houses from
£3O to £4O weekly. Accessories and essentials, a moder-
ately good-looking woman and a male paramour, who
keeps discreetly dark till the whisky works. Mines pay
monthly, men get month’s pay and ciear out, leaving stores
sorrowing. Prices of food high, honest customers paying
for dishonest. Except for situation, which is one of the
best in W.A., and cheap, good water, Leonora has no
attractions. District has had sin. rain since Ist Feb.
Three wells in town, with windmills, giving good sup-
plies. Public baths, three goes daily for 2s. 6d. a week.
Sanitary state awful, flies indescribable. All stores and
hotels, with two exceptions, of mud brick. Fairly cool in
day, if ventilation right, but furnaces at night. Six Jap
brothels, each with two women and two mean whites, do
good trade ;no expenditure on plant. Japs run by syndi-
cate-managing director cycles round, orders goods, and
directs distribution to each house. One citizen rents his
front premises to Japs, and with his wife lives in bush
shed at rear. So much for morality. Mines—
O’Brien and party, a mile from town, have a
big show in the Great Boston, sampling 4oz. They
talk of its being the Sons of Gwalia lode, but that is
three miles away', and all efforts to pick up lode in
intervening country have been abject failures. Paddy
Hannan is out here, too, but has nothing so far ; sorry to
say he has done nothing since his great find—Hannan’s.
He spent two years at Menzies, then struck out west to
Mulline, north to Mt. Ida, and east to Pendinnie, without
luck anywhere. O’Brien has another show at Savannah,
where he expected 3oz. ; Govt, battery gave but 18dwt.
He says balance is in tailings, which battery boss
denies. Further out are Gold Blocks, a mine well-
proved ; battery bought out of profits. Same re
Forrest leases owned by N.S. W. boys. The Trump,
close by, has a small battery paid for out of first
sensational return lOoz. ; but visitors are not asked
to go down—shoot probably lost. North end of field held
by working parties. Bull has the south end altogether.
Govt. 10-head battery would help, but for some foolery,
over water-supply. The mismanagement of these bat-
teries is disgusting prospectors, who are being starved
out in places. Big find made 22 miles out, by Fred
Merton, who got a reward-claim. Found loth March,
and within one month crushed 15 tons for profit of £lBOO.
Said to be £20,000 in sight. Had to borrow the cash to
take up ground, paying 100 per cent, for the accommoda-
tion. Line all pegged. Sons of Gwalia is now managed
by Bewick Moreing. Said to be plenty of soz. stuff in
lowest level, but policy is to keep average at about an oz.
Stuff so soft that 10-head mill runs through 2000 tons a
month ; another 30-head coming. No timber at all
in the country. Forrest, who was up recently, says he is
going to build a railway to the field straight away, so that
the mines may get jarrah and Collie coal. Looks all
right, except for Menzies, which will lapse back into an-
other Southern Cross.
Here is another version of the Merton find, •
which, in this shape, recalls the beautiful lies of early
W.A. boom-time : “ Not so many weeks ago a prospector
named Merton, who was down on his luck, struck put
from Mt. Malcolm and fossicked round about a big out-
crop of quarlz 20 miles from the township. Scores had
been there before him, but their prospecting must have
been of the earl.v-Coolgardie kind because he at once dis-
covered what they failed to find. Merton, who knew, a
good thing when he saw it, got out a crushing of 50 tons
which went lOoz. to the ton, and as there is about half-n-
-million tons of the sa me sort in sight he reckons he is in
for the biggest thing in W.A. At any rate be is said to
have refused £300,000 for his show.” Let's see : lialf-a-
million tons of ten-ounce tack—say, £20,000,000 gross.
Sensible man to refuse a paltry 1300,000.

A promising show (says a Bulletin correspon-
dent) is Boyd’s—not Carr-Boyd, the explorer, but Jim
Boyd, a hardy old battler who tramped up from the coast
in the early days. He has two mates, one of whom (Tom
Matchett) is at present visiting Victoria. After dollying
several hundred ounces, the first crushing of 85 tons
averaged 240 z., and another parcel of same tonnage is-
about to be put through. There are several other good
shows in same locality, about midway between Cue and-
Nan nine.

Plunderderry shareholders in London chop
down their capital to £70,000, forming a new co. for the
purpose ; 140,000 ten-shilling shares, with a half-crown
liability. And to shareholders who pay up on 100 shares
or more some of the original promoters are going to pre-
sent a bonus in the shape of free shares in the Great Cat
Consolidated and other front-rank properties.
* * * *
Lake View Consols, W.A. : “ The yield for last
month, including contents of parcels shipped for treat-
ment, was 14,0000z.’ A great mine, undoubtedly. The
yield this month, if writer is correctly informed, will be
the record. Met a man in Melb. last week passing through
en route to Dapto smelting works in charge of 100-ton
consignment of telluride ore from this mine which had
been carefully sampled, proving its value 670 z. per ton—
-67000z. gold from 100 tons ! ! It will be followed shortly
(though probably will not come within this month’s re-
turn) bv 300 to 6 1 tons of ore worth between 20oz. and 30oz.
to the ton, also going to Dapto works. This wonderful
bonanza was struck in the 300 ft. level at the latter part of
last year, and after its richness and magnitude were fairly
established ft was barricaded, and manager Callahan left
for Port Said, or somewhere, to consult the managing
director. To this phenomenal development the great
rise in shares during the last two months is attributed.
Good business for Dapto works to secure such a parcel-
only another evidence that such well-equipped works
would command the bulk of the trade of Australia if they
occupied a better site.

There appears to be a great demand at present
in Victoria for all kinds of bolts and nuts. Mr. Mephan
Ferguson, engineer, &c., of Melbourne, is working night
and day in this department in order to execute the orders
on hand. The boiler-makers’ shop is also very busy just
now supplying orders for Western Australia, and alto-
gether the general foundry business in Melbourne seems
to be improving.*

On the Kayser-Bischoff subject writes “
“ F.J.E.” (29/4/’99), like many others, praises Kayser’s
method of economising water : “ were it not for the man-
ner in which the machinery is built,” etc. It neverseems
to strike anyone that if the machinery had been built in
446,814 adjacent places the water could be used over and
over again just as economically; also, when Kayser
throws it away, as it were, half-a-dozen other parties pick it
up alternately and knock a good thing out of the waste
tailings and water. Again, B. is a wet place ; rain usually
falls slowly there and continues for long periods, but some
day a whole month’s rain—of usual kind—may fall in 24
hours. Then a huge flood will come along and lots of
people will suddenly find there isn’t room for the
machinery’ and the flood in the- same gorge. Then the
shareholders will inevitably damn “ Old Kayser,” and
point out for the first time that any'praotical miner could
have worked the mine ; in fact, that there is no mining at
Bisehoff; it’s simply quarrying. Kayser gets a lot of
credit fordoing what he couldn’t help doing ; there was
no alternative. The old man was not a bad “ boss,” but
for interfering with his men when away from the mine.

port has ever been made on the Briseis or on the Derby
tin deposits. I am at a loss to know how to help you in
this matter, although I have visited the mine more than
once. The deposit is certainly enormous, but so is the
hole that has been made in it, and from this only £lOOO or
£l6OO has been distributed amongst shareholders. I un-
derstand that hitherto only one shift has been worked,
but now they are arranging to work three shifts; this,
and the high price of tin, will give some divs. The
trouble, I am afraid, will be getting rid of the tailings.
Remember the sketch I sent you ; latterly the river h u
silted up at an alarming rate—what will it do when thee;
are running three shifts? 1 am told they cannot raise
their tail-race and yet have sufficient fail, but cannof
vouch for this. So far, the Briseis has spent £102,000 and
recovered £48,000 worth of tin ; only div., I find, £l5OO.

• * • *
Another slump has occurred in the hope of
Hobart, the Great Western Gridiron Railway float having
been allegedly interfered with by Premier Braddon. It
appears that the Premier lately cabled London, warning
the syndicate that it had forfeited all its concessions as far
back as last Nov. through non-compliance with the Act.
It is alleged that Braddon’s warning arrived just when
Bull was preparing to take the bait, and spoilt the after-
noon’s fishing. Though prominent railway leaguers and
syndicate agents in Ilobart characterise the Premier’s
action as “ unfriendly,” and the cable as “ brutal,” an
addendum to the message promised Govt, support in Parlia-
ment to a Bill reinstating the syndicate in its old position.
The Premier therefore seems to have only done his duty
in warning the British investor of the exact state of affairs.
But Hobart gums are gnashing in fine anti-Braddon
frenzy.

Briseis Tin, Tas. : —Resolutions confirming the
option for sale of mine confirmed. One man wanted to
know why they did not change the management, as it was
time something was done. With a splendid price ruling
for tin they had only got 27 tons out for the year. Chair-
man replied that. the returns would be much better this
month. It was not the manager's fault that they had
been so poor. What nice things to have said about a
mine while under offer in London? Writer can almost
see little Langford squirming.
* * * *
The South Tharsis Copper Co., Mt. Lyell,
have lately entered into a contract with the Austral Otis
Engineering Co., South Melbourne, to supply a complete
crushing and concentrating plant for treatment of their
ores, which are mostly low grade. The plant is to be a
very complete one and comprises compound steam-engine
and. boiler of 100 horse-power, 3 rock-breakers, 2 sets Roger
rolls, 8 4-compartment eccentric jiggs, 4 improved buddies
and Otis circular vanner, with the necessary trommels,
&c., for sizing and classifying the ore. The plant
has a capacity of 100 tons per day, with provision
for 200 tons. The Otis Co. are also supplying a self-acting
tramway from the mine to the mill; also, a pumping plant
to supply the mill with water from the dam.
F * * * *
Mt. Lyell half-yearly report better all round
than last—fair improvement in mine and a gigantic ditto
in copper-market. Developments from No. 5 tunnel very
fine, also in winze going down to determine extent of ore
underfoot —average value of ore in winze, 9’oB percent,
copper. 15oz. silver, and 2dwt. gold. Intended to cease
making in monthly’ reports any distinction between ore
from open cuts and that from other parts of the mine.
No. 2 smelting plant practically completed, bringing
the furnace capacity up to 1000 tons per day, and making
the general equipment one of the finest in the world.
Converters turned out in the half-year 2858 tons blister,
bringing total copper production up to 11,209 tons.
Profit for six months £111,845, from which has to be de-
ducted div.-tax, depreciation, &c. Two divs. paid—
£6l,496. Liquid assets amount to £132,351. made up
of bank-balances, blister copper in hand and transit,
stores, &c. A slight reduction in cost of production,
though now everything is calculated on dry-weight tons,
instead of wet as before.

Gippsland Boulder, Yic. Letter from the boss :
“Recent developments in the mine indicate an early
stoppage of the battery. The stone in the winze is con-
tracting fast, and appears to be getting cut off by a floor ;
it is also much poorer than it was immediately under the
50ft. level. The stone in the stopes will not take more
tha!n three weeks to beat out, so, unless something fresh
is struck within that time, things will be more than
doubtful. I think it would be well to detain any supplies
not already on the road.” Shareholders and directors
were dumbfounded, and the market became paralysed.
“’-The Melb. management is utterly unable to account for
the tone of the manager’s letter.” Within a fortnight
two directors were on the mine, and reported Bft. of stone
.jn the winze. But why all this amazement ?—for such
things have happened before. Probably’ all on account of
the blunt, shocking way in which the truth has been re-
vealed. Had the stone altered from Sin. to Bft., the
change would have been regarded with gratifying com-
posure. If the manager’s statehnent is correct, his frank-
ness is admirable, and an example which others might
follow.

One of the most pathetic of Melbourne
spectacles is to see Mayor M’Bacharn “keeping
up Ins position ” with his robes and chain. Where-
ever he has to appear in his civic capacity he turns
up enveloped in an aggravated ’possum-rug and
with a ponderous mass of gold hanging on his
shoulders. Throughout days of 110 in the shade
Mae sits m “’possum” and chain through the
business of the City Court, a small apartment
reekingwith the odors of vagrancy ; and at every
town Hall function that ’possum-rug is an in-
separable appanage of His Worship. Meanwhile
the proletarian on the footpath hustles for a crust
and thanks Heaven he is not a millionaire with an
ambition to cut a dash in the world.

Recently there was in Sydney a news-
paper letter-writers’ wrangle over tin question
whether the male companion of the man who
raises his hat to a lady friend in the street ought
to ignore the transaction or raise his hat likewise.
The fashion just now is as it was before it came to
be different. Once upon a time the ambition of
every Johnnie was to chip into his pal’s salutation
with enthusiasm. If Gussie acknowledged the
passing presence of anybody, male or female,
Johnnie followed suit. But a few years back it
occurred to the ruling powers in etiquette that a
stranger had no right to bow to the ladv friend of
the man he happened to be walking beside. He
might be a feller whom the lady wouldn’t care to
know, and whom his companion wouldn’t think
of making known to the fair by-passer. For a
time, therefore, it was ordained that no right-
thinking Johnnie should obtrude his lifted cadey,
so to speak, upon the notice of the girl who
smiled casually at Gussie. This arrangement
didn’t suit the other side. .A girl can’t have too
many hats off in her honour. The social standing
of the hat that she hasn’t been introduced to is
not a matter of importance as long as it behaves
properly, she reckons. The old rule was presently
put hack upon the slate, and nowadays dukes who
prowl in couples are expected to take the cue from
one another as regards bowing in a public place.
Even if one of them begs pardon for receiving the
point of a lady’s umbrella in his eye, the other
man must courteously raise his hat.
• * * •
N.S. W. Marine Board censured the second
mate of the s.s. Tomki, which grounded while he
was in charge of the deck, as follows :
The stranding of the Tomki was caused (first) owing to
the default of the second mate, in neglecting his duties as
regards the navigation of the ship, by leaving the bridge
to wash the decks.
Now, was it or was it not a regular part of the
mate s usual duty as understood on board to look
after the deck-washing ? If it was, ought not the
censure to be directed against those who required
the man to be in two places, and do two things at
the same time ?
• * » *
The way in which N.S.W. Marine Board
has handled the Republic case ought to be another
nail in the official coffin of the president. A
strong man would have seized the central point of
the matter in 15 seconds, and thus saved days of
weary, hopeless wrangling. And the central
point in question should be apparent to any
infant out of long-clothes.

the purpose of escaping payment of the mainte-
nance-money.

A deputation of Hobart’s aged unemployed
periodically waited upon the late Minister Pii'lin-
ger to pray for work. (At Hobart, mind you,
they do seek work, even at eighty years; you
might live at Hobart for years and never be asked
for charity.) The Director of Public Works
always expressed the deepest sorrow for the woes
of the old chaps, but invariably suggested that
they should become inmates of the Government
Charitable Institution. As, under the lovely Tas.
woikhouse regulations, this involved the separa-
tion of each old Jack from every old Jill, the old
Jacks always, jointly and severally, sturdily
refused. Whereupon the Minister would heave a
puzzled sigh at the old men’s mania for pick-and-
s hovel work and ask them to call again next
morning, when a few days’work would be found
-for them at 3s. per day/ The same farce was
repeated month after month for nearly the whole
term of this Minister’s office.

* • * *
The boss Rechabite whose evanishment
some 18 months ago badly shook the Cold-Tea
circles of S.A., turned up recently in Sydney.
Over 15 months ago a warrant was issued for his
arrest on a charge of illegal famine among the
cash, and the law proceeded after him by delayed
milk-train. It proceeded so slowly that many
people fancied it wanted the lamentable Rechabite
to keep ahead of it, and that there were decayed
circumstances about the case which would smell
badly if overhauled. The absconder long con-
tinued to be seen with impunity at one town and
another, but at last he dropped out of remem-
brance—till the other day an Adelaide ’tec ran
against him in Sydney, and collected him. Pro-
bably the Rechabites are sorry. The Cold-Tea
brethren are so fond of ascribing all crime to the
accursed Der-rink that when a teetotaller goes
away with the cash they would almost rather lose
the cash than capture the teetotaller.

In grim commentary upon Sydney Presby-
terian parson Auld’s complaint of the dearth of
ministers in N.S.W., it is pointed out that,
according to. the Church’s official returns, more
than half of its ministers in N.S.W. are receiving
a smaller stipend than what the General Assembly
considers the least a minister can adequately
support himself and family upon. And yet Mr.
Auld would cable ’Ome for more candidates for
semi-starvation. There’s nae fule like an Auld
fule! The Bulletin predicts that in the long
run the local Presbyterian clergy will have to
compete, not merely with cleap clerical labor
from Scotland, but also from China—the mission-
ary efforts of the denomination being apparently
centred in the effort to Calvinise the yellow man
«** v *
“ Melb.”: A recent Pleasant Sunday After-
noon at Pastor Edgar’s Wesley Church fairly
staggered me, and I hear it is the regular thing.
No wonder the place was crowded. The pastor
has two monster bands—the brazen, of 20 per-
formers, and the string band of 24, including three
ladies. The latter makes Wesley Church resound
on Sunday afternoons, not by'any means with
sacred music, or with just a whiff thrown in for
decency, while the main staple is of operatic
overtures and selections, and dance music. To be
precise, they thrummed, rasped, and rootle-tootled
in my hearing that Sunday a Minuet, or Gavotte,
and a Waltz, which played the crowd out, just as
the theatre orchestras do. Fora side-show to this
two parsons, Sugden and Champness, a nut-
cracker-visaged celebrity from Britain, tried to be
funny. How Church is altered, since I was
dragged there as a boy ! Poor old Dogma 1

On the Palmer and the Coen,
where Chinese fossiekers swarm'.'
leprosy is common enough among
th e whites— presurnahly a Iso among
the Chinese, but more difficult of
discovery from the secrecy ob-
served. Manner of contagion
the black-gin. Travel-
lers with some knowledge of the
disease state this to be the most
certain, as it is the most deadly,
source of contagion. Estimated
that it takes seven years for out-
ward signs to come, but this is
uncertain. Leprosy is common
among Papuan natives, and
leprous blacks are frequent ori
canoes that come off to trade with
visiting vessels.
Most of Queensland's lepers arp
brought south to Dunwich by the
White Star, a little smooth-water
steamer, skippered by a grizzled,
matter-of-fact Scotchman. The
lepers are carried in a packing-
case fixture, secured as a tem-
porary deck-house. This is lined
with tin, having a small, sliding
window, through which food is
received in a tin dish. Water is
supplied through a funnel which
communicates with a bucket in-
side. The poor wretches thus
cooped up have neither speech
nor sight of anyone from the time
they go on hoard till landed at
Dunwich. These packing-cases
are always destroyed at the fini-h
of a trip and the vessel fumi-
gated, a new house being built
for the next lot—cost .£3O a-piece.
Recently a suspect brought down
was declared free from disease on
the usual examination by three
doctors, and landed quietly in
Brisbane with ,£lO in his. pocket
as compensation.

The life of a bobby on the
West Coast, Tas. is not a happy
one. The weather is dreadful,
tucker is dear, the district is widely scattered and
terribly rough, and the pay is ss. 3d. per day.

The recent Sydney P.0.-order forgeries
were, it seems, discovered through the "forger
losing heart at the last moment, and promising
another person ,£5O to collect the money. This
person took the information straight to the G.P.O.
authorities, but at the police court he was not
produced, as the detectives wanted all the credit
of the discovery.

THE AN GLER
BY J.J.W
E was fishing near the ’sylum, where the lunatics reside,
And patience was a virtue he possessed without a doubt,
For he d waited there for hours, till the rising of the tide,
Without a tug to mention at the line which reached far
out.
And then it started raining, and the wind to blow began;
‘'till the angler waited calmly for the bite he couldn't get.
'Fhen the ’sylum window opened, and a looney-looking
man
Asked politely of the angler, ‘‘Have you got a bite sir
yet?”
“ Not V et ” thc angler answered. “ And how long have
you been there ? ”
Inquired the man at window, and the fisherman replied;
11 Oh, just about three hours; rve got lots of time to
spare. ”
And the looney promptly answered: “ Then you'd better
come inside ! ”

THE GOLDEN GIRL.
[For tub Bulletin.]
The Golden Girl ! Sweet millionaire—
Her eyes were blue, her face was fair,
Her teeth that gleamed, a smiling' row,
Were mated by the pearls below
That bound her throat, a peerless pair.
I heard the golden trumpets blare
Her golden praise in gracious air,
Till all my bosom swelled aglow—
The Golden Girl 1
And as I gazed I dreamt that there,

knows how much more will be wrung out of the bleeding
country to keepJonbert and his hungry crew in billets.

stvles himself, sits at the entrance to the show collecting

week. The whole affair is a ghastly failure —not half the
space is occupied, and the few exhibitors, or their agents,
who still hang on are subjected to all kinds of petty annoy-
ances. For instance, a fee of £2 2s. is charged exhibitors
for their season tickets, while attendants have also to be
paid for—an imposition unknown at any previous show*
Then there is an espionage over all the books with the
view of charging a commission on sales effected, as if
the exorbitant sum exacted for space was not more than
sufficient.

WILKINS—BRIDGEMAN.—On the 26th April, in the
Chapel at the residence of his Grace the Arch-
bishop, at Dana, by the Rev. John O'Reilly, ad-
ministrator of the diocese, P. Banks, fourth son of
the late T. Wilkins, Esq., Cumberland, England,
to Teresa Mary (Tessie), sixth daughter of the late
Henrv St. John Bridgeman, sen., formerly of the
National Bank of Ireland, and of the Queensland
Bank, Limited, Brisbane, third cousin to Orlando
Bridgeman (Earl of Bradford, England), second
cousin to Sir Bryan O'Loghlen, Victoria, and third
cousin to Dan. O’Connell, the Irish Liberator.
It is fortunate the Jews don’t go in for this sort of
thing when Isaac, junior, and Leah decide to
become one. For the Hebrew pedigrees go back
to Chaos, or thereabouts, and the Jew wiio wanted
to ring all his great relatives into the advt. would
finish up ten columns of pedigree with the state-
ment that he was Abraham’s billionth half-cousin
multiplied by 12, and related to all the kings of
Judah and to Ham’s uncle.
• * » *
At the recent Federation meeting in
Sydney Town Hall the rowdy element introduced
a new species of terror to public assemblies. The
hall was packed, and the gang of toughs raised
over their heads chairs held by one leg. The
nearest man was obliged also to lay-hold, other-
wise the chair would be allowed to fall upon him.
In this way chairs were passed along overhead,
and orderly people compelled to contribute to the
disorder. Some dozen chairs were thus kept in
circulation overhead to the great discomfort of
the decent portion of the audience.
* * * *
N.S.W. Railway Commissioner Oliver, to
a deputation the other day, expressed regret that
so far no scheme had been devised to prevent the
“jumping ” of railway seats during the temporary
absence of their occupants. The Bulletin invites
suggestions on this tough subject.
* * * *
Another victim to lead-poisoning at Port
Pirie smelters was T. W. Anderson, who, after
being missing for nearly a week, was found
drowned in the creek near the B.H. co.’s pumping
station. He was engaged on the “sulphide
crusher,” the same work which incapacitated
councillor Connelly, whose case was here lately
mentioned.

* * * *
NSW Postal Department has lately
adopted a new method of closing and sealing mail-
bags. In course of business a bag thus secured
arrived at the bush village of Appletree Hollow,
where the postmistress, after vainly trying to
break the new seal, asked the Irish mailman
the despatching postmaster had not given him a
letter for her, containing instructions how to open
the bag. Mike, after vigorous head-scratching,
gravely answered, “No, mum; didn t he put it
insoide the bag ? ”
* * *
The olcl sovereigns issued by Sydney Mint
had a yellow-greenish tinge, caused by the pre-
sence of silver. They contained the correct
quantity of gold and silver in addition, so that
their intrinsic value, when silver was ss. an
ounce, was appreciably higher than 20s. Ihe
pattern on the reverse was Australia sur-
mounted by a crown, within a laurel wrea,th,
the words “ Sydney Mint ” being under-
neath. At a sale in 1890 a “ proof ’’sove-
reign of this description, dated 1855, "as
sofd at auction for <£lo. A “ proof
half-sovereign to correspond fetched 9.
“ Proof ” coins are struck by mints for
preservation in collections, etc., special
pains being taken with the surface and
the workmanship. Another Sydney
sovereign, with ft plain (pro-
bably a freak), sold for .£26 10s. in
1889. The cult of the collector is
very mad, but there are people in
business now making a good thing
out of his fads, so probably they
don’t mind. By the way, it is
possible that the Perth Mint may
open soon, and the enterprising
speculator might do worse than
get a few of the first issue
(especially in “proof” state, if
they can be got) and hold for a
rise.

The harm that bad people do to
good people is nothing to the dreadful
wrong that good people do by re-
maining good, to the consequent
annoyance and disgust of the
evill y - d i sposed. Consequen t ly,
this wicked paper feels sorrier
for the bad Melbourne girl who
suicided recently through sud-
denly remembering that her sis-
ter was a good girl and sang
hymns on Sunday, than if the
cases had been reversed. It
seems right, somehow, to this
conscienceless rag that the
hairs of the righteous should
go down in sorrow to the
grave for the sins of the
departed, and a burning
shame that the virtues of
the virtuous should be per-
mitted to harass and an-
noy the world in general,
tili the depraved are only
too glad by pistol or poison
to betake themselves to
that better land where
the righteous cease
from troubling and
the wicked are at
rest.
N.S.W. country
newspapers slip one
after another into the
groove dug some time ago
by a well-meaning, but sap-
headed, philanthropic journal,
which proposed to help the local
hospital by suppressing the names of
defendants in minor police-court
cases for ss. per head. I 1 ully
a score of country papers
now notify terms to the
same effect. If they added
a clause that the name of
the defendant really not able to pay t le new levy
would also be suppressed, the philanthropy would
be saner; but as it is, if the oracle works, the
press will become a record of the offences of the
poorest class only.

FREEDOM AND NECESSITY.
Destiny is the road to (?), macadamised by Go d.
Free Will is each individual’s notion of the best
bee-line thither.
As each starts from the same point, so each
reaches the same goal.
M >st, however, being rather tired, take the
macadamised road.
Vic. Danton.
AND THE SCIENTIST SWORE.
THE TRAVELLING SCIENTIST (to wife): “I often
wonder what are those strange circles, as it
were, of ringworm which I see all over Aus-
tralia on the sward. I fancifully ask myself.
‘ Are these the fairy rings of Eld? Or, to be
practical, are they the sacred spots-of the mystic
rites of the Aborrygeens? What a world of
speculation is opened up by these remarkable,
as it were, Druidical remains .”
THE BOY: “G’long, y’old goanner! Them's the
circus-marks.”

“ H. 8.” to The Bulletin :
Two months ago at N.S.W. Central Criminal Court, a
youth of 16 received a sentence of two years' imprison-
ment,on a charge of indecent assault, to which he pleaded
guilty, upon a girl aged nearly 14. An application to the
presiding Judge to release the prisoner under the First
Offenders’ Act was refused, his Honor giving as a reason
why he could not see his way to grant the request, that
prisoner should, under the circumstances disclosed by the
depositions, have protected prosecutrix instead of de-
bauching her. Prisoner, who received this 2-year sen-
tence two months ago, is now at liberty, having been
released from gaol the other day. This strange narrative
would not be complete unless I stated that the offender
lives in a constituency represented by a high politician.
Evidently there is a way in N.S.W. of evading the legal
consequences of one’s misdeeds if you only know
how to go about it. As the boy was only 16 years old,
two months’ gaol, save under very special circumstances,
would probably meet his case as a first offender. But,
as you have frequently urged, all Executive sentence-
remissions should be gazetted, with the reasons therefor.
• i * H 1
They have a queer way of interpreting iij.
Sydney police courts. The other day when a
Maltese was to be dealt with, the magistrate on
the bench adjourned the case because the in-
terpreting was so bad, he feared to do an injustice
under the circumstances. The interpreter said in
excuse that he had only heard of the case that
morning at 9 o'clock and he had not had time to
find a person to interpret. This, following so
closely on the Afghan case at Bourke, where the
Govt, interpreter allowed the employer to put
him aside and himself translate the agreement,
shows that there is a screw loose somewhere.

One Ca.pt. De Hoghten— chief of a fire-
brigade or football-club,,or something or .other-
made a speech, the other day, in the Tas. Angli-
can Synod, against the abominations of the con-
fessional in tlie Protestant Church. It was re-
printed, and sold for a penny, but the local Angli-
can book-dep&t doesn’t sell it. The reason is that
rank Popery is written on all the stained-glass
windows of St. David’s Cathedral, next door, and
is stamped on the altar and o» the candlesticks,
and that a certain Hobart Dean clean-shaves and
hears auricular confessions.

“ D.G.” : However sorry one may feel for
that poor devil of a policeman who accidentally shot
his child, one cannot forget that the man who dis-
played such gross ignorance of firearms would be
allowed, in a police-court, to give evidence as an
expert therein.
* * * *
Among the Birthday baubles Adelaide
expects a knighthood for Chief Justice Way and
another for Agent-General Cockburn. At present
little Way has the distinction of being about the
'only plain Mister among British Chief Justices.
Cockburn is expected to show less backbone than
Playford when approached.
* * • •
Adelaide Club is the only institution of its
kind Which remains in existence in the pious
city. The Austral, affected by a coterie of gay
• Bohemians,- recently wound tip, and its premises
have been purchased by the W.C.T.U., a grim
illustration of the social' decline of poor Adelaide.
* * * *
Adelaide Hospital troubles, which led to
the abolition of the medical school, have driven
students in medicine to Melbourne. As a result,
parents are now having their boys trained for law,
and the University is crowded with law-students,
though Adelaide is already over run with land-
sharks.

Lady Mary Lygon, the Governor’s sister, helped
him noticeably at the start. When that nervous
pallor overspread his face, she sat back like a
woman of thirty, and talked calmly. She wore a
quiet light-fawn coat and skirt with a Govt
House rose m her button-hole, a little lacy hat
with turquoise blue up-standers, white gloves and
a string of pearls. Through her veil there seemed
to be a charming complexion. None of the other
ladies of the party appeared.
When Lord Beauchamp was seated in his
carriage there was a great cheering and just the
faintest suspicion of hooting. One burly gentle-
man, from whose arm hung a diminutive but
herce-looking female, put his hands to his lips
and was just getting up a healthy hoot when his
companion turned on him angrily: “Shut up, you
fool, don t let em know you've got a birth-stain ! ”
And he did shut up —with great suddenness. As
the coach was passing into Macquarie-place there
were repeated cries of “ Soup waw-wn ” —that was
tor the coachman’s legs again.
A f ew hitches were necessary. Otherwise it
might have been painful to a sensitive mind to see
Mayor I arris and the Town Clerk sitting oppo-
site one another in gloomy black clothes and a dark
grey silence: They looked out of different sides of
their carriage. They carefully kept their knees from
touching. And they almost forgot the welcoming
snnle that the Mayoral presence had the right to
shed first m order of precedence. His Worship
was also first in order of nervousness—after his
v' T n there was one supreme moment
when the Mayor and the Governor saw stars to-
gether, and nearly fell over. After that, their
legs composed themselves, and the tremor resolved
itself into uupleated smiles.
And the Society of Artists gavfe itself away with
a dismal shriek. Considering how Lord Hampden
admired and purchased this callow fraternity’s
pictures, it was a rather circumscribed compliment
that waved across Pitt-street announcing 11 The
Society of Artists Welcomes its First Artist
Governor.” It may be true. And may account
for the Australian collection of portraits of Myself
and sheep and gumtrees that so many back-number
Governors have conscientiously carried away to
their ancestral homes (or porters’ lodges). ;
Government House Gates were gorgeous with
evergreens, flowers and flags, and a temporary
border of red and white flowers, and over the gate
was the legend “Welcome” and an Imperial
crown of white immortelles and red kiss-me-quick
flowers. As Copeland, M.P., passed out in his
carriage a basket of flowers fell right into his hat
and hung there a moment like a crown. An en-
thusiastic Federalist shouted “Take it home
Copeland, it’s an omen of victory.” Mr. Cope-
land bowed gracefully to the speaker and drove
away. In Govt. House itself were no decorations
—not even in the room where the swearing-in took
place.
The swearing-in was highly picturesque. Over
each door was a glittering array of bayonets.
High up on the walls hung three portraits. Two
amber-colored lights shed refulgence over an array
of bald heads, military uniforms and black
shoulders. Gold lace shone dully in the shade.
At the end of the dimly-lit apartment was what
appeared to be a huge life-size painting, but was
really a living picture of a group of women, in the
foreground of which stood a smiling damsel with
a gorgeous bouquet of flowers and crimson sprays.
It was Lady Mary. Beside her was an elderly
smiling face on a scintillating column. It was
Lady parley in black, invisible all but her face and
the shining jet trimming. Behind these two was
a sea of dim faces that faded into darkness. From
down where the light shone came the droning
voice of George Reid intoning the “ swearing in ”
—as by letters patent. “God save the Queen”
etc., etc.
Altogether it was a great and cordial reception,
but consider what wild enthusiastic applause would
have torn the skies had it been a victorious Aus-
tralian Eleven returning, and driving round to the
boom of cannon and waving of flags behind coach-
men in red plush breeches ! We must have red
plush breeches on tap when Darling and co
return.

A second and less casual glance at the Beautiful
Boy, bonny New South Wales, suggested quite
new trains of thought. Do you know—sacrilegious
as it may seem—he calls up visions of Charles
By'ey- A beautified, sanctified, sestheticised,
doubly-refined and distilled and spiritualised and
ethereahsed vision, of course—but there’s the
resemblance, all the same. Under a good stage-
manager, I believe His Ex. would be an abnormal
triumph that way. The same intangible some-
thing (do you know what it is, by the way?) is in
that fifteenth-century carving, which centred be-
irresistible Charles’s mundane features,
and butcher-boy hair. No woman can resist either!
And looking on, critically, one can imagine Our
Other Beautiful standing now between two futures
at the parting of the ways, as it were. One road
tods along to a lean, respectable, sanctimonious
old age, composed chiefly of nut-crackers and the
family-pew. (It rather looks as if he had al-
together set his mind on going that way, for
he has started by attending church twice on
bunday—beginning at 8 a.m.) T’other passes
through a lot of beer and smoke, and the joys of
life and other mellowing substances. In the
words of the Sunday-school teacher—“ Which is
it going to be, my boy ? ” The question may be
settled by the time his youthful Ex. leaves Sydney.
The reason why every woman in the country
has succumbed is that the Earl is just like every-
body’s first love in the London Journal. So
very like that one can hardly believe
him flesh and blood and Earl at the same
time. There is the indispensable thinness,
the Air, the Lithe Hands, the Firm but-not-
obstinate Chm, the Chiselled Nose, the Marble
Brow, the Breeding recognisable through three-
barred doors and a glass-bottled wall. All, every-
thing, that goes to make an Earl an Earl.
To corroborate me I turn up this photo, of Lady
Juanita de Vere Clitheroe’s lover, and find it sup-
ports me on every point. “The door opened,
and the Earl entered bearing in his long white
exquisitely clean hands with fleckless nails, a top
hat which he placed on the table with a move-
ment full of wonderful patrician grace. His dark
curly hair was cut close to his small and noble
head. It only broke into bunches at the high
white brow. His long, strong nose was exquisitely
modelled; his lips had that delicate manly curve
that only goes with a true nobleman’s soul; his
magnificent, swan-like neck was partially hidden,
partially concealed (sic) by a turned down collar
of the most expensive velvet; his broad but slim
shoulders and slender waist modestly denied the
brilliant deeds of battle he had rendered in de-
fence of his beloved Queen's country. And his
smile thrilled Juanita beyond all words. It was
the saddest, sweetest, loveliest, most manly and
womanly smile on all God’s earth.” To all of
Which His Ex. can answer, “ Adsum ! ”

The chief thing against Lovell as Lady Jessica’s
lover is that habit he has of pushing his long, thin
nose right into her face. But he looks, in this
part, more like the handsome man that rumor said
he was, and if the dress-suit and black hair make
him look like a waiter, it is probably due to the
dress-suit and the black hair, as a fair-haired
waiter isn t allowed on any respectable stage.
Joseph Carne, m a beard and a bell-topper, loses
his good looks and stands round like a big boy in a
very tight coat. In their own ways, these two
actors are both awkward men. Lovell’s awkward-
ness runs to sharp, self-conscious movements, and
an inability to go out of the room without touch-
ing everything he passes. He lays his hands on
three chairs and a table before he can get to the
door. Carne is roundly and heavily awkward In
repose his arms are an awful trouble to him, and
he keeps his elbows half away from his ribs. But
stilJ, nobody minds awkwardness in a man In
tact, awkward” is still very largely used in
describing heroes.
Colonel Bell, who is known as a very correct
Amurrcan, agreeably surprised everyone by cast-
ing aside the ruffled consular dignity, and taking
his place in the Beauchamp procession like a man
lhe other consuls were so bitterly wounded at
being placed after the University that they all
stayed away from the reception. Which reminds
me that one of these sticklers is noted for the
eccentric way in which he keeps his engagements.
A well-known Society woman sent him a card
lately, inscribed “At Home. Friday , from half-
past eight. MusicF He arrived on Thursday at
half-past seven. The hostess wondered, but said
nothing. After a good deal of desultory talking
the guest remarked, vaguely, that Aus-
tralians had really very extraordinary ideas
about keepung their dinner-appointments. A
little later he observed more emphatically that
the bad manners of people who didn’t come to
time ought to be severely checked. At nine
o clock the hostess woke up to the situation, and
discovered that he thought he was asked to a
dinner-party Finally he was taken away, and
regaled on cold beef in the pantry, and the hostess
felt herself covered with confusion. And this
same gentleman makes himself unpleasant by
objecting to the etiquette that refuses to put
him near the head of the procession to welcome
the new Governor.

Fattorini, who is at present in England and is not
engager] to anyone.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
Guv’ment ’Ouse committed itself, the other
evening, to a Masonic ‘at home ’ of the deadliest
dull character. Several rows of seats in front of
the dais had the ‘ reserved ’ look which keeps the
common herd at a respectful distance, and imparts
an e^ r '7 °hill to their curiosity. On the arrival of
the -Noble Host and party, a procession filed past
to pump-handle the exalted pair, after which the
IN icer portion of the ‘ at home ’ sat down upon the
appalling plush pews, and the nobodies planted
themselves where they could. A churchy pro-
gramme was gone through by Maud Hawie, Armes
Jieaumont, S. Lamble, and the Clarke Lodge
choir, amid much yawning and subdued chatter.
I hen the refreshments were raided and upbraided,
and a few masons slunk away to quiet spots with
bottles, and the talu of sorrow may be said to end.
Sonic of the frocking dated back to the dark
years ago, and was almost an excuse for
the bad mannahs of a somebody's wife, who made
herself conspicuous by laughing and pointing a
huger of scorn at the arky figures. Lady B. was
hxed up in ham-pink satin, with cream lace
piasters and diamond top-piece. Mrs. Pakenham
scintillated in black, with silver sequins galore.
A girl in vivid pink satin, with an attempt at one
of the new sheath-shaped over-skirts, was an
example of how not to dress; and a granny in
black, with a white woollen shawl, ranked as a
good old type of independent comfort.

PEOPLE YOU KNOW.
No. I.— The Baker.
Mr. T. Streater writes: “I have received so
much benefit from Dr. Tibbies’ Vi- Cocoa I feel
that I must thank you for it. lam a baker by
trade, which, as you know, is very hard work, but
I am pleased to say that when I am done of a
night I go home and -have a cup of your Cocoa,
and feel quite fresh again, and then I go and
enjoy myself. But before I took to Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa, I used to mess about indoors as tired as
could be.”
Vi-Cocoa will become a household word amongst
us. Prepared from substances of admitted
dietetic value, Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa is at once
digestive and refreshing, nutritious and invigorat-
ing. This is the secret of its phenomenal success.
It seems, so far as we can gather, to agree with
everybody, and everybody is agreed in giving it a
good name. Apparently it is the accepted beverage
of a people who have recognised and appreciated
its remarkable health-giving properties. And
certainly in this age of rush and worry, wear and
tear, when the energies of mind and body are so
severely taxed, it is an ideal food.
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa can be obtained from all
Chemists, Grocers and Stores, or from Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa, Limited, 209 George-street, Sydney.
As an unparalleled test of merit, a dainty sample
tin of Dr. Tibbies’Vi-Cocoa will be sent free on
application to any address, if when writing (a
postcard will do) the reader will name The
Bulletin.*

Mr. Frank Senior, who has been chemist
to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years past,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-
ache. The new remedy cures instantaneously, is
very palatable, and perfectly harmless to both
ladies and gentlemen. Moreover, it is perfectly
effectual, no matter what the cause of the head-
ache may be. This cure, the result of years of
experience, can be had in 4 sizes, by post 1/4, 2/6,
3/8, 4/8, in N.S.W. only. To all the other colo-
nies 1/8, 2/8, 3/8,5/2. Beware of imitations. Only
address: Senior, chemist, 246 George-st., Sydney.*

is unquestionably the most generally-attractive
poetry-book yet issued in Australia. Price, 4s. 6d.,
post-free from Bulletin office.

The current English sensation relates to
the fact that a very fashionable white peeress has
presented her white husband with a coffee-colored
PrinteCi 'Miat Premier Reid “ didn’t go to
Albany merely to see the Governor, and arrange
for political possibilities ; he went because the doc-

Rumored that John Morley, as Gladstone s
biographer, will have to tell awkward truths.
There are already some ugly stories afloat about
the G.0.M., who certainly lived too long tor ms
own reputation.
Edmund Barton was once M.L.A. for
Wellington, the N.S.W. constituency now repre-
sented by John Haynes. Barton’s first seat was
for the University, but on the abolition of that
institute as a political body he looked afield ana
lighted on this outback territory.
Some of the precursors of Australian
Federation who, in the wilderness of local politics,
cried for a Commonwealth over 40 years ago :
W. C. Wentworth, in 1853 ; Deas Thomson, 1856 ;
Ga'van Duffy, John O’Shanassy, Archibald Michie,
Dr. Evans and Janies M'Culloch in 1857.
Premier Turner summed-up Gov. Beau-
champ as “a second Lord Hopetoun a very
unthinking remark. Hopetoun was a sickly
young man, with a thin, nasal accent, very little
“ mannah ” and a wife. Beauchamp appears to be
a healthy bachelor with a lot of “ mannah.”
Premier Reid’s three defeated Ministers
are now provided for. Gould is M.L.C. ;he is too
rich to want pay, but he wears a gold pass, a big
advantage in itself. Jake Garrard is chairman of
Sydney Water and Sewerage Board at .£7OO a
a year ; and Pumpkin Smith is made president of
the Unemployed Board.
The Queensland J.P. is a festive animal.
T’other day a sanguinary Rattle took place near
Brisbane between two rival political barrackers.
Capital went down in the rough-and-tumble, got
badly bashed, and had his ear bitten by pugnacious
Labor. Principal witness was a prominent J.P.,
who “regretted to see the fight, but did not see
sufficient cause to interfere.”
A CIRCULAR QUAY SKETCH.
Premier Kingston’s great endurance and
capacity for work astonish even his friends.
Despite poor health he worked like an elephant
right through the recent election campaign, and
has been since relieving his colleagues during
their holidays. Kingston says six years of
Ministerial life have left him so poor that he is
compelled to deny himself the vacation urgently
prescribed by his doctor.
Matthew Batbersby, ex-member for More-
ton (Q.), who died suddenly the other day, had a
long run in politics. For 11 years he was the most
wildly-ineoherent Malaprop in the seven Parlia-
ments, and when he dropped out last year most
of the joy departed from Q. Assembly ._ Battersby
wasn’t a premeditated humorist, but in a purely
accidental way he contributed much to the gaiety
of a rather tired community.
A certain illiterate person, recently elected,
will make a characteristic addition to a fossilized
Parliament. When asked by a miner if he
favored the abolition of the Upper House and the
substitution of the referendum he had to ask his
ohairman what the “substitution of the referen-
dum ” meant. When handed the menu-card once
at a city hotel he started to read it upside-down,
and ordered “ tinned meat! ”

Characteristic and true story about a
(junior) N.S.W. Supreme Court Judge. At re-
cent Assizes he was presented with a pair of
white gloves to commemorate the fact that he
would not be called upon to try any criminal
charges. To a sheriff’s officer he confided the fact
that he was already possessed of more white
gloves than he had any use for, and preferred a
request that they be exchanged for a pair of
“ tans.”
The just-published Art Annual contains
a list of the pictures of Broken - Hillionaire
McCulloch, now one of the chief art-patrons in
London. The list is interesting for the reason that
Mac’s Melbourne friends confidently say he means
to give his treasures to Australian galleries some
day. The pictures are just such as a millionaire
might be expected to buy—Leighton’s, Millais’,
etc. ; the sort of things about which a man can say
in an off-hand fashion, “ I gave £5OOO for that can-
vas, and £hooo for this, and I wouldn't sell them
for ,£20,000.
Mrs. Ann Houison, a very old colonist, died
at Parramatta the other day, aged 85. She came
to N.S.W. in 1832, and, two years later, married
Mr. Jas. Houison, an architest and builder whose
handiwork still remains in St. John’s Church, All
Saints Church, Subiaco Convent (the old home of
the Maoarthurs), and Parramatta gaol ; the old
courthouse was also built by him. Mrs. Houison
lived in Parramatta for 67 years. She leaves
eight children and 32 grandchildren, among the
former being Dr. Andrew Houison, of “ Old Syd-
ney ” renown.
Some of the Victorian judges are wholly
devoid of fighting inclinations, as is illustrated
by a story told of Justice Holroyd. It seems
he was asked to join with one or two other
judges in protesting against the proposed ap-
pointment of Dr. Madden to the post of
Chief Justice. He stroked his long white
beard with his old red bundle - handkerchief,
smiled on his importuner and said, “ Well, I don’t
think I’ll bother about it. The next best thing to
being appointed Chief Justice is to have had the
Chief Justice reading in your chambers.”
Bruce Smith is a keen student of Herbert
Spencer, and keeps that author’s works with his
law-books in his chambers. One day, while
Prenlier Reid was visiting Bruce Smith’s cham-
bers, the latter enquired—“Have you ever read
these?” “What are those?’’said Reid, adjust-
ing his eyeglass. “ Herbert Spencer,” replied
Bruce Smith. “No, never. I’ve never seen hi 3
reports before,” said the same old George. Now,
Bruce Smith tells the story as an illustration of
the dense ignorance of G. H. Reid, while Reid
tells it as a fine example of Bruce Smith’s lack of
humor.
The Argus, James Service’s own paper,
in printing 3 cols, of his life, forgot all about the
Brough Smyth incident, and how J.S. revolu-
tionised Vic. Mining Department in the
seventies, and delivered the serfs of the Collins-
street Czar from their Siberia. After years of
suffering, the mining men revolted against the
tyrannous Secretary. The Peter Lalor of the
place was little Hickey, a Liverpool-Irishman,
who caused much amusement by his acting when
he told his story before the Commission. He
illustrated things by attitudes, grimaces, and ven-
triloquism, and made his evidence a most inter-
esting entertainment. R. Brough Smyth, other-
wise, “Robert Smith,” as his real name was
shown to be, got the tip to stand from under, and
on the day that Service and the others submitted
their report R.B.S. tendered his resignation.
James Service’s father
used to work religion as a hobby. Every
Sunday preaohed on the Melb. wharves
to a miscellaneous crowd. Had the
Bible at his tongue’s end, and quoted
like an almanac. A big, broad-shoul-
dered man, with a hoarse voice and a
knock-me down manner of charting
the road to Paradise. He and another
apostle named Peter Virtue never
missed a Sunday while they lasted.
Dry or wet, they turned up on the
wharves and held forth, ohiefly on
Old Testament matters. Great on
the bald-headed prophets, and the
much-married patriarchs, and the
history of begettings—such as read
so curiously like the pedigrees of
blood horses in the sporting news-
papers. Harmless, but irritating,
they used to get into great disputes
with blear - eyed beer - eaters and
wharf-rats, who took heretical views
of things, and ran out their own
primitive notions, and blasphemed
pretty freely. These were the men
old-man Service loved to talk to and
smother with his Biblical lore. And,
that done, he would go home to his
dinner with an appetite.
The other day a smart man
blatantly related in the bar of a big
hotel that it was the custom of com-
mercial travellers, when they re-
turned home after a long absenoe, to
knock loudly at the front door and
then run quickly and quietly round to
the back portal. Whereupon a mus-
cular drummer who happened to be
there broke his jaw, and there is to be
law over the matter.
About those “wolves and thieves.’’
Lachlan Brient, editor of Sydney D.T.
and head sooler-on of the N.S.W. Gee-
bungs, is a Tasmanian, trained in jour-
nalism in Victoria. Watkin Wynne,
manager of the Teleghaph, is from Bal-
larat (not Bungaree) via, Melbourne.
Peters, the bright satirist, whose fancy
paints ornamental descriptions of meet-
ings and evolves mysterious cab-rides,
is a South Australian; and leader-
writer Farrell is an ex-Victorian.
Roberts, the able mining-editor, is a
Bendigonian. Langhan, the boss
advertising-man, is a Melbournite;
and even Macaulay, the harmless but
necessary printer, is another Victorian,
as well as most of the comps. It is
understood that at least several of these
able personages are, in private, highly
respectable Federalists, who, if they
had to write up the Bill . But on
matter. Still it is weird to think of the
frantically parochial N.S.W. Geebung
crowd having to hire all these Vic-
torians and other “outsiders” to howl
for it. Freetiade seems to have so de-
pressed N.S.W’s native industries that
even its parochial curse has to be im-
ported.
Gillies, member for West Maitland
in N.S.W. Leg. Assembly, rivals his next-door
member, Brunker, in thick-headednessandcapacity
for getting roads and bridges. Every party-leader
who wants Gillies’ support has to pay heavily on
the nail in roads-and-bridges. But when Premier
Reid, the other day, tried to borrow Gillies’ vote
for a close division, Gillies for once wouldn’t turn
an ear to the charmer —said he liked his charmers
less than 90in. round the waist, or words to that
effect. Reid argued, pleaded, persuaded—in vain.
“We can’t work together; we can’t vote together,”
said Gillies, on his way to the lavatory. “ Well,”
said Reid, “ there’s one thing we can do together,
Mr. Gillies.” “What’s that?” says Gillies.
“We can —wash our hands together,” says Yes-
No, beamingly. And they did.
“ Honest John ” Duthie—the adjective is
supposed to differentiate him from the other
member of his own party in M.L. House of Re-
presentatives—is at present laid on his back and
ordered to be kept very quiet, because of aortal
aneurism. He knocked himself up by a recent
stumping tour in opposition to the “ Sins of
Seddon.” On the campaign his most notable
utterances were one in which he advocated the
wholesale acquisition of Maori territory, on the
ground that no white man should have a “nigger
landlord,” and a statement that the population
which Maoriland gains from Australia is entirely
made up of “blacklegs and spielers.” Honest
John, though very Scotch and very dour, has since
found it expedient to apologise to the Maoris for
calling them niggers.
The Real Property Act, with its simple
and inexpensive system of titles-registration and
transfer, has become so universally adopted that
it is hard to believe that the man who helped its
originator, Torrens, to get it in operation has
only just died. Torrens, an old political hand
who had had much to do with the issue of original
grants in South Australia, got the idea that it
would involve little trouble to register every trans-
fer ; and that was the beginning of the existing
general legislation, administered first in his own
province by Torrens, and then by his former as-
sistant, W. B. T. Andrews. The latter has
within the last fortnight died, aged 81, having
been run over by a railway train near his home in
the Adelaide Hills.
Two well-known Melbourne characters are
named J. W. Fleming, and each snorts at any
mention of the other. Councillor J. W. Fleming,
who claims to be the first white native of Victoria,
lives at Brunswick, surrounded by his own pro-
putty and clothed in a profound sense of his im-
portance to the Hempire. Is said to have written
a letter to Queen Victoria, informing her that he
was born in the year when she came to the Throne,
j. W. Fleming, the perennial spokesman for the
unemployed, lives, works, and sleeps in one room,
and a small room at that. Every Sunday, wet or
dry, for 14 years the frugal bootmaker has held
open-air meetings, where the working-man is ex-
horted to buck-up for his rights. Has been twice
gaoled for “ agitating.” Reads history and swears
by the French Revolution.
“ The late saccharinely-suasive Gov. Sir
George Bowen,” writes Sydney D.T.’s interesting
London correspondent, “ always overdid his super-
latives. He was once dining with a party of judges
and lawyers, and describing rather exuberantly
the generous character of the Maori. ‘ Why,
sir,’ said he to the late Sir Janies Stephen, ‘ it was
my most painful duty during the New Zealand
war to order the execution of a gallant and very
influential and wealthy chief. Later, when peace
was declared, I made, on behalf of our gracious
sovereign, aprogress through the defunct warrior’s
domains. Would you believe it, gentlemen, his
brother not merely bore me no ill-will, but received
me with profuse acclamations ; yes, profuse accla-
mations.’ ‘Ah ! ’ said Judge Stephen, drily,
‘ I suppose he must have been the heir-at-law.’ ”
i Mrs. Nutting, who lately married
t' i' A r °y> Mount Morgan millionaire, in
London, brings wealth again to a stock that ex-
perienced many Australian vicissitudes. She was
the daughter of poor old Arthur Leslie Bouroi-
cault (brother of Dion, though he spelled his name
with an “r”) whose red waistcoat, velvet coat,
Bedford cords, and other haberdashery for years
brightened the goldfields of Rockhampton, Gym-
pie, and other Queensland mining centres.
I hen he tried Shepparton (Vic.); and a few
years ago he bought into a sheet at Ko-
garah, near Sydney, but was broken-spirited,
was soon carried to Rookwood Cemetery.
.e.Present Mrs. Nutting, a most charming girl,
originally Mary Bouroicault, was his favorite
child. She married Nutting in Australia, ob-
tained a divorce, and at her second wedding was,
curiously enough, given away by her ex-husband’s
brother.
An Age scribe, writing of Marcus Clarke’s
work, says that he wrote some 17 years too early,
and so fared no better pecuniarily than Sheridan,
Theodore Hook, and Poe. Which shows that
that scribe has yet to learn a thing or two. Sheri-
dan’s literary and dramatic work brought him in
many thousands; Hook made from .£2OOO to
■£3ooo a year out of his novels; and Poe could
probably have done well enough had he kept
straight. As for Clarke, he never would have
become a popular writer, in the sense that Kipling
and Barrie are popular. And he knew it. Kind
and generous and lovable as he was, he had not
the secret of touching the heart. And his humor
was not of a popular kind. Clarke, moreover, had
little of the creative faculty. His metier was that
of the French-polisher. He could take the crude
and ill-shaped, and make it a thing of beauty, but
the first mould was not his.

Duncan Gillies, avidly hungering for office,
feels the past A.-G.-ship hanging like a millstone
round his neck. The A.-G.-ship kills most
politicians who succumb to it. Even Berry re-
turned paralysed. But Duncan hopes still and
watches Turner 23 hours out of the 24. Thirty-one
years ago he held Ministerial office, and off and
on throughout the period between then and now-
be h as gathered in the victor’s spoils. For sixteen
y ears he has drawn official screw as Minister or
a.-G. Therefore belief that he will win again is
second nature.

I lie Commercial World (London) writes re-
garding the Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society
“ We always confidently anticipate a good year from this
vigorously-conducted Mutual Society. The circumstance,
therefore, that the figures for 1897 show no falling away
from the steady and substantial progress which is inse-
parably connected with the Society’s past career occasions
us no surprise. As a matter of fact, the Society earns its
success by deserving it, and, that much being conceded,
the best possible has been said for the continuance of the
large measure of public support which it has hitherto
fallen to its fortunate lot to receive.” Pleasant reading.*

Aboriginalities.
When things were “humming” in Ar.v
luen a preacher-man wandered into the valley after
converts, and one pay evening he mounted a giu-
case and held forth on tho Soul. “ Every man,”
said he, “has a soul to save. Even the poor
Irishman has a soul to save.” Then the gin-case
was kicked from under him, and he made for the
mountain witli numerous M‘Graths and O’Hooli-
gans, to say nothing of Ryans, in close pursuit.
Ihey ran him into a swamp at Jembaicumbene,
and he must have perished, for he has never been
heard of since.
X. : Recently witnessed an interesting
marriage at Balranald, N.S.W. Contracting
parties were aboriginals, and the bride, an ex-
pupu of the local convent, appeared in spotless
white, with orthodox veil, bouquet, etc. The
bridesmaids, also “darkies,” were equally la
mode. All rolled up to church in buggies; and,
“ le evening, about 50 corroboree’d on the hank
or the Murrumbidgee. There was but one hitch:
the pnest declining to ask any fee aroused my
lords ire. “You charge a white fellow five
pounds, and, by gum, I’ll promise yer’ll take a
liver from me.”

“N. 8.8.” : Curious the absence of time-
pieces of any kind amongst N.S.W. backblocks
cockles and selectors. “ Day-break,” “ dinner-
time, and “sundown ” are with them the only
time-marks. The children, and even some of the
parents, mostly can’t tell you the day of the
month, or even the month itself; and in many
cases they don’t even know what the year is.
Stayed once with shepherd who, when I
asked the time, would solemnly consult an old
(i^ r ?i ne^er an< l reply in a most specific way:
Alve-pasb height,” “twelve clock,” “ ten-to-
two,” and so on. Heard afterwards that when
the station-manager gave him the ancient baro-
meter he told him it was a clock.

“ Mac.” : Soon before the Clarkes took to
the bush, a. neighboring cookie caught them red-
handed killing one of his fat, two-year-old steers,
and galloped to Braidwood (nine miles) for the
police. Clarkes stood paralysed, but their sisters
“ came to the rescue,” drove up one of their own
two-year-olds, shot it on the same spot, bagged-up
the “ cronk ” steer, slung it on horse, and sank it
in Shoalhaven River, two miles off. The Clarkes
looked quite innocent when, two hours later, two
policemen and a tracker arrived and demanded
the “hoide.” “ The nixt toime, Mishthur Hogan,”
said the Italian sergeant, “ that ye bring me out
here on a woild-goose chase, be me sowl I won’t
come a fut av the way wid ye ! ”

“ Titoki ” tells of a tight place :
We were “telegraphing” posts across a M.L. ravine.
Had with us a new-chum, and one evening, having to
walk four miles round to get to the camp (which we could
see, not 200yds. away across ravine), we kidded Chummy
to cross on the wire. He accordingly fastened a stick on
a bit of wire, and, hanging it over the telegraph-line,
and then sitting astride the stick, let go. First 50yds. he
went like an express train, then gradually slowed up, and
stopped 50yds. from other side ; then came back a bit,
and then vice versa, until at last he stopped dead in the
middle of the wire, 150 ft. from the bottom. To climb
up the wire was impossible, and we hadn’t a rope
to send to his aid, so he had to stay there while we
went round to the camp for a wire, which took us three
hours, night setting-in meanwhile. We couldn’t see him
when we returned, so sent him a wire, but got no re-
sponse-puHed it back empty. Nothing for it but for
wn u f 1! S *?i "° alonsrtho wlr ° after him. But the wire
Di°hnih hai< Th C TC W0 > anil a break meant certain death
to both. The lighter of the pair of us, however ven
tured, and found Chummy in a dead faint, but still
gnpping the wire hard with hands and teeth. But the
o?p-°- f pU nff i up fcwo men b y myself from the very rim of
eternity cured me of practical jokes for life

“Meteor”: “A.A.A.’s” description of
duck-catching (8., 6/5/’99) contains nothing new •
but in a book of short native stories, written
by now-deceased School-Inspector Kevan it is
stated that the gins fastened round the men’s
bodies from waist up, bands of reeds interlaced
v ith twigs, the man having also round waist a
girdle of kangaroo-sinew nooses. The duck-
catcher then proceeded much as already described •
bub would sometimes actually occupy hours in’
leaving the water, so careful was he not to let the
ducks suspect what had been happening.
