“ Ben Sun ” : Can match
“ Ercildoune’s ” cocktail (8.,
22/4/’99). An insect, from
three-quarters to an inch Jong,
is found on the leaves of the
eucalyptus, from which it is
with difficulty detached. Has
on its back what looks like
two rows of tiny cells pro-
tected by thornlike points.
Its color is that of the leaves,
on the under-side of which it
is always found. A white
liquid oozes from it when dis-
turbed, contact with which is
like the touch of fire, and as
quickly felt; a blister is the
sure result. The most viru-
lent sort is found on stunted
box and messmate scrub; a
milder variety on the more
vigorous blue-gum. This in-
sect ought to have a career in
medical circles.

“Alia” ; Be curious
finds by dredges. Amongst
those chronicled in the M.L.
press is a roll of 30 miners’
rights, hauled up from the
bottom of the Molyneux
River. The rights dated back
to the early ’6o’s, and were
m fair state of preservation,
the issuer’s signature being
quite legible on several. At
Alexandra, on same river,
a dredging-party, breaking
down the river bank, found a
perfect moa’s egg 15ft. below
the surface. As this is only
the. second perfect egg of the
extinct bird ever found it
was almost as valuable to
the finder as if it had been
gold. As for such trifles as
human bones, they happen
quite regularly.
Dear Bulletin. —Have we now so few
birds worth shooting because they are “pro-
tected ? Once, when “flock” pigeons were
about, got 39 in two shots ! The birds alighted
on a 40ft. cabbage-tree, and weight of them
brought top of tree within 10ft. of ground. They
were after the berries, and ther'e must have been
thousands of them. At Lake Illawarra, a man
once shot 35 ducks in twice ; and, again, at Lake
Oeorge, I. “ potted ” 34 in two goes. Haven’t
seen any flock ” pigeons for years, and, when I
came upon wild-ducks the other day, I got a
severe start.— Albion Park.

“Yang A.” : Once belonged to a Borneo
club which had for each member a separate book,
recording his drinks and smokes, for monthly
square-up. The club “ boys ”—Chinamen—know-
ing no English, used Malay to communicate with
members, and each member’s book had a label,
with his name in Chinese and English. Present
scribe wrote his own label, and, later on, learned
that the Chinese version thereof was “long-haired,
spectacled fellow, who never wears a hat.” I took
the trouble to translate the Chinese “names ” on
some of the other books. “Solitary boozer,”
“Cock-eye,” “Red-headed slab,” “ Eour-eyes,”
“ Deaf-’un,” are fair equivalents for some of them,
and his Excellency was set down with charming
simplicity as “The big ———.” Another promi-
nent official appeared as “ The bigamist.” You
can’t beat the Chow for going straight to the
point.

“W.S.”: Not generally known that the
bustard, or plains turkey, lays two eggs (only)
each year, which are totally different in size,
shape, and markings. Have heard old bushmen
say the larger egg contains the male chick.
* * * *
“ J.A.” : Have found aboriginal stone
axes in Yie. and on the Murrumbidgee, Lachlan,
and Bogan. They are always made of same kind
of stone, very hard, fine in grain, and pale-green
in color. Any Bulletin reader say what stone
this is, where found, and for what purpose these
axes were chiefly used ?
“ C.D.” : Got this from eye-witness a few
days after occurrence. Pascoe River (Q.) blacks
have a standing feud with the Margaret Bay
tribe and sent a hostile expedition against them,
which, however, found the M.B. blacks too
numerous to deal with. Result of a parley was
that a nig. known as “Pascoe Johnny” had to
undergo the spear-ordeal, in return for several
former killings. Had to stand up and defend
himself with a woomerah from the spears thrown
at him from short range. He parried 14, but the
next struck him on the shoulder, turned down-
wards, and pierced his lungs. Blood gushed from
mouth, nose and ears, and in five minutes he was
dead. His body was taken charge of by his tribe,
who next day hung it in a tree and made a fire
underneath to smoke-dry it. It was then taken
down and back to Pascoe district.
* * * »
“ A.A. A." :Be scars on black-gins’ arms.
Have never seen any coast-tribe women marked
as “ H.M.” describes. The male aboriginal, how-
ever, is thus gashed on both breasts and arms on
attaining puberty. The scars, called “ Bheerans-
mer,” are always made after he has been through
his first keeparra, during which curious perform-
ance no women are allowed to be present. In
fact, have heard of women being killed for peep-
ing.
* * * *
“W.T.S.”: Was once told by an old
rabbiter, when I asked if he wasn’t afraid of
snakes, that there was no danger in putting your
hand in a “ covered-in ” or breeding burrow.
Found one, last winter, on a sand-ridge. Cleared
away the rubbish, poked a stick up about arm’s
length and felt something soft. Young rabbits, I
thought, and put my arm in to get them. Felt
something, and as it did not move, caught hold
and pulled out a big black snake in a state of
torpor. Have hardly recovered yet!
* * * *
“Kooka”: Be animal companionship. A
selector near Rockhamption, Q., had an old tom-
cat which chummed with one of his dogs but
would not let any other dog come near him. The
two friends would hunt together, and when the
owner went out shooting the cat would follow at
heel as well as the dog. Same selector had a
wallaby which had been brought up with the dogs.
Used to follow them all over the place, and if they
started a ’roo or a wallaby would join in the chase
with gusto. Was always in at the kill, and would
hop round the ring of tearing dogs as if to get a
good view of the proceedings.
DOWN THE BRIGHTON ROAD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Rapid hoof-beats ran a before ns,
Hitting with our hearts in chorus ;
Cabby sat in state decorous
In Ms high abode :
Did he see, or just surmise,
Cabby with the cunning eyes,
Driving us to Paradise
Down the Brighton Road ?
Rippling fleece that Heaven wrought
Fit to tempt an Argonaut,
Rebel to its r ibbons taut
O’er my shoulder flowed;
A nd, upon its gold, my sight
Caught a disc of silver light
Dear, the moon was full that night,
Down the Brighton Road!
THE PAGAN.


Roderic Quinn’s charming selection of
verses, “The Hidden Tide,’ 7 has been warmly
welcomed everywhere. The price is Is. 9d., post-
free from The Bulletin office.
“ Tom " Courtney (whose portrait is given
in this issue) is the political sledge-hammer with
which Sydney Daily Telegraph batters its Joss
and destroys all other things that temporarily
obstruct its conscienceless path. Acquaintances
who see the hard-hitting writer walking down
King-street, with hands behind his back, accuse
him of deep cogitation upon fresh methods of
falling upon whatever poor political gnat the D.T.
finds troublesome for the time being ; but Mr.
Courtney strenuously repudiates the idea. When
he thinks, it is to the accompaniment of his pen.
Neither does he ever take a note of any political
utterance ; if an orator’s shafts do not strike him
sufficiently hard for remembrance, they are pro-
bably not worth flinging at other people’s heads.
Mr. Courtney takes little stpck in politics for
other than journalistic purposes, and is regarded
in newspaper circles as the possessor of an ideally
journalistic temperament. There is the scribe
who can walk towards the office without thinking
of the leader which he is to be writing within the
next few minutes ; who, when he sits down, can
think of nothing else ; and who, when he
stands up again, after turning off a column,
can put on his hat and utterly forget
what he has written —the man whose heart
goes not one beat a minute faster with the flow of
his praise or denunciation : such a man is built
for daily-newspaper work, and lasts at the game.
Of Tom Courtney’s 40 years or so, twenty have
been spent in press work—more than two-thirds
of the time as D. T. leader-writer. It is said that
he stepped into serious newspaper work through
getting himself embroiled in a S. M. Herald
controversy with ex-Editor Garran; and the
Bulletin is devoutly sorry, for Australia’s sake,
that the timid organ of “ neutrality ” does not
employ writers of his fighting capacity—mind
you, they are hard to get—to sling bricks at Dibbs
and Haynes and S. A. Rosa and the two Heydons
and G. Pile’s dog—and the D. T.
Arthur Russell, American dentist, 8 Bourke-
street E., Melb., makes beautiful teeth or teeth
beautiful, whichever is necessary. Also painless
operations.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Dear Bulletin.— You ask what Meib.Onekeb
Club has done that is inimical to the interests of tru
sport I’ll mention a few things I happen to know off-
hand. It has bought up, or tried to buy up.everyfooL
bailer and cricketer of promise in rival clubs,, till the
wonder of it is that the huge monster has not dc.eated its
own ends by having nobody to play against .“ h “
the moving spirit of the cricket and football leagues
which were formed a few seasons ago for th e sole Purpose
of getting all the gate-money into the hands of the big
and wealthy clubs and blocking the aspiring and poorer
ones from any chance of progress ; and, in its play on tne
cricket-field, it has been guilty of devices as mean and
contemptible as any that have ever disgraced the shadiest
suburban pony-course. In addition to this, the M.u.U
is responsible for the bringing to Australia of those or-
ganisations of cads and beer-sparrers known as English
elevens. The visit of an English team merely tends to
drag in the shekels to the promoters, and, as lntercluD
cricket is practically spoilt for the season, lads whose
play would be gradually improving during that time nuss
a year’s play.—M‘G.

“Face”: Re gray horses. Bulle-
tin par recalls the wonderful little Lillie
Bridge pony of a few years back—Fashion.
She was a'gray and only 12 hands high,
but a regular little race-horse, and wor-
shipped by pony punters as though she
were a Carbine—which she was in her
class, and there w ere smart ponies about
then. Fashion carried some remarkable
weights home in record time, and in her
last race humped 12‘9 (more than a stone
per hand) in a 3-furlong h’cap and won in
a canter, the crowd cheering her home
from when she went to the front at half-
way. She was entered again but the han-
dicapper piled 13'7 on "to her, and her
owner refused to allow her to start and
sent his little champion to the stud. Al-
though gray horses are as a rule even-
tempered, Fashion w r as a little tigress and
always gave trouble at the post.

Amusing to read that Dawson,
who is fully 25 years younger than John
Roberts, attributed his defeat, in their re-
cent billiard-match to ill-health, and threa-
tened to have another go at "the failing old
’un on even terms next year. Meanwhile
somebody offers to back the champion
for £lOOO to give Dawson 5500 points in a
game of 21,000 up, w 7 ith the proviso that
the balls shall be bonzoline, instead of
ivory. The objection to ivory is in the
impossibility of ensuring an even distri-
bution of weight throughout the ball. Two
balls may be of exactly the same size and
weight, but either of them is likely to be
heavier iii one place than another.
* * *
Miss Grace Fairweather, the pre-
sent champion lady billiard-player of Eng-
land, got her first instructions in the
higher branches of the game from John
Roberts and his brother Charles, who seem
to have advised their fair friend to go in
chiefly for nursery cannons. Her best
breaks up to date are 82 (all cannons) and 72 (made all
round the table), and she gets her 30’s and 40’s as freely
as the average male amateur of the advanced rank. All
the same, Miss Fairweather pleads guilty to being “no-
thing but nerves ’’ when she faces an audience. She
starts badly every time, and would probably get her
heart broken early in the game if she matched herself
against a mere man who was no better than herself in
private practice. Not that the average man can play his
best before a crowd, unless he has a soft thing on, but
Miss Grace would be the necessary soft thing. Now, sup-
posing Tliekla Dubberke had been born to “ live on a
billiard-table.’ She might have developed into a shining
light.
• * * •
“ Gobung ”: Re Sloan seat. Pouy-jockey
Albrecht was riding this style at Breakfast Creek (Bris-
bane) some six years ago, before Sloan’s riding was ever
commented on in Australia. Being then only a beginner,
he got very few decent mounts, but managed neverthe-
less to get some rank outsiders home. Believe he is at
present in Sydney, but cannot say if he still rides same
way.
* * * *
“ Burnie ” : Re the Tod Sloan seat. What
would happen to Tod or his imitators should a horse
stumble? Writer can sit as tight as most people, but
wouldn’t take a 1000 to 1 about sticking on a horse’s
withers if he stumbled.
* * * *
“ Scrip ” : Experienced cab-horses become very
cunning. A big-repository runner tells me that some of
his best workers are the most eccentric when at home and
out of collar. One, reserved for early-morning jobs, un-
dresses himself when harnessed and tied up, getting every
scrap of leather off with fire-brigade smartness. Then,
chewing the rope through, backs out, uncovers all the
corn-bins in turn, and concludes supper with a few dozen
carrots. Another, given the liberty of the yard, eats any-
thing, from bread to blackberries ; knocks at the kitchen-
door for his dole, and, if it is open, walks in and helps
himself. He can turn the pantry-key, and is an incur-
able thief. A piebald, once owned by an indulgent publi-
can, whips out when he sees a gate open, and trots into
the nearest pnb. to see if his name’s good enough for a
beer.
* * ♦ *
There are a hundred ways of driving a coach-
and-four through an Act of Parliament. “ Tattersall,”
precluded by legislation from advertising his consulta-
tions in Queensland, simply inserts his card in the papers :
“ ‘ Tattersall,’ care of George Adams, Hobart, Tasmania.”
It gets there just the same.
* * • *
When Charley Turner visited Woolwich Ar-
senal, he was asked to bowl through the electric screens
used for measuring the velocity of projectiles. At a
point representing half-way between the wickets it was
found that the velocity of the ball was 81ft. per second,
or 50 miles an hour. At this rate the ball just takes
22-27ths of a second to traverse a cricket-pitch. Now,
“Jonah” is a considerably faster bowler than Turner,
therefore, it is not overstating the fact when one says
that the South Australian bowls at the rate of a mile a
minute.

Many cycle-racing smashes are caused by one
man who, acting as pacemaker for another, brings the
pacee up to the front lot and, having accomplished what
he was paid for, calmly sits up and has the track a mass
of broken machines and men a moment later. The thing
has been long going on.

Howell’s ten-wickets’ feat will make him in de-
mand in England. It was asserted a while ago that
Phillips intends to make Penrith Bill his next recruit for
Middlesex. The prospect of one or two more trips to
England may influence Bill against staying, but the big
benefit which comes to every English pro. is also alluring.

HOODWINKING JUSTICE.
"MARYBO[?]OUGH, Friday.-The boy Windsor, aged 14, who pleaded pleaded guilty to having attemted to wreck a train on the Wycheproof line, was brought up for sentence at the Marybough Criminal Court to-day. Mr. Justice Hood felt very much perplexed what to do with the boy. If he had the power he would order
him a smart nogging; but, unfortunately, he had not that power. If he sent him to prison it would be be for a long term, and that would undoubtedly make the lad a confirmed criminsl. The only course was to send him to the reformatory, at Royal Park, where he ordered him to be kept until he reached the age of 1S
years. His Honor expressed a hope that some excuse would be found at the reformatory to give him a sound flogging."
JUDGE HOOD: "The law doesn't allow ME to flog this boy; but there's nothing to prevent you from commiting reformation with violence." THE REFORMER: "Suppose the boy's conduct is exemplary?" JUDGE HOOD: "Then give some exemplary punishment!"

June next The estimated number of appointments to
be made is 40. The limits of age for candidates are as
follows Males, 16 to 19 years ; females, 16 to -•> leais on
the first davof examination in each case, lliej must be of
.mod character, and in sound health. Persons already
employed in the General Division of the Publics Service
will be admitted to the examination, provided their age
does not exceed 30 years. The subjects of exam'mitw
will be as follow :-(l) Handwriting ; (2) English. Dicta

_ PUBLIC SERVICE BOARD,

A COMPETITIVE EXAMINATION OF CANDIDATES
A for the POSITION of “CADET DRAFTSMAN ” in
the PUBLIC SERVICE of the COLONY, will be held in
SYDNEY, and at various country centres, which will be
fixed as far as possible to suit the convenience of candi-
dates, on the 26th June and following days. The esti-
mated number of appointments to be made is 10. Candi-
dates must not be less than 16 nor more than 21 years of
age on the first day of examination, of good character,
and in sound health. Persons already employed in the
General Division of the Public Service will be admitted to
the examination provided their age does not exceed 30
years. The subjects in which candidates will be ex-
amined are "—Handwriting, English (Dictation, Compo-
sition, precis writing, Parsing, Analysis, and derivation
of words); Arithmetic (full course) ; Geometry (Euclid,
first book) ; Geography (outlines of physical and political
geograph, and the geography of the Australasian
Colonies in moderate detail); English History, from
the Middle of the Eighteenth Century to the present
time, with special reference to the Colonies and De-
pendencies ; Mathemathics (Geometry, Euclid, books 2 to
4 and 6, Algebra, up to (Quadratic Equations, and to
include the theory and use of logarithms, Plane Trigo-
nometry, up ..to and including solution of triangles) ;
Elementary Plan Drawing. Application to attend the
examination must be made on a form for the purpose,
which can be obtained at the Office of the Board, and
must reach the undersigned not later than Ist June.
Candidates will be required to pay an examination fee of
10s., which must be lodged with a Clerk of Petty
Sessions or the Treasury, and the receipt therefor for-
warded to the Secretary to the Board with the form of
application. Candidates may be examined at any centre
(outside the Sydney radius), where persons approved by
the Board are" Willing to superintend the examination.
By order of the Board,
9a.78. J. W. HOLLIMAN, Secretary.

Dreyfus Still a Martyr.
Imprisoned on a lonely island. Surrounded by
miasmatic vapours, and deprived of all but the
necessaries of existence. Poor Dreyfus suffered
untold martyrdom, and thousands thought of his
probable innocence, and pitied him in his suffer-
ing. There are, however, martyrs in our midst
deserving of our pity—those who are quite as
innocent; those who are starving, with plenty
around them,' hungry all the time, but dare not
satisfy their craving for food, because they are
martyrs to indigestion. The well-known phreno-
logist of High-street, Dunedin, Mr. A. E
Wheeler, thus pictures his experience : “ Having
been a martyr for many years to that terrible
malady, indigestion, I was induced to try a bottle
of Warner’s Safe Cure, which was said to give
speedy relief,
“ Not being one who swallows every new pre-
scription that is forced upon the public, it took
some persuasion before I tried the aforesaid ‘Safe’
Cure, and I found, to my great delight, that its
virtues were in no way over estimated. Although
not through with the first bottle, I have been
agreeably conscious of its merits in relieving this
terribly afflicting complaint, and can only in fair-
ness add my testimony to the many others who
have secured relief from using Warner’s Safe
Cure.”*

On Household Suffrage, the reform which

• P°hcy, S.A. Assembly is almost equally
divided. In a House of 56 members the Govt,
reckon on 28 votes in favor.
Jack ’ Want was Attorney-General in the
<ennings, the Reid and the first Dibbs Ministries.
He declares himself an out-and-out Freetrader,
but he assisted Jennings and Dibbs to ad valorem,
auties and Reid to sugar and other Custom House
imposts.
®! en the tar - off Canterbury Times
(Christchurch, M.L.) has got hold of that har-
rowing idea propounded by the Sydney anti-
Federalists, that, because women have votes
m o. A. and not in the other provinces,
A. will have twice the voting-power
of the other provinces in proportion
to population. The fact that every pro-
vince is allotted its members in the
i ederal Lower House according 1 to
its population (men, women and child-
ren all counted), and that S.A.’s
seven members will still be only seven
whether they are elected by manhood-
sutirage, or man- woman-child-and-dog
a j") 6 ’ certainl y looks clear enough.
And the further provision that, at a
referendum, only half the votes of the
man-and-woman-suffrage states shall be
counted ought to make the rest of the case
comprehensible even to the worst variety of idiot.

o rope-m ex-prize-fighters to bear political testi-

chair of Queensland Assembly looks as if the star
of Boodle was on the wane. Cowley was a useful
Speaker. No man could suspend the whole Labor-
party for alleged “ disorderly conduct ” more
promptly than he, and for shutting down on any
remark which was unpleasant to the Fat Man
Ministry he had no equal. Cowley dragged the
Boodle Ministry out of many a tight place and he
will be greatly missed.

The inner influences of Q. politics can be
suggested by saying that had Byrnes continued
premier, Glassey would have remained leader of
Opposition. Dickson, like Nelson, hates Glassey.
In Queensland particularly the leader of the Govt,
nas often been a powerful factor in the setting up
and knocking down of the Opposition chief.
Jack Gordon, who was one of the S.A.
Convention delegates, says his profession (law)
will prevent him giving up local bread-winning
tor federal politics, and so, when Kingston,
Play ford, and Holder go away, he intends to stop
at home, transfer his services from the Council to
the Assembly, and pick up the vacant Premier-
snip.
it likely that the State Parliaments
will reduce their numbers ? ” cries John Haynes,
as a wildly irrelevant argument for provincialism.
Well, the oldest Parliament in Australasia is just
about 50 years of age, and already two out of the
seven Legislatures have reduced the number of
their members. Perhaps that has some bearing
on the frantic John’s case.
Sydney D.T. reaches out desperately in-
deed when it “interviews" Mr. L. Foley the
pugilist, on “ the downfall of Sydney ” under the
Constitution Bill. Most Sydneyites, while cheer-
fully acknowledging Mr. Laurence Foley as a
competent authority on cross-counters and biffs
on the jaw, are somewhat indisposed to accept
him as an oracle on Constitutional affairs.
One little two-seated S.A. country con-
stituency—the Burra—is represented by one man
who has been Treasurer four times, and another
who has had that office three times. And both of
them are local-born, and neither is much pasb 50.
It illustrates the lightning changes in S.A.
politics—until Kingston consolidated the Liberal
forces and bound the Labor-men to him.
The Kingston (S.A.) Govt, completes its
sixth year of office on Friday, June 16, which is
the week before Parliament meets. Its original
members were Kingston, Playford, Cockburn,
Holder, Gordon, and Gilien. Of these, Playford
and Cockburn in succession have been made
Agents-General, Gillen is dead, and Gordon re-
signed over the wretched Hospital dispute.
Premier Dickson has informed Q. Parlia-
ment that the present session is to be devoted to
Federation only; so, apparently, plain Dickson,
the bald house-agent, is- going to really make an
effort in the cause of union. And, as for M‘ll-
- who used to pose as the great brass god of
tne movement, there is talk of raising a subscrip-
tion to pay that fallen Joss’s expenses back from
Lurope, so that he can lift up the remnant of his
curse and influence in the cause of Disunion-at-
any-Price.
The Kingston Govt. (S.A) is as hard-up as
ever for lawyers’ support—or should it be as for-
tunate as ever in being without it ? Of the 54
members of Assembly, the Ministry can rely on
the support of, say, 31—and Kingston himself is
the solitary lawyer of the crowd. Of the other
23, four only are lawyers—Downer, Glynn (a
doubtful quantity always), Homburg (ditto), and
a new man, Yon Doussa. Apart from Glynn,
there isn’t a University graduate, not even a
licentiate in medicine, in the House.
The S.A. Referendum on Household Suf-
frage at Council elections produced 48,936 votes in
favor, 33,830 against, and 11,094 informal, almost
all the latter being voting-papers on which the
Household Suffrage question was left unanswered.
So the good old Tory-party rises to wrangle that the
noes ’ and the informal votes added together are
only 4000 less than the affirmative vote, and that
when the people who didn’t get to the poll at all’
and the dead people, and the imbeciles, and the
dogs and cats, and th© people wjho aren J t born yet
are added, S.A. gives a clear majority J against
Council reform.

Premier Reid has now set himself to show
Sydney Daily Telegraph what his power is on the
piattorm. It will be an interesting struggle, and
ill size-up the value of the different factors in the
various Tree trade victories. We know what

The Sydney provincial organ keeps digging
up, as an argument against Federation, that luck-
less last-year’s declaration of Statistician Coghlan:
• not know of any country in the world in which the
™v™ue, stated as an average ad-valorem,
ported 8 *° 27 Per ° ent ‘ 0n the whole of the % oods im-
And in the latest edition of Coghlan’s own “ Sta-
tistics of the Seven Colonies,” which was in circu-
lation at the time of that misguided remark, this
appeared : ‘ Tasmania (1896), imports <£1,192,410 :

Tas. Agent-General Fysh has cabled Pre-
mier Braddon asking permission to accept a seat
on the directorate of the Great Western Railway
syndicate. In view of the Premier’s own flirting
with syndicates and the loaning of his name to
various ventures, The Bulletin hardly sees how
he can refuse Fysh’s application to do a little
guinea-pigging. The only possible bar to the
appointment is the fact that the Railway co.
has forfeited its rights under the Act, which
rights cannot be re-instated until Parliament
meets (May 30), therefore it would appear inde-
cent for the Agent-General to take under his
protection a co. that is practically outlawed.
Capt. Russell, leader of the Tory Opposi-
tion in M.L., assures the people : “ I never ob-
-jected to the principle of the Old Age Pensions
Bill, and nothing would be further from my
thoughts than to repeal it even if I got the oppor-
tumty ” Which is very beautiful indeed—only as
Russell voted and spoke against the Old Age
Pensions Bill at every opportunity his assurance
doesn t seem to be worth the oxygen it took to
utter it, And his promise that he won’t repeal
THE “P.M.G.” ON HIS BIKE .
the Pension Law if he gets into power wouldn’t
sell for 2£d. He doesn’t need to repeal it. It is
on!y a temporary Act and will expire automatic-
ally without any repealing. Unless Russell will
furnish his solemn oath in writing that he will
renew the law if he is in ppwer the above effusive
promise is only a whited sepulchre—and not much
of that.
The Advances to Settlers “Act, having
weathered all storms, is now in operation in N.S.
Wales. The Board established in Sydney re-
ceived in one week applications for ,£60,000 in
loans from <£lo to £2OO. The principle is far from
being the novelty in N.S.W. which its opponents
have been, constantly maintaining 1 . In the partly-
nominated and partly-elected N.S.W\ Legislature
of 1843, the wool and cattle growers, then mone-
tarily embarrassed as selectors are now, were
treated to a similar Bill at the hands of Richard
Windeyer, father of the late Judge W. A Land
Board was appointed ; the squatter who wished
to obtain a loan applied to it; the Board valued
his property and gave him a State-credit amount-
ing to one-half. That was the scheme, at least,
but it didn’t quite come off. The Governor of the
day withheld the royal assent, and, as communi-

“People take more interest in a cricket-match
than in Federation.”
Then, why not let Georgie and Toby make the

cation was then slow, the crisis passed before
further steps were taken.
When Sydney Daily Telegraph puts
Premier Reid and Joe Cook and other present
federalists of the Cornstalk Ministry in the
witness-box by quoting their anti-federal utter-
ances of a year ago—what does it prove ? If the
Pill isn’t the same Bill (and the Telegraph some-
times says it is the same bad old Bill and some-
times that it is a worse new one) then the circum-
stances may account for their change of front. If
it is the same Bill then they may have told the
truth last June (as the Telegraph said) and be
telling untruths now (as the Telegraph says). Or
they may have told untruths last June (and the
lelegraph may then have perjured itself by say-
mg that what they said was true) and be telling
truth now (consequently the Telegraph may be
again perjuring itself by saying that what they
say now isn’t true). This paragraph is recom-
mended as a test in cases of suspected intoxica-
tion. Any person who can clearly explain the
situation is sober enough to pass muster.
Louis von Doussa, elected for Mt. Barker
(S. A.) by 30 votes over the second Liberal candi-
date, is a talkative solicitor with hopelessly-
retrograde views. Yet in ’B7 he worked most
enthusiastically to put Dr. Cockburn in for the
district, and he succeeded. However, he soon
recanted, and until the doctor’s departure for
England last year, “Yon’’worked with great
eagerness to put him out again. He was born
in the district, and has lived there all his life,
but rias only just managed to scrape past the
fvingstonian who stood third on the poll, the
senior member being also a Liberal.
Theodore Uninack—“ Ten-bob ” Unmack
—a sour person who once was Minister of Some-
thmg in Queensland, croaks on the pallid bust of
Pallas against the Federation Bill. He told his
hearers that Queensland would lose, among
other things, £400,000 paid as duty on Eng-
lish and foreign goods brought from the other
provinces. As it happens the £400,000 will
still belong to Queensland ; clause 93 of the
Commonwealth Bill distinctly provides that
the State consuming the goods shall be credited
with the duty. Before this ghostly ten-bob Ger-
man again takes the anti-Federation stump lie
should get a copy of the Bill and try to under-
stand it.

The long-missing steamer Perthshire has
been sighted at last, drifting helplessly about be-
tween the Auckland and Wellington tracks. The
general remarks about the Perthshire illustrate a
curious kink in the public mind. To speculations
about her prooable loss, the invariable replies were
either Yes, but fortunately she has no pas-
sengers on board”; or else, “How many pas-
lfe7 e ‘<Vb^> boar m • T!r ne \ Then > whh i-e-
-{• > J-hats a blessing!” Apparently sailors’
lives don t count, fortunately she had passengers
on board or the popular deity might have neg-
lected her, too. It is the same kink that allows
the compulsory-inspection clause of Navigation
Acts to apply to passenger-ships only.
Chief-Justice Griffith’s chairmanship of
Barton and Deakm’s pro-Bill meeting in Brisbane
proves how tenaciously he adheres to the views
he expressed as to the non-party character of
federation in the Sydney Convention of years
ago. Then he said that office should not hinder
any Australian from takingthe part his convictions
favored in respect to Federation. His views
however, don’t commend themselves to the Chief
Justices of other provinces.

Stevenson, M.L.A. (N.S.W.), who died
suddenly the other day by the roadside, is the
first parliamentary martyr to anti-Federal deter-
mination. Though in feeble health, he insisted
on touring his straggling oonstitueney of North-
umberland in angry altercation with Billites, and
his heart gave way. He was an ideal M.P. of the
roads-and-bridges order—the type of which a
Federal Parliament will know nothing.

The Bulletin lets “ Run-Out ” indulge in lugu-
briousi prophecy Amusing for the Agk’s Oracle to set
down (11 5 99) that “ he had staked his reputation as a
judge on his statement that he could not believe it
possible for Laver to be a success with the bat in big
cricket on the slower English wickets.” “Mid-on’s”
reputation as a judge ! When Trot.t’s last Australian XI
was being chosen “Mid-On” untiringly advocated the
claims of Eady, partly because he could play cricket a
little, and mainly on account of his “ social qualities”—
as he expressed it. And all the while Turner then Aus-
tralia’s best bowler, remained unchosen. Again when
the present team was being picked “ Mid-On ” ignored
Giffen as a past number, and boomed Worrall as a rising
star - Worrall, one of the failures of the Australian XI. of
88, probably the weakest team that ever visited England
Yet, despite these achievements, “Mid-On” cavils at
Laver’s inclusion, and gravely talks of staking his repu-
tation on Laver’s failure. For the reason that the present
team does not comprise Australia’s full strength, many
judges with reputations expect' it to go down every time
it meets England’s best. Worrall, Laver, and Trimmer
are “passengers” who can thank newspaper and social
influences for their presence in England. When the Aus
tralian XL of ’99 returns home utterly beaten, the public
who provide the gate-money will want to know why Giffen
and M’Kibbin were not in the team, and why cricketers
were not chosen in preference to newspaper and drawing-
room men. b

. Steeplechasing threatens to die out altogether
in Sydney, for, when the old ’chasers die-and they can’t
in the natural order of things, live much longer-there
won’t be a nag left capable of completing the course.
The Steeple at Randwick on Sat. was an absolute farce
Four out of five starters were new-comers, two of whom
promptly fell in the first half-mile, the third laboriously
climbed his fences for a round, and the fourth (Echo)
stood up and performed so brilliantly that YVhitefoot 11..
aged and slow as a top, was able to beat him by furlongs.’
Poor old Whitefoot has been running in Sydney steeples
for years and now stops almost to a walk in a finish, so
what sort of an embryo G.N. candidate must Echo be?
it is strange that some Sydney owner doesn’t step in and
help himself to the shekels that await the first man who
letches along anything like a decent steeple-horse.
* • • »
Size counts for little judging by the recent suc-
cess of the galloway mare, Water Color, at Randwick.
She was the smallest candidate in the Turf Club handi-
cap, and carried top-weight.

Old-time showman Stuart Cumberland,
after playing many parts (including that of Uit-
lamlers' champion) bobs up in London as the
newest member of the Stock Exchange.
The man who turned Queen’s evidence
against six companions accused of outraging
a girl at North Shore 27 years ago, died last week
at Prince Alfred Hospital, Sydney, from self-
inflicted wounds—his second attempt at suicide.
The death-sentences were all commuted.
An American female missionary in the city
of Nanking, recently married a Mandarin of the
seventh rank whom she had converted. Her
action caused a scandal in missionary circlps and
she was “sacked.” The lady has now started a
rival establishment, and intends appealing to
American churches for support.
The two last commutations make five
death-sentences—all for different offences—an-
nulled by N.S.W. Executive in one fortnight.
Time N.S.W Judges gave up shedding tears and
pathetic prayers over victims behind the spikes.
Indeed, if death-sentences are to be saved from
degenerating into burlesque, Judges should with-
hold them until the will of the Executive is
known.
“ British fair play ” was strong at the
Melb. meeting of sympathy with the Uitlanders.
Mayor M'Eacharn, who expects a knighthood
shortly, was respectably enthusiastic about “our
kinsmen across the seas,” and the lead of the
Cockney Highlander was followed in a wild, up-
roarious strain by the rank-and-file. Then there
rose a quiet-looking young man in spectacles, who
evidently knew what he was talking about, and
who managed to get out several nasty and telling
truths about the British cry from Boerland before
the meeting rose and howled him into siience.
Then Mayor M‘Eacharn tried to block the speaker
by asking him if he were a British subject, and,
hearing an affirmative reply, the meeting saw no
hope but to prevent the other side being heard by
the good old British-fair-play method of continu-
ous hooting. ; 1)

“ If I were asked which single medicine I should

The Tonga news published in Sydney last
month turns the mind back to the days when
Shirley Baker was Premier. That bitter Wes-
leyan was the beginner of the trouble. The Rev.
Baker raised a loan of .£3OOO from a German firm
trading in Tonga to replenish the coffers of the
Tongan church. The security he gave for this
loan consisted of I O TJ’s signed by certain boss
islanders and redeemable in copra. But only a
few of the notes have yet been redeemed. Owing
to Baker’s position as Premier the trading co.
endeavored to make the Tongan Govt, responsible,
but the late King George repudiated liability. A
German war-vessel was then enlisted, and has
ever since been hanging around screaming for
the money, and sometimes receiving a trifle on
account, but running up other accounts at the
same time. The present King, the long, lanky
youth who picked up general knowledge at Newing-
ton College, Sydney, is more easily cornered than
King George, and is besides so bewildered over a
long-standing marriage difficulty that it is impos-
sible to forecast whether he will fight or resign.
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
El i zabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
Melb. Herald having recently asked to
whom would the spouse of seven husbands belong
in the “spirit world,” has been swamped by a
flood of “intelligence” from the astral plane and
other regions beyond the jurisdiction of the
divorce court. Some of the correspondents h ve
invoked the opinion of departed spirits on the
point, and one seer, upon the authority of a
“meejum,” asserts that everybody shall find in
spirit-land a true affinity, irrespective of all mun-
dane connections. In support he quotes Robert
Burns, who, “now forever happy in Paradise
with the young woman known as Highland
Mary,” forwards per “meejum” aforesaid, the
following “ pome ”: —
“ Tell them that, as I passed frae earth,
Ma angel-lassie, crown’d wi’ flowers,
Met me wi’ glowing love-lit torch
An’ led me to the Nuptial bowers :
That a’ we dream’d o’ wedded bliss—
An’ mair—was meeted to us there,
Whar sweeter was my deary’s kiss
Than the flowery banks o’ Ayr :
Whar love’s celestial fountains play’d,
An’ rosebuds burst, an’ seraphs sang,
An’ myrtle twined our couch to shade,
I clasp’d the ane I mourn’d sae lang ;
An’ whilst by angel-harp -i was play’d
The bonny bridal serenade,
Tho’ nae gown’d priest the kirk-rite said,
Burns was wi’ Highland Mary wed.
There’s nae destroying death-frost there,
To nip the hope-buds ere they bloom :
The bridal tour is through the spheres—
Eternity’s the honeymoon ! ”

Whereas I suffered severely from a very troublesome
affection of the NERVES, which affected me so much that

several doctors and many remedies, but all of no avail.

mussen’s Purely Herbal ALFALINE VITALITY PILLS,

this is over five years ago I have remained well ever since.
The ALFALINE PILLS also cured my wife, and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the same
to be true.
Declared at Sydney this 29th day of April, 1898.
TIIOMAS MUDIMAN.

„ . Bourke-street, Melbourne.
Dear Sir,—
In November, 1898, I was seized with a violent
attack of muscular rheumatism in the left arm and
side, which continued getting- worse, until I could not
straighten my arm, also could not get any sleep.
I was attended l>3 r a doctor, and received no benefit
therefrom.
I also tried many other remedies, which did me no
good.
About the end of March I was advised to try “ VITA-
DATIO,” and I did so.
I am glad to say that after taking a few doses I began
to get better, and now after four bottles I am cured,
and I feel quite “ A New Man.”
I am sending this testimonial, and I wish you to
make the best use you can of it, as I wish others who
are in the same condition that I was to try Vitadatio,
and get cured.
WILLIAM SIMMONDS.
Warren-road, Caulfield.
Witness—F. J. Wooster.

Palmerston North, N.Z.,
25th April, 1899.
Mr. Palmer (VITADATIO)
Dear Sir,—On Bth August, 1898, my son, aged 12,
complained of swelling on his right side ; on examina-
tion by a doctor it was pronounced to be “ hydatids on
the liver,” and an operation was ordered to take place
immediately for the removal of the growth.
Meeting Mr. Hepworth, eclectic chemist of this town,
to whom I mentioned the trouble, he recommended me
to try a bottle of Vitadatio before having recourse to an
operation, which would be necessarily attended with
more or less risk.
I procured a bottle, and within an hour after the first
dose an improvement in the symptoms was perceptible,
and within a period of two or three weeks he was per-
fectly well, and has remained so, enjoying excellent
health without any return of the former'trouble.
I obtained for him altogether four bottles, and the
trial proved most satisfactory indeed; far beyond my
anticipations.
ALEX. M‘MINN,
_ „ Journalist.
Witness—J. Hepworth.
A WELL-KNOWN CITY MAN COURTEOUSLY
GIVES VITADATIO CREDIT.
Prell’s-buildings,
60-70 Queen-street, Melbourne.
_ . May 5, 1899.
S. A. PALMER, Esq.,
45-47 Bourke-street.
Dear Sir, —
I have been hesitating for the last six months relative
to the actual merits of your famous medicine, “ Vita-
datio,” and although I am anxious at all times to re-
commend any sterling recipe for complaints similar to
that from which I have suffered for the last ten years,
namely, sciatic rheumatism, I, knowing that my opinion
and recommendation would carry weight amongst a
wide circle of acquaintances, felt I must actually satisfy
myself beyond the slightest doubt before expressing an
opinion, good, bad, or indifferent.
I have during my long career travelled pretty well all
over the world, tried many prescriptions, and acted
upon medjcal advice from gentlemen of repute and
standing.
I must say now conscientiously that although I have
not been at all consistent regarding diet, &c., during
the time I have taken “ Vitadatio,” still I have derived
greater benefit from the same than from any other
medicine. Each attack of sciatic rheumatism gradually
lessens in its intensity, and without doubt is rapidlv
being eradicated from my system.
I think it a duty to give this testimonial, and fer-
vently hope that those who have suffered like myself
will be equally successful in obtaining relief. With
good wishes for the future.
Obediently yours,
FRED. B. NORTON.

Two of a Trade.
That two of a trade seldom agree is a common
saying. Its weakness, however, resides in the fact
that it is a cavilling, sneering saying. The idea
sought to be conveyed is that the disagreement is
the outcome of reciprocal jealousy. While that is
likely, it is not a necessary, or even a philosophical,
inference. Two of a trade may easily see reasons
for an honest difference of opinion to which the
outsider is blind. Again, two of a trade may agree
and both be wrong—on a point, of course, con-
nected with their own industry. Some years ago
there was high debate over the question whether a
painting, exhibited in Paris, was an original
Velasquez or a copy. Half the artists and
connoisseurs in Europe got hot under the collar
about it. It was one or the other—so they said.
Later on the fact came out. It was neither an
original nor a copy ; it was a replica. The experts
were mistaken. And so runs speculative judg nent
in everything.
Here is the case of two doctors ; both, doubtless,
competent men. If they were wrong, or if only
one was wrong—but let us have the story first.
It comes from a reputable source, and is well
corroborated.
“In March, 1891,” says the relater, “I had a
severe attack of influenza, which prostrated me
for two months. After this I could not get up my
strength. My appetite was poor, and what little
I did eat gave me much pain at the chest and
around the heart. Sharp, cutting pains in the re-
gion of the heart seized me every now and again,
sometimes so bad I feared I was going to die. At
night I got little or no sleep on account of wind,
which rose into my throat until I fairly gasped
for breath. During the painful attacks of my
complaint perspiration would stand in beads upon
my face.
“ I soon lost strength to that extent I could not
stand. Indeed, I was weak as a child. I was
often so dizzy I had to catch hold of something to
keep me from falling. Several times these attacks
have come upon me at concerts, obliging my friends
to conduct me home. As time passed on I grew
more and more feeble, and abandoned all hope of
ever being well and strong again.
“ I had two doctors attending me who prescribed
medicines ; which, however, eased me only for a
time and then I was as bad as ever.
“ One doctor said I had pleurisy ; the other said
I had heart disease.
“For two and one-half years I lingered along,
nearly as much dead as alive, all my relatives and
friends thinking 1 would not recover. In Novem-
ber, 1893, a book was left at my house in which I
read of a case like mine having been cured by
Mother Seigel’s Syrup. My wife procured me a
bottle from the Provincial Drug Stores in West-
gate-street, and the first bottle gave me so much
relief that I continued with the medicine. I could
then eat well, and the food agreed with me, the
pain around the heart soon ceasing.
“ In a short time my strength returned, and I
got back to my work well and vigorous. Since
then I have been in the best of health,.. You are at
liberty to publish this statement and refer tome.”
—(Signed) William Henry Jervis, 48 Rendlesham
Road, All Saints, Ipswich, November 13th, 1897.
One of Mr. Jervis’s doctors pronounced his com-
plaint to be pleurisy; the other said it was heart
disease. Were they both right, or both wrong?
Or was one right and the other wrong? In the
latter case—which one ? Judging from the symp-
toms as set forth by Mr. Jervis the probability is
that both were right— as far as they went.
The sac or bag which surrounds the heart (called
the pericardium), and the sac in which the lungs
rest (called the pleurae), are parts of the lymphatic
system ; which is the especial abiding place and
stamping ground of the kind of poison, produced
by the' diseased digestive system, and the cause of
rheumatism, gout, pleurisy, and heart disease.
Now, after (if not before) his attack of influenza
Mr. Jervis suffered from acute dyspepsia with
torpid liver, which engendered the poison that set
up a mild form of both pleurisy and heart dis-
order. When the real and underlying ailment of
all—the dyspepsia—w as cured by Mother Seigel’s
Syrup these supplementary or consequential
troubles vanished, as might be expected.
So we see that - strange as it may seem—two of
a trade can differ and both be right.*

A DARK AND GLOOMY PROSPECTI
A RAY OF SUNSHINE and a PERFECT RESTORATION.
(by ottr special reporter.)
What is locally
Considered a Miraclo
has lately been performed at Silver Water,
sometimes called “ Ibbettown,” so many
lb Jetts live there, a prettily-situated village
near Auburn, and about 10 miles from
Sydney, on the main suburban line.
News of the perfect restoration of health
to Mr. William lbhett having got abroad,
we dispatched one of our reporteis to the
district to institute a searching investigation
into the case, and find out
The Absolute Truth
from Mr. lbbett and the neighbours, who
appear to have made the case one of general
interest in the neighbourhood for some time
past, for it has been a drily subject of
conversation amongst the many people
acquainted with the particulars.
lhe reporter could not make the people
“® Questioned understand which member of
the lbbett family he wanted to see till he
Mentioned the Illnes3
of the man in question, at which the re-
quire! house was at once pointed out as that
of the man the doctors
Could not cure, and Hospitals
turned out as Inctsrable,
hut Clements Tonic had
Thoroughly Restored.
The reporter was fortunate enough to
obtain the interview he sought, when he
ask(d : —“Will you do me the kindness, Mr.
lbbett, to tell me how your complaint first
started, how it progressed, and how you
finally recovered ? ”
“I will,” answered Mr. lbbett. “It
commenced with seve e pains in the head,
and afterwards pains down the neck and
spine came on. 'then all at once I lost my
appetite. I could’nt look at my food, the
very sight of it turned me sick. My
digestion went all wrong. What little
I did eat (and I forced myself to eat some-
thing now and then to keep body and soul
together) gave me great pain. I only took
soft food, but even this would not digest,
but
Turned Sour and Fermented
in my stomach, causing me to suffer terribly
from wind and flatulence. Besides, I could
get no sleep. I did not get, as a rule, more
than an hour’s sleep a night, and that in
disturb d, uneasy snatches, each of short
duration, which did me no good at all. I
had to
Give up my Billet
at the Auburn Meat Works, and I used to
sit all day long in the chair feeling nerveless
and languid, with no energy or desire to
move. The pains in my back got worse
daily, especially along the spine. I got
most distressingly weak, and strangely
nervous. I have been accustomed to be in
the works with the w'hirl and crash of
machinery going on all round me, and I
never suffered the least inconvenience, but
now every sound went
Through me like a Knife,
and would startle and give me such a shock
that I should almost fall to the ground, and
I trembled like a leaf ”
“ Why didn’t you see a doctor’ ”
“I diil and what use wa id I had
geveral, and obeyed their orders, and took
their physic. To what end ? I only got
worse and worse. Finally they gave me up.
They said they could do nothing more for
me, and they had done nothing then ; and
one of them told me that he had given me
medicines strong enough to kill a horse.
This, I believe, was a fact, but it evidently
was not the treatment my disease required,
for it entirely failed to relieve me.
“ You must have got pretty low-spirited.”
“Of course. I became very much de-
pressed. Everything ahead
Looked Dark and Gloomy
to me. My Fiends tried in yain to cheer
me up, hut nothing could give me hope, as
every day my disease increased. I got very
thin. I w’as like a skeleton. I fell away
in a very short Time ft om 10 stone 4 lbs.
to 8 stone 5 lbs., and my face was pale and
colourless.
“ Did you suffer in any other way that
you’ve not yet mentioned?”
“Yes; my kidneys were affected, and a
dull, dragging pain in the small of the back
was continually distressing me (the doctor
said this was a sign of kidney disease), and
my water was very high coloured, but I
don’t care to go into further particulars as
to my kidney symptoms; but I can only
assure you they caused me great distress.
As a last resort, on my wife’s suggestion, I
was taken to the Hospital, Sydney. I
thought that if it was possible to cure me
at all I should have the best chance there.
Qhe doctors gave me a very careful examin-
ation. They sounded me all over and took
my temperature, but they could make
nothing of my case. I beli ve that they
treated me there
Foi* Kidney disease.
But none of the treatment I received at the
Hospital relieved me, and the pain in my
back whilst at the Hospital was agonising
After being there some weeks I was at last
Discharged as incurable.
The doctors said they could do nothing for
me. My wife and friends were very much
grieved that I had to return home without
relief. As for myself, I fully thought I was
going home to die. I gave up all hope of
ever getting on my feet again.”
“What induced you to resort to Clements
Tonic ?”
It was recommended to me by my cousin.
She said she had heard of someone in whom,
so far as some of the symptoms went, had
been afflicted as I was, and who had been
Entirely Restored to Health
by that remedy. She was so positive that
I got my wife to obtain some Clements Tonic
for me. I had, however, very little hope of
its being of any service, but you know how
a drowning man
Catches at a Straw.
My wife bought a bottle and I commenced
to take it regularly.”
“ How did it act in your case ?”
“First, by relieving the awful pains in my
back Then, as I continued to take it, I felt
my nervous system gradually get a better
tone. I was not so fretful and irritable, and
I began to sleep much better. I was soon
glad to find my digestion greatly improve.
Clements Tonic prevented my food going
sour and disagreeing with me, and I was
able to once mere eat solids freely and
without suffering afterwards, as I had been
accustomed to.”
“ And what about your kidney trouble ?"
“ I found a most
Distinct Beneficial Effect
upon my kidneys. It didn’t take long for
Clements Tonic to make those organs work
properly again, and to rid me of all disagri e
able symptoms which had formerly trouble*
me.”
“ You, of course, continued Clement*
Tonic?”
“Yes, you may be sure I didn’t miss a dose.
From the time of starting I put all othei
medicines aside. Day by day 1 got
Stronger and Stronger?
I was able to look hopefully to the future,
and my depression had vanished as though
it never had been. No longer was I dull
and heavy in the day time, but was bright
and lively. Once I started to get better the
rate of my recovery was astonishing. I
Came on hand over hand,
so to speak. Clements Tonic gives you a
great appetite and no mistake, and I ate
splendidly and put on flesh again rapidly.”
“ Your relations and neighbours must
have been surprised at the way in which you
were restored to health ?”
“ I should just say they were—you just
ask them about me and they will tell you
all about it.
All my Troubles Vanished
under the influence of Clements Tonic, and
I was able to go to work again, though till
I was completely strong I always took a
bottle with me when I went to woi'k, I
found it so invigorating.”
“ And your health now j”
“I am now quite cured, and as strong
and well as ever in my life, for which J
thank Clements Tonic. If anyone wants to
know more about my case, they have only
to call on me and I shall be only too pleased
to tell them how Clements Tonic brought
me back to health and strength when the
doctors gave me up and
The Hospital turned me out
as an incurable, and I thought my days were
numbered."
“ May we publish all you’ve said to me?'
“ Yes ; you are quite welcome to do so.’’

M'Kormic’s Woe.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
’Twas in a township’s single street,
He sat upon a log,
His swag was lying at his feet,
As also was his dog;
His brow was furrowed deep with care,
And in his ancient eye
Was woe untold, and tears were there
He did not wish to dry.
’Twas my belief that cankered grief
Was gnawing him. My q ueries brief
He answered with a sigh.
The bushmen in the public “cussed,”
And drank their pewters off.
A “ drunk ” was sleeping in the dust,
Another in the trough.
We saw the foaming liquor, heard
The pump’s eternal clink,
And roaring shouters pass the word :
“Say whatyer goin’ ter drink ? ”
Yet like a stone he sat. I own
He breathed a hollow, awful groan,
But never stirred a wink !
His eye was dull, its glassy stare
Was pitiful to see.
At last his tale of grim despair
Confided he to me.
“ I was a drunkard, boss,” he said,
“ I had a famous thirst.
I loved the best of liquors red,
And well I loved the worst.
The whisky dear, and rum and beer,
Were my delight.” He maundered here
Just like a thing accurst.
“ If honest drinks were not to spare
It did not matter much ;
I took to washes for the hair,
And liniments, and such.
My reputation travelled far,
I won a great renown.
No man this side could breast the bar
And drink M’Kormic down.
But one sad day I went away,
Inveigled to a Home to stay
’Longside of Sydney town.
“ It was a celebrated place
Where fellows who get tight
Are soon deprived of every trace
Of whisky appetite.
They gave me mixtures made of gold,
And splashed the rum about,
And fed me full as I could hold—
I grew extremely stout.
The boss one night said I had quite
Renounced my horrid appetite.
Next day I paddled out.”
He shook his hands, he tore his hair,
His groans were wilder far,
And still his eye’s mesmeric stare
Was fixed upon the bar.
“Poor man,’’ I said, “ you suffer still
The craving you endured.
They failed to cure you—to fulfil
The promise that allured ? ”
He fetched a cry that pierced the sky.
“ It’s worse, it’s worse ! ” he groaned, “ for I
Oh Heaven ! lam cubed ! ”
EDWARD DYSON.
Daughter and Barometer.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was a still, stifling night. Several loafers
lounged in the bar. A big fly bumped stupidly
along the ceiling, and a couple of kangaroo-dogs
made dull thumpings on the verandah as the fleas
tormented them. Minnie Parks, 14 years old,
attended to customers, standing on tiptoes to
roach bottles and glasses.
“Yer dad’s worse, ain’t ’e?” inquired a big
carter, leaning along the counter, and tracing his
name with some spilt liquor.
Minnie nodded.
A little man on a bench endeavored to look in-
telligent and make some remark ; he squinted
abominably in the attempt to keep both eyes
open, and with a vacuous smile began, “ I—l—yer
p ar but tremendous hiccoughs prevented
speech, and he subsided, though still keeping up
a pretence of being wide awake and sober.
Minnie stared at him solemnly. “He’s only
had three glasses,” she remarked with the con-
tempt born of the knowledge of what some men
can carry.
“ The doc.’s with Da, ain’t e ? ’
She nodded again and looked round anxiously,
her ear keenly alert for the doctor’s footstep. She
was a small slip of a creature, with a peaky face
and bright eyes. Her work in the shanty was
never at an end, and she had never known from
babyhood what it meant to be a careless, irrespon-
sible child.
Hearing the door open from the crazy room at
the back, she glanced anxiously at Ben. “ Tell
anyone to wait for me, if they come, will you ? ”
He nodded, and she disappeared noiselessly, and
crept after the doctor as he crossed the soft dust in
the roadway. He turned, feeling the touch of a
hand on his arm.
“It’s me, Minnie Parks. Is Dad better?’’she
whispered.
“ I can’t say he is, child. This hot weather is
against him. If it woidd only change’’—and he
glanced into the starlit sky, innocent of clouds.
“ If it changed d’ye think he’d get better ? ”
“ He’d have a chance.”
“ D’ye think it will change, doctor ? ” Her tone
was eager.
“ No, I don’t.”
“ You’ve done all you can, haven’t you, doctor ? ”
“ Yes, child ; his isn’t a case for doctors.” His
thoughts reverted to the sodden beast he had just
left, and a puzzled expression crossed his face.
He could hardly believe Minnie had much affec-
tion for such a creature. “ What’s worrying you,
child ? Let’s sit here, on this log, while I have a
smoke, and you tell me about it.”
The doctor was an old fellow, and he knew the
world pretty well, and it was to his credit that he
still believed in it to a certain extent. He felt
that something more than filial anxiety was at the
bottom of Minnie’s questions. “ Come, let’s hear
the trouble,” he said, kindly, and for answer
Minnie burst into tears.
“I don’t want it to change,” she sobbed. “I’m
a bad, wicked girl. I don’t want it to change,
because I don’t want him to get better. The
minister said I ought to love him, even if he did
beat me. But I hate him—l hate him ! ” The
words were repeated, not with the passion that
follows one act of cruelty, but with the cold
decision of acknowledged fact. “ I know I’ll go
to hell for being wicked ; but he hurt mo so. He
beat me; he pulled me round the room by the
ear. He kicked my puppy and killed it. My
mother died because he hit her when” She
paused, shaking with excitement, and with the
memory of the old horror of which she had never
before spoken to any one, and which it had taken
her many years to realise to the full. The picture
was sharp in her mind. The cowering woman,
her screams for mercy, her words, which the child
did not understand until years after. A kick, a
blow ! She shuddered, remembering it.
“ I say, Minnie Parks, yer wanted—two
whiskies! ” shouted Ben’s voice, and Minnie
darted off, wiping her eyes on her dress. The doc-
tor walked home slowly, and, as he opened his
door, muttered, “Thank Heaven, I can do no
more ; that is, if Heaven worries itself to any ex-
tent over a woman or girl being kicked to death.
If we were in a city, and could get ice and so on,
the brute ’d pull through ; as it is, he hasn’t
Buckley’s chance.”
And when the doctor rose the following morn-
ing, he and Minnie were probably the only people
glad to see the earth quivering under an already
intense heat.
Nevertheless, before evening came a change—
storm and rain and coolness.
But the man was dead.
POTTS.
Some anti-Federalists have reduced their
estimate of what N.S. Welshmen will have to pay
extra, from <£l 2s. fid. to £1 per head per annum.
Possibly the odd half-crown is for the registration
of George Pile’s dog.
Not long ago the Tasmanian Government
wanted a Chief Commissioner of Police for the
re-organised force, on the abolition of municipal
police, and it went for him to—the Benevolent
Asylum ! The particular qualification of the new
man is, that for some years he has ruled several
hundred old men and women, many of them im-
beciles. Also, he is a Volunteer captain or some-
thing. The fact that he has had long and
intimate acquaintance with “vags.” may count
for something.
The Melbourne Progressive Spiritualists
held their 51st anniversary the other day. It was
a mild shivoo—mediums were off color, and spirits
sulked. Mrs. Mellon was there. She said Sydney
was full of spirits—but didn’t venture an opinion
as to quality. This lady is stickin’ at the biz—real
old rock-Mellon, so to say. Like most of the cult,
she doesn’t seem a bit the worse for being hung up
as an impostor. Indeed, it suits spiritists to
get an advt. that way, now and again. Cissy
and Geordie, Mellon’s spooks, didn’t turn up on
the occasion. Perhaps they’re dead !
The presidentof Sydney Benevolent Society
announced, at a recent meeting of the board, that
the Premier was to be asked to ad vance £3OOO extra
(beyond the Govt, subsidy and grants, which
amount already to .£5000) in order to see the
society through the winter. The same board
published, the other day, an article on the de-
crease of poverty in Sydney, as compared with
previous years, so that its chance of securing
.£3OOO from the needy Reid is about equal to
Buckley’s. As a matter of fact, the cause of the
falling-off in the society’s figures is that the poor
decline to patronise the institution unless com-
pelled, and appeal to the Chief-Secretary’s Depart-
ment, whose sources of relief are just now taxed
to the uttermost.
The Wickedness of a Woman.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sir.—You are being heartlessly deceived by a false
woman and a pretended friend. Where does your wife
fjo on Thursday evenings when you are working back? I
am unknown to you, but I am, nevertheless, your sincere
—Wellwisiier.
“It’s a lie ! ” he thought, as he crumpled the
paper up in his hand and threw it away from him.
But he picked it up again, and spread it out' care-
fully on the office-table. “ Could it have any
truth in it?” No, he persuaded himself that he
did not believe it, but he put it carefully in his
pocket and took it home with him. Being a man,
the thought of who sent it did not much trouble
him—to a woman this would have been the first
consideration and its truth the next. He was
honest and unsuspicious, and fought with his doubt
all day and went home believing he had conquered,
but he unconsciously determined to watch his
wife closely. Genuine faith, once doubt-poisoned,
never does revive in its full strength. Uncon-
sciously his feeling towards her had changed, and
he himself dimly felt that he was acting. The
quick-witted, bright-faced little woman at home
noticed the change at once. The keen intuition of
a guilty conscience told her the cause. Each knew
the other’s part —but it was the heavy acting of a
man trying to do the right against the cunning
deception of a woman. The odds were unequal.
She searched his clothes, found the letter, warned
the Other Man, and was hot on “ Well wisher’s ”
trail.
Two days later her husband received another
letter:—
Sir, —The wicked deception still continues. Where is
your wife going next Tuesday afternoon ? —YVellwisher.
He might, honest man, have given little heed
to this letter; but on the dressing-table that
evening he saw this confirmatory note in his
wife's hand-writing, evidently ready for the post:
Dear Old Boy,—l will meet you in the Strand at 3. I
hope you have engaged our room for Thursday night.—
Yours very affectionately, Amy.
He was staggered. He would go at once and
denounce her. No; she was cunningly tricking
him ; he would as cleverly trap her. He would
go to the meeting.
His wife, whose handiwork both these letters
were, had reckoned that he would. The success
of her scheme for clearing herself depended on
that.
They had hardly shaken hands when he bore
down upon them. It was her brother —that good
old useful brother who serves so many purposes.
Relieved, but still suspicious, he said, “ What’s
this ? I thought you were going to stop at home
this afternoon ? Where are you taking my
Amy ? ”
“ Don’t be angry, Harry ; I thought you
wouldn’t mind very much,” she said, caressing his
arm. “ I know how you hate the theatre, so we
didn’t tell you, bub Tom and I have been taking
lessons in acting, and we’re going to have private
theatricals Thursday night.”
“My God ! what an evil-minded brute I am,”
he thought. A revulsion of love and pity for the
little woman he had wronged overcame him. His
wife watched his face closely. “ Come and look
at the shops, dear,” she said, and she worked his
repentance for a new blue dress.
The Other Man said he liked her best in blue.

The Fleets.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Black Finn lay in his bunk agasp—
Sore fretted his soul for flight ;
I owed thee a favor once,” said he,
Bend down till I give thee sight 1 ” . . .
He touched his eyes where the death-films spread,
then clammily touched lie mine—
“ Now, open the port-hole wide,” he cried, “and
repayment shall be thine ! ”
Ihe Night was crowned with a lakh of stars ; but
West, where the sun had died,
His blood burned red at the sea-line yet, and sor-
rowful mourned the tide.
This was the gift of the dying Finn :—A-covering
all the Seas,
I saw the ships of the Seas, in line, come over the
centuries:
I heard them bringing their anchors up in the
Harbors of Cockayne,
I heard them laying their anchors down at the
Blessed Isles again.
The corposants at their mast-heads glowed. Be-
times, as they passed me by,
I saw the stars through their sails afar, shine dim
in the lower sky !
The Ancient Sire in his fire-scooped bole, rowed
first through the falling dusk—
His flint axe-head by a tough thong tied, his spear
of the mammoth tusk :
And o’er the boom of the brooding waves, I heard
as he paddled on
The hungry growl of the Great Gave Bear, the
tramp of the Mastodon.
The echoes died in a voice of crowds as out from
the Tigris’ mouth
The bulrush boat of a*Bagd id sheik came rolling
her cargo South ;
Her strong palm wine in its earthen jars, her dates
on their lea ves laid,
To passion the lord of Babylon, and pleasure the
Hittite maid . . .
Soft fell the clink of a brazen bell, and under our
counter passed
A quaint-built craft of acanthus wood, on her twin
acanthus mast
A papyrus sail, all lotus-flowered ; recline in its
shade a girl
With deep, orbed eyes of the despot East, and a
mouth of rose and pearl. . . .
I heard the roll of the Prakrit speech, and a galley
tall rode on,
All clinker-built of a cedar trunk from the Mount
of Lebanon :
Her deep keel leaped to the mighty sweep of the
thrashing three-banked oars,
As she held away from the pirate hands that
itched by the Tuscan shores.
“But this,” I cried, “from the dockyards wide
of the blue Piraeus swung ;
These be the men who have wrought with stone
and written in Homer's tongue.”
I hailed them loud as their brave ship passed, and
the echoes answered me :
“ Oh sang the seas when the world was young, sweet
vjas the song of the sea ;
The days are dead and the days will die, but liveth
the Odyssey."
* * * *
The Black Finn muttered a Norland spell and
groaned like a dog in pain—
“l owed thee a favor twice,” said he, “look out
on the seas again ! ”
I looked, and lo ! from the Ten Lost Isles to the
Island of San Chen-San,
From Hy-Brasil to the Port of Lob, the host of
their numbers ran :
Tied in the link of a thousand years, the Celt, in
his skin canoe,
Rode prow and prow with a Kentish chief hard
pulled by a painted crew ;
And, linked again by a thousand years, a proa
with her sail aback
Rode neck and neck with the Hunting Man in his
walrus-hide kayak.
The long, beaked neck of a Punio: barge shot by
like a scared sea-snake,
Her lights ablaze on the brazen helms of the Latin
foe ; a-wake
Abiding not to his haughty hail they galloped
along the foam, —
“ The gods that ye know avail ye not 1 Give heed to
the gods of Rome l ”
Aye, now the Cub of the Wolf at Rome a-cleaving
the seas doth go,
To clear the road to the Westward Isles that her
traders’ keels may know
The weight of the British hides and wool and the
Western fish and game,
That garnish the proud patrician board of the
Proud Patrician Dame.
<■ » * *
The Black Finn moaned on his bed of Death.
“ Make haste ere I go,” he cried ;
“ Thrice have I a favor owed to thee ; now open
the porthole . . . wide !”....
From the silken South, the sinewed North, from
the shining East and West,
With their pale corpse-candles all alight and the
Four Winds all at rest,
They drifted in from the Bay of Sleep and the
Gulf of Acheron,
They drifted out from the Isle of Souls ; from the
Isles of Avalon ;
Theysteamed from the frozenDumslaf Land, away
in the frozen seas ;
From Fly-away-Cape, in Isle-au-Sein, and the
famed Hesperides.
The outer plain with their host was hid, and the
inner plain was filled
With ev’ry rig that the seas have known ; with
every make and build.
A mat-sailed junk of the China Coast and a white-
washed Arab dhow
They passed to port and to starboard on with the
liner build of now.
The narrow ships of the Oreekmen bold spread
their strong black raven wings,
With blue-sailed boats of the Irish bays and fleets
of the Saxon Kings,
A buoent uir, with her long red oars i.nd a high-
decked hull of Spain,
They rode with the tubs that traded wool for the
wine of A juitaine;
With B'ack Sea tubs of the Genoese, with the
cruisers of Algiers,
With slavers, pirates, and corsair-men in their
fighting privateers ;
With koff< which carried the Dutchman’s goods
langsyne to the Javanese ;
And Mogul's ships that were fain to yield to the
plundering Portuguese.
The fleets of the Nations gathered in ; the fleets of
the Nations grew,
But the fleet of the Little Islands spread till they
counted six to two.
Along the Downs and abreast the Sands, like a
great cloud came they forth,
And some went West to the S j as Unknown, and
some to the South and North.
Then heard I a Voice from over-seas, and the
Voice in thunders spake : . . .
“ The spoil of the world is his for aye who courage
shall find to take ;
The codfish spawns on the Banks for him ; the
bees of the Alp slopes toil,
The fruits of the fruiting Earth are his ; the corn,
the wine, and the oil ” !
A silence fell on the Nations all. Then a strong-
tongued voice up-spoke,
“ With grammars and guns we go, oh Lord ! with
the Heart of Steel and Oak ;
The plunder of Antwerp first we take. But, Lord,
ere our flags be furled,
We'll spoil the spoilers of all the earth and loot
the loot of the world.
“Our English blondes in the rajah’s silks shall
shine as they walk arrayed,
Our workmen’s hands they are deft to build, and
our hearts be unafraid ;
We’ve lack o’ sable for Whitehall folk, and port
for our noble dames,
And the weed that grows where Raleigh went —in
spite of our good King James !
“The Doge of Venice may bite his beard, and
counsel his lords profound,
The I urk hath greeted our Tudor Queen, and the
Susan's outward bound;
She s ta en a cargo of London truck, and her
skipper trusts in God,
The length o’ stroke and the strength of oak and
his great guns thirty-odd.” . . .
* • * *
I saw them spreading and spreading far, yea, over
the utmost seas
The black-hulled catts of the Steelyard firms, and
the Livery Companies,
The sailing-ships of the First Great Queen, the
steaming ships of the Last,
By the rogues of the realm all stoutly manned, and
the red flag at the mast.
The last to cross was a battle-ship of a clean ten
thousand tons,
A two-foot wall of twice-tempered steel and a
grinning tier of guns;
Her triple-expansion engines throbbed at touch of
her engineers,
She lit the seas with her bright arc-light and
blotted away the years.
She hulled them down on St. Brandan’s Road,
and I watched them one by one,
Till the last great liner thundered out, and the
last drab tramp was gone.
The stars lay still on the lonely seas, I turned to
the Finn, and said :
“Whither in truth have the good ships gone?
but the lean Black Finn was dead.
Sydney, 1899.
E. J. BRADY.
The American clergy of a prominent
Yankee town recently declared kilts to be inde-
cent. It was mentioned, by way of palliation,
that Queen Victoria’s servants (some of them)
walked about daily in aforesaid “ indecent garb.”
Hereupon, Rev. Frank Hoagland, a leading
Y.M.O.A. light: “Queen Victoria is doubtless
respectable, but she is very indiscreet.”
A Bulletin man : “ Heard an earnest
Salvarmy officer at it a few nights ago. He was
praying mightily for * the great invidel.’ Who ? I
wondered. Ingersoll,. Joe Symes—who ? Listened
for a name. Who had been scoffing now? Per-
haps the Bulle But he roared louder than
ever: ‘ Gawd bless’n. Restore’n to us. Thou
knawest what we shud do without the invidel—
Gawd bless’n —we dawn’t.’ And so on. By-and-
bye it transpired. He used ‘General ’ instead of
‘invidel.’ It was while the old man was sick.
He meant‘invalid.’”
The Australasian Medical Gazette per-
tinently but awkwardly asks what there is in the
way of contribution to general medical knowledge
to show for the .£BO,OOO spent during the last 20
years on Sydney University Medical School, and
points out that the only practical result erf the
“labors” of the elaborate professional staff has
been the swamping of the medical profession
with callow practitioners, who, as being pre-
ferred for resident hospital positions, have
to “ gain experience upon Sydney maimed
poor.” When you come to think of it,
Premier Kingston’s Napoleonic solution of the
Adelaide Hospital trouble was a great democratic
and humanitarian move. It is beginning to slowly
dawn upon the public that every big hospital
should always have on the premises, night and
day, two or three high-class medicoes of wide ex-
perience, men who could command extensive prac-
tice and high fees as consultants outside—instead
of relying almost exclusively on the casual and
often perfunctory visits of overworked “ hono-
raries ” and the services of boys just' out of the
medical schools.
